THE RED PAGE.
BLUEBELLS.
“ One day, one day, I ’ll climb that distant hill
And pick the blue-bells there ! ”
So dreamed the child who lived beside the rill,
And breathed the lowland air.
“ One day, one day, when I am old I’ll go
And climb the mountain where the blue-bells blow 1 r
“ One day ! one day ! The child was now a maid,
A girl with laughing look:
She and her lover sought the valley-glade
Where sang the silver brook.
" One day,” she cried, “ love, you and I will go
And reach that far hill where the blue-bells blow l ”
Years passed. A ivoman now, with wearier eyes,
Gazed towards that sun-lit hill.
Tall children clustered round her. How time flies!
The blue-bells blossomed still.
She ’ll never gather them ! All dreams fade so.
We live and die, and still the blue-bells blow.
GEORGE BARLOW.
MONARCHS DYING!
IN its issue of February 15, Reynolds’s News-
paper stresses the fact of King Edward’s con-
tinued ill-health. His constitution has broken
down, and “the experts know that Edward VII
will have but a short reign.” No whisper of the truth
breaks the conspiracy of silence preserved by other
English journals, sycophants of the Court. Queen
Victoria was torpid, dazed, and imbecile for months
before her death—a typical victim of senile decay ; yet
she was carried, moribund, to solemn function after
function, while the Press congratulated her upon the
health she had lost, the speeches that she never made,
and the duties that were discharged by her underlings.
Doubtless King Edward will die in the odour of similar
mendacity. The history of his postponed coronation
is a sufficient token. On the Sunday before the date
fixed, when all other English newspapers were announc-
ing the King's excellent health, Reynolds’s published the
news that he was seriously ill, and that the coronation
ceremony could not take place. The Press Association
wired for confirmation to the King’s private secretary,
General Sir Henry Ponsonby, who wired back a denial.
The next day King Edward underwent the operation for
perityphlitis !
THE REAL KING AND QUEEN
The real king and queen bear little likeness to
the false king and queen that one sees in the re-touched
photographs of “ loyal ” journals, and reads of in
“loyal” paragraphs. King Edward is in his sixty-second
year ; but he has lived his life, and appears older.
A short, stout, German-looking, German-speaking
man - : his height is sft. 4in. ; his weight before
last year’s illness was about 18 stone ; his blood
is two parts Scotch, two parts Scandinavian, 1024 parts
German; like the Prince of Wales, he says “de man,”
“my tear vater.” His face is fleshy and sallow ; his head
bald ; his beard quite white. At the time of his corona-
tion he looked sleepy, intelligent, human, humane—a
bonhomme debonair—“ a jolly good fellow.” As a man
of the world, he is wise : it is likely, as alleged, that to
his influence England owes the ending of the war in South
Africa on the terms asked by the Australian Anti-War
League, and the projected settlement of the Irish land
question for the benefit of the tenantry. But Queen
Alexandra !—the woman miserable, the wretched victim
of nervous disease, the sad Queen who said at Victoria’s
death, “ I have been out of it for years past; I’m deaf now,
and lame : what can a throne do for me ! ” Deaf, arid lame,
and bald, and toothless, and painted, and padded—with
her necklace constantly worn to hide her throat, and her
false neck and shoulders to preserve the illusion of youth
in public—no one could have dreamed a more tragic
spectacle than she presented on the day of the coronation
ceremony. Her eyes were frantic with suffering. Her
anguish seemed intenser than that of the writhing figures
of the Laocoon, more terrible than that of Prometheus
upon the rock, for she had still to smile, with the serpent’s
coils around her; to smile ! while bit by bit the vultures
ate her heart.
THE LOWERING CLOUD
Queen Alexandra looks what she aoubtless is —a
good and noble woman ; but the tragedy of her life !
“ A jolly good fellow ” does not make, from a woman’s
point of view, the most satisfactory of husbands.
Then the cry of the monarchy for heirs, what-
ever a woman’s weakness. Her first son dying at
his birth, a year after her marriage ; her second
son reared painfully to manhood —then he too dying,
degenerate; her third son, the good-natured Prince
of Wales, bearing the same fatal seeds of decay, shadowed
by the same fatal doom, an invalid at thirty-seven. Hi s
eldest son a weakling—eight years old—and what in
prospect when King Edward dies, if King George should
not long survive him? All monarchies dread a Regency;
yet between the British monarchy and a Regency there
are but two lives, both hanging on a thread. The physical
state of the Prince of Wales, if London clubs are to be
trusted, is hardly less serious than that of King Edward.
It is said that the son ha? taken refuge in a habit
that his stronger and stronger-willed father did
not carry to mortal excess ; and his signal absence from
the great Indian durbar is given in proof. As she sits
sadly upon her throne, with pain-haunted eyes questing
mournfully over the future, England’s unhappy Queen
may well seem to herself a new Cassandra, shrieking with
the wild tongues of a thousand griefs the fate of monarehs
dying.
CURRENT CLIPPINGS
George Meredith to The Manchester Guardian :
“ I hope that ultimately we shall be able to take teach-
ing out of the hands of the clergy, and that we shall be
able to instruct the clergy in the fact Christianity i 3
a spiritual religion, and not one that is to be governed
by material conditions. A spiritual God I most perfectly
believe in. I have that belief constantly before me—l
feel it within me ; but a material God that interferes in
material, mortal affairs I have never seen, and that I
don’t mind anybody knowing ; and it is, I am sorry to
say, for the material God that the clergy seem to be
striving.”

For me the old British Solar God lights up the squalor
of Billingsgate. The Sea God, Lud, and the brazen horse
give me more pleasure than the railway bridge at Lud-
gate. Oaasar’s sword at Bishopsgate and the head of Bran
buried on Tower Hill are real City assets. London is rich
in romantic lore. In her cathedral Arthur was crowned
and drew the sword from the stone. Here Iseult attended
the council called by King Mark. From the quay Ursula
and her virgins embarked ; Launoelot swam his horse
over the river at Westminster, and from it Guinevere
went a-maying.—lF. R. Lethaby, a romantic antiquary,
in London Before the Conquest.
The ancient, brave writers—A wonderful throng 1
They died in a garret, To live in a song ! They told us
the story At which the world thrills, Locked in a rude
corner From bailiffs with bills. The modern, mad
writers Who thunder away—They live in a palace, And
die in a day 1 They tell us no story Humanity feels, And
ride to oblivion On automobiles.— Atlanta Constitution.

enough for movement, but not life enough for peace.
That it h a s life, whether restless or at rest, is the fact
which proves its title to the name of style.— Alice Mey-
nett.
She smiled widely, showing little white teeth, each
detached from its neighbour, like a row of doll’s tomb-
stones. — Edwin Pugh.
The second Charles was good-humoured—really. Even
from a complete stranger, who had been bitten by one of
his celebrated King Charles spaniels—a very ill-tempered
and snappy breed—the King good-humouredly endured
the remark : “ God bless your Majesty, but God damn
your dog! ”
Have you ever known martyrs, real martyrs ? I have.
They are grand, they are sublime—on condition that you
kill them. The very finest kind of martyr must have his
trimmings all complete—torture, peril, freezing weather,
judicial treachery, governmental cowardice, the last
gesture, the last word- especially the word and the mag-
nificent gesture. Deaf-mutes and cripples are no use to
martyrology. But if a martyr fails—that is to say,
if he earns martyrdom and does n’t get martyred, it’s all
over with him. Othello’s occupation’s gone. The
martyr who misses becomes a public nuisance. He comes
in without knocking, fascinates your wife, and bores you
with his exploits till after midnight. In the end the
martyr, become no-martyr, hangs himself or blows his
brains out. Possibly he poisons himself. Because, you
see, the martyr is a man set apart for death, made on
purpose for death ; he has nothing in common with life.
Those Christian virgins whom we admire as torches in
Nero’s gardens would not suit at all as friends or wives or
mistresses. They burned beautifully, but you could
never live with them. A martyr is a martyr simply
because he has to be. It’s in his bones. In order to live
his life, he must die. A true martyr cannot not-die. If
he lives, he misses his vocation, and bitterly he feels it.
For their own sakes (as well as ours) let us encourage our
martyrs to die quickly.— Louis Stiti.
CONTRIREUTORS’ COMMENTARY
E.W.D. : Re “ ‘the struck eagle’ Byron stole
from Waller.” ASsop, the world-renowned Greek courtier
and fabulist ( circa b.c. 619-564) tells that tale in his Fables,
“ An eagle sat on a rock watching a hare, whom he
sought to make his prey. An archer, who was watching
the eagle, took aim and wounded him mortally. The
eagle gave One look at the arrow that had entered his
heart, and saw’ in that single glance that its feathers had
been furnished by himself. *ltis a double grief to me,’
he said, ‘ that I should perish’by an arrow feathered from
my own wings.’” ASschylus (B.C. 525-456) calls it a
Libyan legend in The Myrmidons. Gabrias, too, has it
in four Greek iambics—translated into Latin by
Alsop in Fenton’s edition of Waller’s works (Lond., 1730).
A variant is found in the fables of the French La Fon-
taine (1621-1695), and in other collections. Now, while
Henry Kirke White, whom Byron apostrophises in the
famous simile, might.be said to recall Waller—inasmuch
as he wrote an additional verse to Waller’s poem, “ Go,
Lovely Rose ’—yet Tom Moore, a much closer colleague,
had used the figure just before Byron. For in 1808
Moore’s satirical poems, Corruption, and Intolerance,
appeared. In lines 95-98 of the former the familiar figure
is used:—
Like a young eagle, who has lent his plume
To fledge the shaft by which he meets his doom,
See their own feathers pluck'd, to wing the dart
Which rank Corruption destines for their heart!
Byron’s scathing satire, English Bards and Scotch
Reviewers— wherein (lines 817-834) he uses the same
image—while written in 1808, did not appear till the year
after.
W.S.T. : As to our flower-names, compare the old
world flowers:—
Daisies pied and violets blue, .
The flower that’s like thy face, pale primrose ; nor
The azur’d harebell, like thy veins — no, nor
The leaf of eglantine, whom not to slander,
Out-sweeten’d not thy breath . . .
These words are woven into ourlanguage as naturally as
the sun shines, or the silver rain falleth, Then, again,
the woods—
The oak, the ash, and the bonny brier tree,
They all grow green in the ivest countree.
Any versifier could make poetry with such names ; but
how is he to employ such sesquipedalian monstrosi-
ties as tallow-wood, pittosporum, silky oak (which
is no oak), and their ill-named and misnamed congeners.
Acacia and mimosa are better, but they are not charac-
teristically Australian. Maorilanders are luckier than
we ; for they have kaui-i and rata, and many mellifluous
Maori names. We need especially new names for the
native fuchsia, the native rose, the “false” sarsaparilla
(what a cognomen for a dainty flower !), flannel-flower,
and a dozen diversified and beautiful bulbs—all called
lilies.
L.S. : Amusing to note variations of Melb. municipal
notices re anti-spit crusade. Port Melbourne: “ All
persons are respectfully requested not to expectorate on
the footways.” Prahran : “With a view of minimising
the danger of infection from contagious diseases you are
respectfully requested to refrain from expectorating on
the footpath.” South Melbourne : “ Don’t spit on the
footpath.” [What Clues to the quality of municipal
minds! And note the.Nmental difference implied by a
common American notice : “ Gentlemen will not spit.
Others must not.”]
Riverina : Reading “ Nil’s ” complaint (28/2/3) of the
poetical misuse of a-words (a-shine, a-quiver, etc.), I was
reminded of an effective piece of verse that appeared in
The Bulletin some time ago. It was entitled “ School’s
In,” and ran like this—
When prices take to dropping, and the crop is “ cleaning
light,"
And the tanks are nearly empty, and the feed is turning
:white,
And the poison-weed’s a-growing, and the '■‘■norther” is
A-BLOWING,
And your credit is a-sinking till it sinks right out of
sight —
Then it’s come and meet the boys in the little private
room,
Where the beer goes a-begging and the pipes are all
A-FUMB ...
Singularly enough, these lines are signed N—l—L—
“ Nil.!*
“ Ignotus” discusses a bookfellow’s pockets, and others :
—About a year ago I got married. It seemed a thing one
ought to do once, just as an experinient. My idea was to
Mould it. I have learned a lot. It has no pockets. It
doesn't carry a handkerchief. Nor a pencil . . . well,
nothing ! And if it must carry money, it carries an
absurd little white Maori kit, with the purse inside.
Now look at my pockets. They have never altered for thirty
years, save when I came to town and added the pocket-
book. Coat, left breast : Handkerchief and spectacles.
Coat, right breast: Papers. Coat, right side : Tobacco-
pouch, cigarette-papers, pencil, knife. Coat watch-
pocket : Watch. Coat match-pocket: Matches. Waist-
coat pockets : Four, all unused. Trousers, right : Small
change and latch-key. Trousers, left : Gold, notes, keys,
watch-key. The pocket-book measures 3J" x If", and
contains blank leaves, visiting cards, ’bus tickets, tram
tickets, cable-car tickets, stamps, etc. It might be
interesting if other bookfellows would note what I carry
that they don’t, and what they carry that they think I
ought to. And what they put in their waistcoat pockets.
And, if one of them has a wife, how many pockets it has,
and what it puts in them. I had an aunt when I was six,
and she had a pocket as big as a sugar-bag in her skirt,
and carried all sorts of things. Cork-screws. Door keys.
Combs. I used to be sent to cupboards to look. She had
a bruise on her leg where this weighty collection hit, like
a pendulum, when she walked.
J.F.D. : “R.Q.” (4/2/3) expresses what every bush-
man with imagination feels—the “ weird expectancy,”
the awful sense of waiting that is peculiar to Australian
scenery. The “ weird melancholy ”of Marcus Clarke is,
I take it, a secondary effect of the expectancy. It is the
oppressive sense of coming disaster that brings the
melancholy. I remember how, staying once in the Liver-
pool Ranges, far from a habitation, the day-long, night-
long, waiting attitude-of everything tautened my nerves
until it seemed they would break in insanity. The
wallabies in the creek were listening listening a
hurried bite—listen ! And a rabbit or hare, ail were
waiting—not with the quick, watchful movements of
wild animals in other countries, but in a statuesque pose
as if they could hear the Something coming. The fishes,
even, in the shallow creek seemed to be clustered in
waiting groups, and an occasional iguana on a tree seemed
to look at you inquiringly. You could read a question in
the attitude of all; Have you heard? Has it come?
I bolted—there was a disaster pending, some\inysterious
calamity : and I didn’t want it to strike me up there
where I was aloffr. When I got to town I asked the first
man I met if anything had happened. He said “ No-
why ?” I could n’t explain. But when I read now of a
suicide in the bush, and read the evidence taken at the
inquest—“ The man was subject to fits of melancholy”—
I shudder. He is the man who could n’t get away.

Emerald,! you who, in the olden time,
Lay on her breast and heard its hidden rhyme :
When her sweet passions fluttered to be free
Did not, your pale heart strive in sympathy f
In your green depth glowed there no rose sublime ?
Or, if her spirit wandered in a clime
Of tranquil joy, you were her gentle mime:
One happy wave above a halcyon sea,
Emerald t
Alas ! Love’s temple bells no longer chime:
Dead are his altar-fires ; his rites a crime:
Now from my life the years fall piteously
As fall the wan leaves from a stricken tree;
And you are all the hoarding of my prime,
Emerald!
To think that an English poet is publishing his col-
lected works in ten volumes, in these latter days of
degeneracy! It is George Barlow, a lyrist of some
faculty and greater facility, who might be valued more
had he written less. An example of his work heads this
page.
A word is wanted as the correlative antithesis of sequel.
We can say Twenty Years After is the sequel to
The Three Musketeers ; but what is The Three Musketeers
to Twenty Years After ? Prelusion and precess are
English suggestions. We shall use precess.
When ordering linotype machines, our daily papers
should see that they have dies for accented letters. We
are abolishing accents as far as possible : e.g., dlbutant
now is written correctly “debutant”; dlpdt, “depot,"
and so on. But one hears even University students refer-
ring to M. Delcasse, French Minister of Foreign Affairs,
as Dellcas —because the linotype does not accent the final
letter of his name.
The lady moon is my lover,
My friends are the oceans four,
The heavens have roofed me over,
And the davm is my golden door.
1 would liefer follow the condor,
Or the seagull soaring from km,
Than bury my godhead yonder
In the dust of the whirl of men.
Celtic? No, sir ; translated from Chinese.
Somebody flings at Henry James the lines that he him-
self flung at Flaubert: “ That a novel should have a
certain charm seems to us the most rudimentary of prin-
ciples, and there is no more charm in this laborious
monument to a treacherous ideal than there i.»,
interest in a heap of gravel.”
A remarkable collection of pottery, statuary, and clay
records, excavated by Arthur Evans in Crete, and
exhibited in London, proves (says The Academy) that
Diedalus, the “ flying-man ” of the fable, was a maker of
history, and the first European genius. It proves that
the alphabet was known in Crete circa 4004 8.C., at least
1000 years before the reputed date of its carriage, ready;
made, from Phmnicia into Greece. More: statuettes
demonstrate that 4000 years ago, at least, the superficial
muscles of the fore-arm, among other things, were pre-
cisely the same as in each of us to-day. Another proof
of the essential completion of physical evolution many
thousands of years ago.
Stories of Douglas Jerrold—going round once more.
The saying of Jerrold’s that hurt Thackeray most was in
reference to his lectures. “ Very good, Thackeray,” said
Jerrold, “ but wants a piano.” Thackeray stood sponsor
for a child; “ Good Lord, Thackeray,” commented
Jerrold, “ I hope you did not present the infant with
your own mug.” During the height of the Tractarian
Movement someone remarked : “They are Romanising
old Thack.” “Then,” said Jerrold, “I hope they'll
begin at his nose.” (Thackeray had had his
nose broken in a schoolboy fight, and was very
sensitive to any allusion to that fact.) A 150...
A Mr 3. Glover was complaining that her hair was
growing grey, and attributed it to using essence
of lavender. Jerrold asked her “ whether it was
not essence of thyme?” “Is the legacy to be
paid immediately ? ” asked some one with re-
gard to a bequest just made. “ Yes,” said Jerrold,
“on the coffin nail.” A bore said to a company, which
he had reduced to exhaustion, of a certain tune, “ It car-
ries me away with it.” “ For goodness’ sake,” said Jer-
rold, “ let somebody whistle it.”

is imaginary ; the Bible story of Cain and Abel is a dream ;
the Bible story of Noah and the Flood is an invention;
the Bible stories of the Patriarchs are fancies; the Bible
stories of Moses are a fraud ; the Bible stories of Elijah
and Elisha are superstitions ; the Bible story of Jonah is
a rmrsery-rhyme ; the Bible story of Nineveh’9 repentance
is a parable ; the Books of Samuel and the Kings are not

Bingara,ft.S.W., December 12th, 1902.
Gentlemen, —I am -pleased to tell you that I see a
decided improvement in Mr. Finlan already. For one
thing, he has put his walking-stick in the corner, and
yesterday he carried out a bag of chaff for the horses,
a thing he has not done for six months ; and he told
me this morning, “ I feel as if I could jump over the
palisading.” Yours gratefully, A. FINLAN.
Mount Marlow. Isisford (Q.)
Dear Sirs, —I have been wearing your Belt accord-
ing to directions for over a month, and must say feel
the benefit of same—rheumatism gone, and appetite
good : in fact, an improvement all round, and 1 must
say I think it is the best investment I ever made.
Yours faithfully,
W. H. PERRIER.
Geelong.
Dear Sirs,—l now write you these few lines to let

I don’t think so much of a day’s work. The disease

feeling that I used to have wnen working.
I remain, H. GRAHAM.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Pessimist’s Plaint.
Bodeful, reiterate, for ever rising,
The voice of those who wait
The confirmation of their vague surmising,
Guessings of small and great.
“To what end? For what good?” so loudly
calleth,
Questioning, “ Who can say ? ”
In mournful symphony responsive falleth
The chorused lay
Of sigh and sob, unholy glee and terror,
The curse of suffered wrong ;
Waft breeze and storm-gust life’s o’er-saddeued
farer
The burden of a song,
More ghastly strange than even the owl’s weird
screeching
Through night’s dim sphere,
From deep to height the farthest sundered
reaching,
Re-echoing drear;
For there is naught so slowly apprehendeth
That deep refrain,
But to it some responsive murmur sendeth,
Some throb of pain.
The fallen tree, crushed flowers, and withered
grasses
Their record blend
With man’s endeavor foiled, and hope that passes
To futile end.
All pleasant things this spacious earth containeth
Flee fast away,
And there is nothing holy that remaineth
Beyond the day.
We haste along, the desert mirage chasing
More fair, it seems,
Than cloud-shot western sky, with silver tracings
And golden gleams.
“Behold ! ” we cry, “of life the consummation !
Solace of groans.”
Lo ! sand and wash, a weary desolation,
And dead men’s bones !
vio.
ROBT. G. M'LACHLAN.
On the Shore.
The sea hath music in its wave,
That sings of the Evermore ;
But the waters wash on a mouldering grave
When they foam to the sounding shore:
For the ebbing tide leaves the broad sands bare,
And the dead lie scattered there.
Beautiful shells and white, bleached bones,
And roots of the twisted pine,
Torn sea-weeds, and encrusted stones
Roughened in sand and brine ;
But the ocean sings, and the sea-winds sigh,
Till it seems ’twere sweet to die.
Polished shells of perfect mould,
Complete in themselves they seem;
No groping ghouls of the ocean cold
With its cruel charnel gleam.
And strange it is that we feel no dread
When the sea gives up its dead.
Death and decay ; but all so fair
And lulled with such lullaby,
That the keenest life is enchanted there,
Forgetting it too must die—
That when its wondrous house is gone
The ocean still sings on.
Life has a sea with a sounding shore,
We are as shells in the deep :
We hear its music of Evermore,
We live, and we laugh, and weep—
May we, to a God of eternal breath,
Look as fair as we lie in death.
M.L.
AHAU.
Love by Moonlight.
The spirit of a breeze moves through the pines:
Midnight upon the river broodeth low !
Under the high moon’s beams the road’s long lines
Trail white; and on the plains, for miles the snow
Of level moonbeams glitters; shadows show
Where stretches the dark empire of the grass;
Scarce sigh the Avon willows to and fro . . .
Yet moons like this must set, and years must pass j
Within my lady’s eyes the love-light shines;
We tell the moon what only lovers know :
And very softly, where the brown hair twines
About her neck, with clustering curls a-row,
I kiss her, with a lingering kiss and slow,
And thank high Heaven that sends so dear a lass
To walk with me beneath the gold moon-glow.
Yet moons like this must set, and years must pass j
Such nights are like long draughts of finest wines
Pressed from the grapes that in the star-fields
grow,
Meet to be sipped with one whose thought divines
Your thought, and answers silently: and though
No word is spoken, do not you forego
To pledge a toast out of Night’s crystal glass;
Drink to Don Cupid and his golden bow . . .
Yet moons like this must set, and years must pass !

Tl)e Finger Post to Health
This is an Interesting Story
for Liver Sufferers.
The case of
Mr. G. HEATHCOTE.
(BT a special reporter.)
Since enlightened humanity requires that
the trials incidental to sickness should be
minimised wherever possible, and since by
kindliness and consideration much can be
done in that direction, a reporter called
upon Mr. George Heathcote, of Surry-
screet, Waterloo, who willingly assented
to answer any questions.
“ Were you once a tesident of Bunda-
berg, Queensland ? ” asked the pressman.
“ Yes,” answered Mr. Heathcote. “ I
was living there when it was necessary to
cross the liridge by boat instead of walking
over it, or, to be more explicit, during the
time of the great flood, and it was during
that exciting and disastrous period that my
blood was so impoverished through exces-
sively hard work for long hours at a stretch
that sores broke out on my hands and arms,
and my legs swelled terribly. Previous to
this I had suffered from fever and ague,
which left me very weak and shaky, and
for some weeks I noticed that my appetite
was falling off ; but I put that down to
fatigue more than anything else, until other
symptoms cropped up to show me it was
not so, but my 'gyer and kidneys which
were responsible.”
“ What reason,, were there to enable you
to arrive at that conclusion ? ”
“ Any number of them, one of the most
unmistakable being a nasty pain which
caught me here (Mr. Heathcote indicated
the part affected by placing his hand on the
small of his back). My back ached so in-
tensely that my wife made flannel belts for
me to wear, thinking that they would afford
me some relief. If I was doing anything
which caused me to bend down, the pain on
straightening myself was something terrible,
while walking also provoked great distress
and inconvenience. Other proof that my
kidneys were in a bad way was also in
evidence, and as for the rest of my ail-
ments—well, I hardly know where to begin
first.”
“ Tell me I few of the most important—
those that are uppermost in your mind ? ”
“ Oh ! I hive not forgotten any of them,”
was the quick response, “especially the
heavy feelings in my chest, which made me
short-winded. After eating, a weighty sen-
sation appeared in that quarter, and before
I knew where I was my stomach began
swelling out with wind, which made me
very uncomforta le always, and at times
gave rise to sharp, cutting pains. The
sour gases gradually worked their way up,
and caused me to belch, after which, and
until I took a little more food, I felt
slightly easier, but the same unpleasantness
followed each meal, however trifling. When
making my morning preparations for work
1 used to feel as if 1 would like to go back
to bed again, as I was entirely devoid of
energy, and had no desire to attempt any-
thing. This languidness was due, no doubt,
to the restlessness every nig \ I could go
to bed feeling so drowsy tliat my eyes
seemed swollen for the want of rest, yet I
found myself awake the greater part of the
night, and when I got up my limbs were
all aches. Nearly every day I was dis-
tracted with intense headaches, which ap-
peared to bring a mist before my eyes, as
if my sight was failing me, and without any
warning my head became so giddy that I
had to take a rest till the sen~a r ion passed
away. Every month I was getting weaker
and more wretched, beit.g continually be-
set uitli pains under my shoulder-blades,
which travelled right through to the heart,
and caused me considerable anxiety. The
white coating on my tongue explained the
unhealthy condition of my stomach, too,
and what I wanted was a real good medicine
to clear away all disorders and impurities ;
but in those days I did not know as much
as I do now, or I would have been out of
mv troubles very quickly I went from
bad to worstf and had arrived at that
melancholy anl desperate stage when the
least and most simple thing excites a feeling
of irritation, and I am sure you will admit
that such a life of misery is not worth eu-
during. Had I gone on a little longer in
that sorry plight I question whether I
would have been as sound a man as I am
to-day, for I was fairly in the grip of my
complaint, when, owing to the recollection
of some conversations between a few of my
friends, 1 hit upon the i lea of t king some
Clements Tome. I took immediate ac'inn,
as I firmly beloved that further delay
would have brought irretrievable disaster
to my constitution.”
“ Your good fortune came at a very ac-
ceptable time, I should imagine?”
“ Yes ; and a very critical time, too, and
I regretted that I had not bought a few
bottles of Clements Tonic at the outset;
but we all have to buy our experience, you
know, although I intend doing all I can to
help other sufferers by letting them know
what Clements Tonic can do. Personally,
I can truthfully say that it generally re-
vived me from my lethargic state when I
had drank but a small quantity, and I found
myself rapidly improving. Clements Tonic
had a magical effect on my kidneys and
greatly relieved the pains about my back
and shoulders. This relief, coupled with
the abatement of my headaches, had the
effect of making me feel more restful at
nights, and the sound sleep I got through
using Clements Tonic did me an immense
amount of good. My slumber was not in-
terfered with by dreams, and when I awoke
at the usual time for getting up I arose with
alacrity and hastened to my morning meal,
after which I was ready for work. It was
a marvellous change to be full of eoergy
and cheerfulness again, but the invigorating
properties of Clements Tonic were so re-
markable that I could not have restrained
my feelings of joy had I wanted to. Wind
never formed in my stomach after Clements
Tonic got to work, neither did my chest
trouble me, while in the matter of palpita-
tion the same medicine was equal to the
prevailing requirements. There is no
denying the fact that Clements Tonic
was wonderfully effective as a blood
pnrifier, for the sores were gradually
effaced from my hands and arms, and the
cause of my legs swelling—whatever that
cause might have been - was entirely re-
moved. My illness had considerably
affected my nerves, which became shaky
whene er I was disturbed, but to my heart-
felt thankfulness I must say that Clements
Tonic was as effective on my nervous
system as it was on my other deranged
organs.”
“ What was your appetite like then ? ”
“As it wai soon after commencing on
Clements Tonic—very good, indeed, and it
has remained so ever since. Hut the
strengthening qualities of my newly-found
medicine surprised me. for although my
work was very heavy I always got through
it without feeling any ill effects from the
time Clements I onic cured me, and I have
not once had the faintest return of any of
my old symptoms, in acknowledgment of
which I give you permission to publish my
story as it pleases you.”

SATURDAY, APRIL 11, 1903.
The Case of Tasmania, and Other Matters.
Now that Tasmania has finished fighting its
general election the present is as good a time
as any other for the winning party to settle
down to a serious consideration of the
financial state of the island. The fact that
Tasmania is, in some respects (though not in
all) the boss financial drunk and imbecile
of the Commonwealth, lends it a certain
importance that has no relation at all to its
lts dimensions are small, but its public
drunk is enormous. During the recent
electoral campaign certain politicians told
the people that they had “ awakened to a
knowledge of the seriousness of the position,”
and if they really had awakened the fact
would have been of value. Unfortunately
their further utterances showed that they
had not really awakened—only turned over a
trifle in their sleep. Even the most wakeful
of them was still subject to one incurable
delusion —that the debts of this country,
being incurred for public works purposes,
don’t come into anything like the same
category as the debts of other countries,
incurred for wasteful and useless wars ; that
we are not to be judged by the same stan-
dards as other countries; and that our
insolvency will, therefore, be a different kind
of insolvency from theirs. That deep-
rooted impression that no country save Aus-
tralia has assets to set against its public
debt, and that, consequently, all compari-
son, and all admonitions based thereon, are
futile, is an old mental stump that requires
a terrible amount of digging up. As a
solemn matter of fact, very few countries,
save Britain, France, Turkey, and Spain,
now owe any war debt which, when reduced
to so much per head, amounts to anything
worth mentioning.
99mm
Almost the worst example of a European
country without payable assets to show for
its national debt is Great Britain, where
railways and docks are owned by private
enterprise, while tramways are either
private or municipal property. Let this
very bad example be set against Tasmania,
and see how the two stand :
Britain. Tasmania.
Public debt per head .. £l9los. .. £5O
Annual interest per head 10s. 7d. .. £1 17s. 6d.
Total interest .. .. £22,000,000 .. £333,111
Net revenue from works re-
presented by this debt—
Railways .. .. .. £33,111
Suez Canal shares.. .. £830,000 ..
Post and Telegraphs .. 4,550,000 ..
Interest payable out of
taxation ' £16,620,000 .. £300,000
Interest payable out of
jaxation (per head) .. Bs. .. £1 14s. Bd.
These calculations are not accurate to a
penny, for it is impossible to quite keep
pace with changing conditions, but they are
as accurate as the latest available figures can
make them. Britain doesn’t even begin to
compare with such countries as Sweden,
Norway, Denmark, Prussia, and Russia in
the matter of having revenue-yielding assets
to set against its debt, yet Britain, bad as it
is, is vastly ahead of Tasmania. With a
debt not more than one-third as much per
inhabitant as Tasmania’s, it has remunera-
tive works to set against nearly one-quarter
of its annual interest bill, while Tasmania’s
proportionately much larger debt is only
represented by one-tenth its value in pay-
able assets, if the revenue derived therefrom
is accepted as the test of value.
* • * •
The explanation which is generally held
to be sufficient is that this is a new
country which had all its roads, railways,
telegraphs and public buildings to construct,
and therefore debt is its natural and essential
condition. But other countries commenced
to build practically all these things at almost
the same time that we did. Even the old
roads of the old countries were almost all
reconstructed during the last century. The
real point of difference is that this is the
only country where the State was practically
the sole landlord at the time the rail-
way-building era commenced, and which had
almost all the land values of the country
as a railway-building fund. The difference
was all in our favor ; in fact, it is still in our
favor, though with the sale of the most
valuable portion of our public estate, and
the constant turning into cash of the less
valuable part which remains, the advantage is
soon likely to be a thing of the past. Where
the Australian started wrongly was in the
assumption that he should be a shareholder
in an immense railway, telegraph, and dock
concern without paying for his shares. No
man expects to own £35 worth of stock in
the private Deniliquin and Moama or Silver-
ton railways, or in the Commercial Bank or
the Melbourne tramways without paying the
£35, and when he pays it he calls the money
savings. But the Australian does expect
(Heaven only knows why) to own £35 worth
of State railway and tramway without paying
a copper, and if he is asked to pay he doesn’t
call it savings—he calls it outrageous taxa-
tion, and throws out the Government. He
doesn’t expect to get dividends out
of private enterprise without paying
for his shares; he does expect to
get dividends in the shape of reduced
taxation, old age pensions and the like, out
of his State enterprises without paying for
his share in them ; and naturally the scheme
doesn’t work. And in later years the disease
has spread till he expects to get even his
roads and culverts without paying for them.
That scheme, naturally, works still worse.
The result is that an Australian State, which
has a public works policy of fifty years’
standing, now shows, at the end of that
time, less profitable works to account for its
debt than Britain, which only got its payable
assets in an accidental way with no fixed
policy at all.
* * • m
Almost all the European countries
where railways are owned by the State,
work on an idea quite the reverse of
ours. New railways are charged to
revenue, and only that portion of the cost
which the revenue can’t immediately
carry is borrowed, generally with a sinking
fund attached. Even Russia, in the last year
for which accounts are available, paid for all
that year’s new railway expenditure out of
revenue, and paid off part of its old railway
debt as well. As for charging roads,
bridges, dredging works, and such like non-
revenue-yielding and perishable sundries to
the permanent debt, hardly one of the coun-
tries about whose “ war debts ” Australia is
so contemptuous, ever dreams of such a
thing. Tasmania, the horrible example of
the Commonwealth, has got things down to
such a point that, in the five years to the end
of 1901, it spent £336,000 of loan money on
works that paid very badly, and £675,000
on works that didn’t pay at all. Yet the
Tasmanian is firmly convinced that his debt
is in a category vastly superior to that of
such a country, say, as Russia, where the net
revenue from railways alone covers more
than half the annual interest bill. Tasmania
even possessed a Minister, in the Bradbon
days, who stated in Parliament that it was a
perfectly legitimate and solvent proceeding
to build a wooden bridge out of loans, and
keep renewing it out of loans, till the price
of ten bridges was owing, and the tenth
bridge had decayed into mere firewood.
Tasmania didn’t throw out that Minister for
holding these dangerous views; it didn’t
even actively disapprove of him.
• * . *
By reason of its gold, silver, copper and
coal mines, and its amazing luck in inherit-
ing the fee simple of an empty continent
without either fighting or paying for it, the
Australian community is still rich —com-
paratively. It isn’t anything like so rich as
it was, but it is still rich—comparatively.
There are only a few countries where the
average individual has a larger income, and
there is none which holds, in proportion to
population, so much gold coin, out of which
to pay for public works. Yet this is almost
the only country in the world which depends
almost entirely on foreign loans for its public
works funds, and there is now hardly a
country in the world which has so heavy
an unproductive debt, or which is so
devoid of anything in the way of sinking
funds. And there isn’t one which is heap-
ing up unproductive debt so rapidly.
State socialism is in a fair way to be
a failure in Australia, not because it
isn’t the grandest and noblest idea in
the whole political creed of man, but
solely because there has been tacked on
to it a strange disease and delusion
which has, in itself, nothing to do with
State socialism. If our railways belonged to
a certain number only of Australians, these
Australians would expect to pay for them as
the natural condition of ownership. Because
they belong to all Australians, these Aus-
tralians have been taught to be firmly
convinced that they should own them
without payment. If the individual
Australian is invited to have £35 of
his own money invested in the savings
bank he regards it as a great idea ; if he is
invited to have £35 of his own money
invested in his own State railways he calls it
disgraceful tyranny. There is a yearning
for community of goods so far as public
works are concerned, without paying for the
goods. Therefore Australia is going where
parson Tremaynb Dunstan, of Sydney,
lately went. That estimable pastor showed
how a man may go bankrupt on £750 a year,
simply by not paying for things at the outset
and not being able to keep ahead of the com-
pound interest afterwards. Australia, with,
on an average, less than a tenth of Pastor
Dunstan’s income, is also finding it harder
every day to keep .ahead of the compound
interest.
• * • •
The other Australian States are drifting
more or less rapidly in the same direction as
Tasmania, and still the great majority of
people are shocked when the question is
asked: “Is Australia bankrupt?” The
answer doesn’t depend on the country’s
assets ; they are perpetually quoted as an
answer, but they have little more to do with
the case than has the transit of Jupiter
Australia will be bankrupt unless its people
care to shake off the habits and tra-
ditions and delusions of 40 years; un-
less they can become quite a new
people ; unless they can realise that
their principal political ideas have been one
vast insanity ; unless the political leopard
can change his spots and the political nigger
can become white. Australia may become
bankrupt in any case, if the political leopard
postpones the changing of his spots too long.
A great deal depends on whether Australia’s
luck in avoiding attack and keeping out of
expensive wars continues. A great deal
more depends on whether the community,
when it comes to consider the stupendous
sacrifice that would be involved in keeping
straight, thinks it worth while to keep
straight. The problem is an interesting one.
Most Australian politicians simply meet
it in an off-hand way by saying that there
isn’t any problem.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Downfall of Neil Lewis.
Tasmania had an election last Thursday, and
there is nothing left of the Neil Lewis Ministry.
The Premier himself lost his seat, beaten by 150
votes. Treasurer Bird fell in his old age, wiped out
by shirt-merchant Brownell. Lands and Works
Minister Mulcahy was extinguished by almost
two votes to one. Three members of the Lewis
Ministry held seats in the House of Assembly,
and they all went under. It was a rout of the
worst description, and of the old Government
party only about six remain. If the downfall
had been due to the Tory principles of the
Lewis Government, which had a few bright
Democratic ideas and a good many Tory
ones, the situation would be one to be
thankful for, but it was the good qualities of the
Ministry that killed it. It perished of its in-
come-tax and its ambition to make ends
meet. Tasmania lost a huge proportion of
its revenue by the abolition of inter-State cus-
toms, and the Lewis Ministry, after, hesitating
and allowing deficits to accumulate a great deal
too long, decided to square the ledger with a
graduated land-tax, graduated income-tax, in-
creased railway charges, re-assessment of under-
valued lands, and a certain amount of retrench-
ment. It was impossible to retrench much in the
under-manned and ill-paid civil service of Tas-
mania, by far the most cheaply-governed State in
Australasia, and Premier Lewis refused to be a
sweater of a sufficiently bad variety to gratify most
of his opponents. Tasmania bore with Lewis in a
friendly way while he let deficits accumulate, but
when he pushed through his new tax legislation
Tasmania rose against him like one agriculturist.
The West Coast mining districts, which are
largely occupied by people from other and more
E regressive States, returned three Labor mem
ers, which makes the first beginnings of a Labor
party in Tasmanian politics ; but the great
majority of the new members were elected
as people who want to reduce taxation and
chance the consequences. All this happens
at a bad time. N.S.W. Attorney-General
Wise and other excited persons have just
been assuring the London money-lender that
interest is never paid here out of anything
save revenue, and that his security lies in the
“unlimited powers of taxation” possessed by
Parliament —in other words, it lies in the people’s
power to tax themselves through their own Par-
liament. But the power to tax themselves
amounts to very little without the inclination—it
amounts to no more than their equally indubitable
power to boil themselves if they feel a desire to be
boiled, which they don't. Now, just at this awk-
ward juncture our creditors see a Ministry go
down utterly as soon as it tried to raise enough
money to pay interest out of revenue, even though
it proposed to put on less taxation than had
been taken off. It sees the Ministry that wanted
to reduce loans and start an enlarged sinking fund
promptly and utterly abolished. It sees Parlia-
ment’s unlimited power of taxing the people tried
in a very small way, with the result that the
Parliamentary majority which taxed the people
can’t now be found with a microscope. If Cohen
is as wise as his nose would indicate, he will make
a note of these things.
The Victorian Railway Unions.
Three months have passed since the Victorian
Tommy Bent Department gave the unions of rail
way workers notice that they must sever their con’
nection with the Trades Hall. Nothing having
happened since then, the Tommy Bent Department
has again addressed the employees and asked to
know within a fortnight what thev propose dointr
about the matter. If the railway employees’
unions are wise they will continue to do what thev
have been doing, which is nothing. They will
just think hard over the matter indefinitely If
the Bent Department becomes very insistent for a
reply it should be informed, with all possible
courtesy, that the railway unions are thinking
over the matter something tremendous, but
have, so far, been too much tom with’ con
flicting emotions to decide. If the unions
refuse to do what Bent wants they will
make it necessary for an excited Bent to do some-
thing to redeem his reputation as the angriest and
most ill-mannered man in the Government, so the
wisest course is not to refuse, but just to drive the
locomotive as usual. No man should rise up at
any meeting and propose not doing what Bent
desires—in fact, the matter should not be alluded
to in public at all. There is room in a case like
this for the biggest and longest and deadest silence
in Victoria. Bent can then throw himself up
promiscuously against this dead wall of silence till
further notice. There is nothing more futile than
a big angry man hitting at silence with both fists.
A point might be strained, if it was considered
advisable, to enable the railway employees to be
represented at the Trades Hall by someone who
is beyond Bent’s jurisdiction, and everybody
who is within Bent’s jurisdiction should just
consider Bent an ass in his own mind, and leave
him to bray first. Bent can’t sack everybody,
and when everybody is equally buried in thought
it is difficult to decide whom to sack first. If he
does sack anybody over the matter then it will be
time to act, but meanwhile there is wisdom in a
far-off expression and a deeply-abstracted frame
of mind. The difficulty may settle itself if left
alone. Among other possibilities Bent may burst
at any moment with bottled-up emotion.

The Principle of Compulsion.
Syme-on Fraser, by the grace of the Age, a
Senator for Victoria, leading a deputation of
Federated Employers, recently, to Premier
Barton on the subject of industrial arbitration,
“trusted that the Government would not, at a
slack time like this, embitter the relations of
employers and employees by introducing the
principle of compulsion.” Why “ the principle of
compulsion ” should embitter these relations the
Senator didn’t say. If, in Bourke-street, owing
to the bad times, everybody was allowed to drive
as he darn-well pleased, subject only to a process
of voluntary arbitration between him and the
person he drove over, the pretty certain conse-
quence would be that some day, on his way up to
save the country, Senator Fraser would be
landed a cripple in the gutter. Then there would
be unpleasantness; and when the Senator had
got the dirt out of his mouth and bad seen the
doctor’s bill, he would ask in good Scotch, and
with emphasis, why the traffic wasn’t regulated.
If, thereupon, anybody were foolish enough to
reply that to introduce th<f principle of
compulsion would cause unpleasantness, and
embitter relations between the driver and the
executors of the person driven over, especially at
a time when 3 per cent. N.S.W. securities were
down to £B7, Senator Fraser would snort that it
would cause fewer broken heads, anyway. And
supposing, owing to the bad times and the
deficient wool supply, the civil courts, which com-
pulsorily arbitrate between Senator Fraser and
the people who refuse to pay the money they
s‘ustly owe him, were closed up, and Senator
Eraser were left with no remedy save Christian
remonstrance —how would he feel about that?
Suppose even—to put a less extreme case—a
large and violent drunk used threatening
language to Senator Fraser outside Young and
Jackson’s well and favorably known public-house,
and the policeman whom he summoned refused to
do anything more than speak kindly to both
parties till times were better, and A.J.S. Bank
inscribed stock reached at least 125., how would
that appeal to Mr. Fraser ? It is a poor argu-
ment that can’t stand being inspected at the rear
end as well as the front.
Dill Macky Approves.
Cardinal Moran, having suggested in an un-
dignified and distempered speech the formation of
a Roman Catholic association —presumably as a
counterblast to tbe Protestant Defence Associa-
tion—the Rev. Dill Macky has ingenuously ex-
pressed his approval of tbe proposal. Of course
Preacher Dill Macky approves. His precious
association, with its mission of stirring up sec-
tarianism, might at any time be destroyed and
wither like a torn-up weed if the daily newspapers
were to withdraw their support from it and put an
end to reporting religious rowdyism on one side or
the other. But with a Roman Catholic association
also in the field, the Protestant association
would be much safer. The two bodies could lean
up against one another, and each would provide a
fairly solid reason for the existence of the other.
The N.S.W. population does not go to church to
any material extent, but, provided there is enough
encouragement, it will join freely enough m
religious rows. When heads can be broken for
the love of the Lord, the ritual of religion wins a
new attractiveness. There must, however, be the
encouragement. Preacher Dill-Macky has been
doing his best to provide it, and, owing
to the scandalous co-operation of the news-
papers, he has been having some success.
Still, so far, only the fringe of the popu-
lation has been touched —the hoodlums anxious
for a row on any account, the rabbit-brained
capable of being frightened or persuaded into
anything. The great mass of the people looks
upon the whole Macky-Moran squabble with
disgust and contempt. But let a Roman Catholic
association be formed, and it will provide a reason
why thousands should join the Protestant associa-
tion who at present would not dream of doing so,
and they, in turn, will provide a reason for other
R.G.’s to join their particular association tor
stirring up strife, and the two institutions will go
on providing reasons for one another’s exis -
ence indefinitely. Thus folly will breed foliyt
and crime will give rise to crime, and one
broken window to-day will give rise to ten
broken windows and a broken head to-morrow.
Cardinal Moran has given many indications
recently that he has altogether lost that goo
judgment which he was once credited with. Has
the Roman Catholic Church no system by w * uc
it can pension off its servants when they hav
done their work, before they can spoil a goo
record with the querulous follies of advancing
age ? Is there no home for broken-down cardi-
nals, and no ice-chest for overheated !P res by 1 i
clerics, and for the inflamed followers of both
them ?


The Tariff and State Imports.
The decision of the N.S.W. Full Court that
Roods imported to Australia for the use of the
State Governments are not liable to pay duty,
may be good law (that has yet to be settled by a
higher tribunal), but it is certainly bad politics,
and if it is finally established as “good law’’
prompt Parliamentary action will be necessary to
change the law. It would never do for the State
Governments to have the right to import goods
duty free. That would involve a possible
negation of Protection in regard to many
industries, and an injurious differentiation
against private employers. The Australian
State Governments at present carry on
many enterprises which involve the importing
or the manufacturing locally of goods on a
large scale; and the tendency is for Govern-
ment enterprise to extend. At present the
N.S.W. Government is a huge purchaser of iron-
ware of all descriptions, cement, railway car-
riages, machinery, timber, paper, ink, tiles,
pottery, cloth, leather, chemicals, groceries, etc.,
etc. In many of these “lines” it is the
chief purchaser, and its custom is able to
determine the fate of an industry, whether
it is to be local and support Australians,
or foreign and impose a further burden of
remittances abroad on the country. If, then,
State Governments are to be allowed to’import
under Freetrade conditions what goods they
require, there might as well be no Protection at
all in many industries. Experience proves that
when an alleged sturdy Protectionist of the Owe’-
Sullivan type gets into office and is faced with the
necessity of prolonging a works boom on insufficient
funds, he is apt to forget his principles, and act as
a Freetrader to secure a small apparent saving in
the immediate present. Local industry needs to
be protected just as much against'the State
Government as against the individual. The
N.S.W. Supreme Court verdict means that under
the law as it stands, whilst the Austra-
lian Government may declare for high Protec-
tion, State Governments can ignore its decrees,
and follow, as regards their own imports, an
entirely Freetrade policy. And they may ex-
tend their operations to so many classes of
industry that they may become almost the
sole importers of the country. Under such
circumstances a State Government can override
the Federal scheme of Protection in some in-
dustries at once, and in all industries when it
chooses to do so, for there is nothing to prevent a
State abolishing the private importer and doing
all its own importing if it chooses. Then, so far
as that State was concerned, there would be no
tariff, and, with inter-State Freetrade, there
would be no tariff anywhere to all intents and pur-
poses. One State Government that chose to do so
could import dutiable goods free for the whole of
Australia. It seems impossible that a decision
which makes for so much present harm and has so
many possibilities of future damage can be “ good
law ” in spite of the unanimous declaration of the
N.S.W. Judges. But in any case it should not be
allowed to remain as “good law ” when the Aus-
tralian Parliament meets. That body should lose
no time in stating clearly and emphatically that
it intends to impose the same revenue conditions
on State Governments as on State citizens.

The “S.M. Herald’s” Disreputable
Acquaint ances.
The Sydney Morning Herald, in a recent
leading article, gravely defends the swindling
importer after this fashion :
The whole situation created under the Act is a dis-
agreeable one, and, the more successful the Customs
Department is in its prosecutions, the more the feeling
grows that these crime-creating regulations should never
have been rendered possible by the Ministry’s anti-
Federal tariff.
It is a strange thing that this oft-repeated argu-
ment only crops up when some patriotic and
democratic legislation rouses the Herald’s bile.
Of course, law makes crime, and if there were no
law there would be no crime, and so far the
Herald is right. The Education law made the
offence of not sending one’s children to school.
Sanitary laws created it an offence to expectorate
on the pavement or empty offensive matter in the
middle of George-street, Sydney, or to leave large
quantities of bone-dust on the Herald’s doorstep.
Th e law authorising th e issue of paper money created
theoffenceof counterfeiting paper money. Thesta-
tiit-e which prohibits anyone to stave in the head of
“Mr. James” as he goes home from the S.M.
Herald office makes it an offence to stave in the
head of “Mr. James,” and converts an other-
wise perfectly lawful proceeding into a crime.
Temptation and opportunity and law are the
three things that make crime. The chance of
robbing the customs; the chance of defrauding
the local rates department, the income-tax, or
land-tax, or dog-tax, or excise department; the
opportunity of robbing the S.M. Herald office,
or the Bank of New South Wales, or of burgling
Mr. James’s” house; the hope of finding .some
loose change in the pockets of “ Mr. James’s ”
dead body—all these are equally good proof that
indirect taxes, direct taxes, local taxes, the
S.M. Herald, the Bank of N.S.W., and
“Mr. James” should be abolished. Even the
trams and railways should be obliterated by
the Herald’s reasoning, for if there were
no trams and railways, the crime of travelling
thereon without a ticket would never have arisen.
But these are only theories—S. M. Herald
theories. As a matter of practice civilisation
can’t break off short because the S.M. Herald is
on friendly terms with a lot of fraudulent and dis-
reputable characters in the importing business.
And if the tariff were abolished, and the Herald’s
disreputable friends didn’t defraud the Customs
because there were none, the world would be no
single whit the better. The fact that the
Herald’s disreputable friends didn’t perpetrate
this particular swindle would not arise from the
fact that the Herald’s disreputable friends had
become a whit less disreputable, less unpatriotic,
and less of a disgrace to thamoral paper that keeps
company with them. They would be the same
evil-minded, evil-living lot as before —only
their wickedness would be diverted into other
channels. The world can’t afford to abolish the
crime of breaking windows by having no windows
and sitting in the dark. And it ill becomes a
holy paper like the S.M. Herald that, in the
case of the mean, dishonest importer who swin-
dles the customs, it should only see a reason for
having no customs ; while, if a man hits an im-
porter with a bottle, that never, by any chance,
strikes it as a reason for having no importers or
no bottles.

The Naval Tribute Foolishness.
Melbourne Age touches the spot in pointing out
that, when they federated, the Australian States
made no provision for a Federal navy simply
because they had an agreement with the Admiralty
limiting the range of the Australian squadron to
Australian waters. Possibly Australia ought to
have known better than to have trusted blindly to
an agreement which the other side might tear up
almost whenever it pleased. But, then, there did
not appear to be any reason why the other side
should want to. tear it up; on the contrary, the
agreement provided for local defence, and it had
oeen for many years agreed by practically all
j " authorities that Empire defence de-
pended for one of its essentials on local
defence of outlying dependencies. Australia had
received no intimation that this opinion had
tered, and that these local defences were no
onger regarded with a friendly eye. Then the
information was sprung upon this country,
1 t ? r , lnner > that local defences are no longer
looked upon with a friendly eye; that they
are rather regarded as wicked and almost
insurrectionary ; and at the same time Australia
was requested to pay a vastly increased contri-
oution to a fleet which isn’t to remain here for our
fk ? n^ e i n war time - Australia, in fact, is told
that the fleet it pays to defend it will, in future, be
liawe to go away in war time and leave it
efenceless, and at the same time that any
Jdea of organising an Australian fleet which
will defend this country is regarded by the high-
oned British authorities as most objectionable.
Suppose what has been for years regarded as
necessary is presently again admitted to be neces-
®^’.5 hat then ■ Supposing, having agreed to
subsidise a navy which maybe ordered to Hong-
kong or elsewhere, the naval authorities say again
(as they have been saying for years) that local
defences are indispensable ? Only one thing can
in that case follow—Australia, although burdened
with the part-cost of the maintenance of the
miscalled Australian squadron, will, for its own
protection, be obliged to support also a force for
iome defence. So the thing that it says now it
can t afford, it will then have to afford, and the
other thing into the bargain.

The Population Problem.
Ha v ing, m its own quaint way and to its entire
satisfaction, settled the question of finding the
land for people to settle on, by making them the
rack-rented tenants of the big land-grabber,
Melbourne Argus has tackled the question of
finding the people to pervade the said land. The
respectable, but bewildered, journal holds that the
Australian birth-rate is not as buoyant as it should
be ; also, it goes into details, and “the conclusions
froni its premises are frivolous and incompetent,
in the first place, any laws for the suppression of
the use of “preventives” will be practically
ignored ; an insistent demand, backed up by good
coin, is bound to be met by a supply. Suppressive
regulations only mean smuggling and illicit deal-
ing. Moreover, the idea of sundry Argus corres-
pondents . that the traffic can be stopped by
blocking importations is more than absurd • the
drugs mostly used are in daily use for
other and legitimate purposes, and can’t be dis-
pensed with. Secondly, there are two classes
of whom the complaint is made that their families
are too small, even invisible; these two classes are
the men who can t afford it, and the women who
won t, because they think more of a figure than a
family, prefer a ball-room to a nursery. For these
latter there is no remedy, nor would it be much
good if there were; their progeny is likely to be
weedy, the spawn of weaklings mental, moral,
and physical; their contributions to the race
would be neither permanent nor advantageous;
let them go. But the other class must be con-
sidered if the race is to continue. It is from the
children of the worker the race is recruited, and
the worker has begun to say of late years, in
silent but insistent fashion, that he will not sub-
ject his sons to the struggle he has endured, and
that he will in future, as far as he is able, not
handicap himself unnecessarily. He hears his
starving child cry for bread when he is
unemployed, and he asks “What better
am I? ” It is here that the yeoman has one
tremendous advantage over the city toiler; though
the two incomes, pound for pound, be absolutely
equal, the man on the land can rear a family at
far less cost than his city brother. Until they
reach the age when they begin to bring money in,
the farmer’s children cost him hardly anything
beyond the money for an occasional new suit and
boots ; the city worker has to put his hand in his
pocket for everything. There— in the capability
to raise decent-sized families—is where lies the
profit to the State of putting the men on the land,
from the dairy-farmer and cultivator, down past
the village-settler to the workman’s-home occupier
with his acre or so. Every man necessarily
cannot be “on the land,” but, whether he is
there or elsewhere, it is none the less the State’s
duty, if it expects him to raise a family,
to first make sure that he shall have a
fair chance of the wherewithal to raise it.
A casual stranger might be excused for doubting
the genuineness of this cry for more population,
since in every Australian city and town he would
see child-life regarded as heedlessly as a piece of
waste paper; he would see Governments that
apparently thought no more of the culpable waste
of a score of baby citizens than they would of the
spilling of a box of wax matches. Australia
would go a long way to increasing its population
as fast as the most prolific nation if it would spend
more money and time and care in rearing the
children it does breed. (Incidentally it would
also make much profit out of the deal.) And in
addition to raising its birth-rate by making the
lives of its workers better worth living, it can
increase its population by immigration by making
it worth a white foreigner’s while to come here.
Canada is to-day showing this country how to do
that, with its 75 per cent, tariff. Anyhow, the
falling birth-rate and the dozens of letters in the
Argus clearly show that the worker, especially he
of the city, has arrived at the conclusion that the
fuller his quiver the less is he blessed, and he is
now acting on that deduction, and the only way
the Commonwealth can hope to draw him away
from that notion is to provide him and his
apprenticable sons and daughters with ample,
properly-paid work in mills and factories, and
make the locked-up lands available for close
settlement. That plan should at least be tried
before attempts are made at any futile scheme for
compulsorily forcing up an unwilling birth-rate.

The Toryism of Sydney “Telegraph.”
Sydney D.T., under its new editor, departs more
and more from any affectation of radicalism. On
the Victorian constitutional reform position it
openly barracks for the Property House after this
fashion :
Parliament has shown that it is competent to reform
itself. The convention idea, which was adopted by the
Turner-Peacock Government, was fundamentally unfair,
inasmuch as it was intended to be chosen by the As-
sembly’s constituency, and it would have been irrespon-
sible (vanishing when it had done its work), costly, and
certain to delay reform. The Council has successfully
asserted its right, as a co-ordinate House of the Legisla-
ture, to have a real voice in reforming the Constitution.
The first attitude of the Government and Assemblv
amounted to an insolent assertion of the Assembly’s
superior power in the making of Constitutional changes,
and this had to be abandoned. Under the provisions of
the bill the Council is likely to become a stronger House.
It will have the right to request amendments in financial
measures (one-House financial legislation has had a
disastrous history in Australia).
As an unscrupulous misrepresentation of the case
this puts up a pretty fair record. The Turner-
Peacock proposal was that a convention, for the
purpose of drafting a new Victorian Constitution,
should be elected oy the 135,000 Council electors
and the 140,000 electors who have votes only for the
Assembly. Every Council elector was _to
have his say in the choosing of that Convention,
and any statement to the contrary is a lie and a
slander. The only visible reason why the idea
was utterly abhorrent to the Council, and the
Telegraph, and the new Telegraph editor, and
the whole blighting dead-weight of Toryism, was
that any and every elector was to be qualified to
sit in the Convention. Instead of being, as at
Council elections, allowed to choose only rich
Tory landowners, whom most of them hate and
despise—which is just as shamefully unfair as if
only Protectionists were qualified to sit in the
Australian Parliament, and the Telegraph had
to vote for them or not at all—the Council elec-
tors were, for once, to be represented by anybody
they wished to appoint. That was where the utterly
objectionable nature of the Turner-Peacock
Convention proposal came in. As for the jibe that
“one-House financial legislation has had a disas-
trous effect in Australia ” that is also a lie, for
there has never been one-House financial legisla-
tion in any Australian State since the luckless bi-
cameral system commenced. Every Upper House
has had absolute power to reject every Loan Bill
that came before it, by way of insisting on the
country living within its means—and can anyone
recollect when one single Loan Bill has been re-
jected ? Every Upper House has had power to
throw out every disastrous railway scheme in its
State’s history, and how many have been thrown
out ? It is under two-House financial legislation
that Australia has accumulated all its disasters:
it had now better try the one-House variety for a
change.

Sydney Labor Council held a meeting last week
at which various things were proposed—this being
one of them :
Mr. Rose declared he would be in favor of borrowing
another £17,000,000 to distribute among the people who
required it. If they could not get the money in London,
let them go to another country. He was sure that
“ Yankee Doodle ” would fork out what they required.
If they went to Germany he believed the Kaiser would
put his hand into his pocket and give them as much
money as they wanted.
First instinct of the distressed Australian—to
borrow something. And last year N.S.W. im-
ported something like .£18,000,000 worth of goods
from foreign countries, at least half of which could
have been easily made here. That would mean
£9,000,000 worth of work, £9,000,000 worth of
wages, employment at =£lso a year each for 60,000
men, and support for their6o,ooofamilies. But New
South Wales persistently sends Freetrade impor-
ters to the Australian Parliament, and votes for not
having these industries, and not having £9,000,000
a year to distribute, and not having work for these
60,000 extra men, and it is N.S. W.’s vote that
made the Federal tariff the comparatively futile
thing it is.

The Maoriland W.C.T.U. has just completed its
annual wallow, or convention, and has emerged
from the mud-hole of moral consideration with its
mouth full of shocking information about the
social evil, illegitimacy, disrespectful assaults,
age of consent, and other tasty fragments chipped
from the weakness of humanity. The delegates
struggle back on to the dry shore of everyday life,
cheered and invigorated, and armed with material
sufficient to make the proceedings at every
Thursday sewing guild in the country sizzle like
a fried sausage for a year to come. As a result
of the Convention’s deliberations, it is abundantly
manifest that the world outside of the clamorous
coterie of Cant is made up of irretrievably tight
men and loose women. The usual deputation,
largely composed of the usual helpless and hope-
less spinsters, was appointed to wait on the
Premier, urging him to raise the age of consent
from 16 to 21 years. Save as affording an oppor-
tunity for a number of ladies to speak severely to
a man on an embarassing subject, these annual
deputations are not remarkable in any way. They
do very little harm to the morals of the Premier,
and they lead to nothing worth mentioning. There
is the usual speech, the usual glare at Seddon
through several pairs of blue spectacles, and the
usual answer. Then the deputation gathers up
its moral titbit, and backs off into private life.

J. H. Carruthers, leader of N.S.W. Opposition,
at the Retail Grocers and Storekeepers’ Associa-
tion picnic, t’other day:
Although he had voted against female suffrage being
made law, on the grounds that he thought more of the
women than those who had given them the right to vote
—now that the women had the franchise, he believed
their moral influence would raise the tone of the political
life of the country, and make the Parliament of the State
better than it was to-day.
In other words, either (1) Mr. Carruthers deli-
berately and wickedly voted against a measure
that would make the N.S.W. Parliament better;
or (2) he voted as he did because his ignorance
and stupidity led him then to think woman
suffrage was bad ; or (3) he talks as he does now
because his ignorance and stupidity lead him now
to think woman suffrage is good ; or (4) he simply
said woman suffrage was bad when he thought it
wasn’t coming, and he simply says it is good now
that it has come, and it is necessary to placate
the woman voter, and it is all a matter of oiliness
and expediency. Anyhow, Carruthers says
now that woman suffrage is a good thing, and
that he fought against it.
Nearly the whole Australian tragedy was written
in three magnificent paragraphs in Friday’s papers
last week :
1. —Altogether nearly £4,500,000 in gold has been
shipped from the Commonwealth during the first quarter
of 1903.
2. —The coroner inquired to-day into the suicide of
Eliza Jane White. Her husband stated that she had
been despondent owing to poverty. The husband tried
to comfort her, but on Tuesday she disappeared, leaving
the following letter :—“ I cannot bear to live on and see
you suffer as you do in mind. We have loved each other
dearly and got through great reverses, but now there
seems no hope or prospect for our future. Do not try to
find me, as it is better I should go into oblivion and not
be an expense to anyone. You, unfortunately, I know,
have not any means.”
3.—THE UNEMPLOYED.
Labor Council Discussion.
Suggestions for Creating Work.
Further Public Borrowing Favored.
Four and a half millions gone in three months to
pay interest on money which we have borrowed to
get things that mostly do not pay, or to pay for
things that we ought to have been making or do>ng
for ourselves; our people starving because they
can’t get work, the things they ought to make
being imported; and the people’s leaders still
crying for no other remedy than a hair of the dog
that bit them.

There begins to be hope when men like David
Elder, of the N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Co., the
Union Trustees Co., and other concerns of the
kind, will publicly say most of the things that
The Bulletin has been saying for years regarding
Australian finances:
It would be well for the Government of the day to
make an authoritative announcement that, except for
loans for conversion purposes and possibly for irrigation
works, no further appeals for money would be made to
the British investors, and this could safely be done, as
there was now a sufficient yearly accumulation of savings
to meet all other prudent public expenditure. In addi-
tion, it would be well also for Parliament to give the
Savings Bank Commissioners power to purchase deben-
tures in the London market when selling at a price which
would show a margin of profit over what the bank is
paying depositors.
This was spoken “ from the cold-blooded point of
the investor; ” but the result is precisely tne same
if the position is viewed from the warm-blooded
Australian nationalist standpoint. The first prin-
ciple of patriotism, in fact, is to pay your debts,
keep out of pawn, and retain possession of your
own soul.

Aggregate Respectability, through its represen-
tative men, its parsons and its press, holds up its
hands in horror at the spectacle of two boxers en-
gaged in what it calls “a brutal fight to a finish,”
yet the same Respectability utterly declines the
intervention of any third party when itself en-
gaged in an even more savage combat. The
locked-out Outtrim miners have repeatedly asked
the mine-owners to go to arbitration, and these
have just as repeatedly refused, preferring to fight
the matter out till one side or the other is ex-
hausted and wives and children are starving.
And fool Governments stand idly by and allow
such fights to a finish to take place, while their
citizens and their property are wrecked and ruined.

“ Arthur D.” writes :
. Under the Western Lands Act, Parliament undoubtedly
intended that the lands of the Western Division should
be controlled by a Board free from political influence.
The salaries fixed were high—£lsoo per annum for the
Chairman and £lOOO for each of the other Commissioners
—while the appointments were made for a period of seven
years. Evidence does not seem wanting, however, that
the Minister for Lands intends to, as far as possible
assert his views in much the same fashion as he and his
predecessors have done in dealing with Land Boards in
the past. The evil of Land-boardism has always been the
levying of excessive rents by means of the leverage
afforded by the tenants’ improvements—a process of
extracting the life’s blood of the pastoral industry which
has worked most successfully for the revenue, in spite of
the many checks received by Lands Boards from the
Land Court. It was not unreasonable to hope that a new
and independent order of Land Board would determine
at all costs to put an end to the evil which has for so long
had a bad effect on our staple industry. The provisional
rents, however, gazetted by the Commissioners—amount-
ing as they do to an average of 21 ’6 pence per sheep for
the Wilcannia district and to about 20 pence per sheep
for Goodooga holdings—are more suggestive of an astute
lawyer, bent on raking in the shekels, than of an inde-
pendent Board appointed under an Act, the 19th section
of which lays down that “in no case shall the rent or
license fee be fixed at a higher rate than seven pence per
sheep on the carrying capacity.”
