“ Yanco ” writes from a Western town
I notice in your issue of 21st inst., a leader under the
head of “An Unemployed Deputation,” which certainly
boils down the question of the future government of
Australia to the dimensions of a nutshell. But, more’s
the pity, to some extent it will be so much wasted energy
cm your part, because it seems to be an utter impossi-
bility to get the Australian to see past the end of his
proverbial nose. In your “ Plain English ” column I also
notice a reference to State education as advocated by
Minister Perry. . Besides instilling honor, patriotism,
integrity, self-reliance, and (above all) independence, it
would be to Minister Perry's credit to have your leader
printed on calico, in big type, and hung in every school
in the State, and to have teachers specially instructed to
inculcate the same into the young brains under their
charge. It would also be a good idea to have the
same nailed to every gum-tree in the Commonwealth.
Even then the casual Australian would prob-
ably take a generation or two to wake up to a
sense of his actual position. If Australia lived
under Freetrade—in its actual sense, and not in the pie-
bald sense that is now attached to the term—it would
produce no revenue from the Custom-house. Then White
Australia, with its “ inexhaustible natural resources,” its
eight-hour system, and its minimum wage, would find it
impossible to compete against the made-in-Germany stuff
and similar tack manufactured in other foreign countries.
If, therefore, the Freetrade party had its way, practically
the whole population —barring, of course, the “man on
the land ” and those engaged quarrying those mountains
of gold out Arltunga way—would be more or less engaged
in the importing trade. Analysing their policy, we get
this mournful picture. Imagine a man perched on Circu-
lar Quay firmly convinced that Freetrade, eight hours,
and a minimum wage are ideals for the people, and also
resolutely convinced that it is impossible to compete
against foreign productions, awaiting the arrival of the
foreign vessel, manned by colored crews, and stocked
with goods the production of sweated serfdom, and then
borrowing money from foreign Jews at 4 per shent, out
of which to pay himself his own wages in order to enable
him to purchase the necessaries of existence. Surely
another sad example of the waiting-for-something-to-crop-
up trait of the Australian national character. The sooner
Australia gets a new school of politicians the better.
♦
The drought has filled the Far West (N.S.W.)
tracks with evil-smelling camels, and dotted the
townships with still more evil-smelling camel
camps; and Broken Hill, which has enough native
odors, begins to rebel, and to kick the colored
alien hard. But the alien, who has waxed fat and
offensive, dosen’t take it sitting; one Musa Khan,
who drives the flashest turn-out in the place',
makes a public defence that it is the white who is
the mean person. “It will not be very long
before white men,” he says, “will be employed as
camel drivers because the Afghans are demanding
too much wages.” And in that Musa Khan pro-
bably speaks the truth this far: that, though
the alien will still accept little, the white
man is being driven to accept less. From the
national point of view, an even worse feature is
the “settling” of the Afghan; round almost
every camp there is already growing up a piebald
population. The white man has only one hope :
he must keep the tinted alien out.
■ ♦
In a logical, forceful speech lately, Vic. M.L.A
Anstey showed Premier Irvine and his Bent
what they should have known all along—that the
Victorian railways pay better than the average of
thinly-peopled countries, given a fair comparison,
and that there is no hope of making them pay
better, except by closer settlement on the locked-
up lands alongside. The new Commissioner from
Canada—whose salary jumps at a bound from
£9OO to £3soo—is foredoomed to failure. The
Canadian lines in ’Ol averaged a little over 2 per
cent, interest, and Canada’s 18,000 miles of line
were helped along by the rents from, and the par-
tial sale of, 52,000,000 acres of land. One-fourth
of the capital of the Dominion’s private lines
was a present from the people of Canada.
The Canadian co.’s don’t have to fence their
lines, or provide cattle-pits all over the
country, or carry seed wheat and starving stock
free, or convey produce at special drought rates.
Their working expenses for every train mile run
are heavier than those of Victoria, and they make
less profit on each mile run with all their ad-
vantages. The Irvine-Bent Government brings
a man from this land of railway failure to
show how to make the Victorian railways pay,
when they pay already a great deal better than
the lines in Tait’s own country. Evidently Bent
and Irvine don’t understand a joke when they see
one.

HISTORY REPEATS ITSELF.
About the time Australia was taken possession of by Captain Cook, the following
was law in some of the then British Colonies of America : “That whosoever kills, or
suffers to be killed, or sells or disposes of to any person whom he has reason to believe
intends to kill, any Ewe Lamb shall be guilty,” &c., &c.
Most of the thoughtful and patriotic colonists of New South Wales are of opinion
that our Legislature might do worse than enact something of the same kind for the
protection of the remnant of the State’s flocks which have been decimated by the
droughts of the past seven years. If our Statesmen should rise to the occasion and do
what is necessary, Mutton and Sheep’s Tongues would of course be much enhanced in
value. In any case, you can now obtain the ROSEWARNE PACKING CO. S
brand, which is the best in the market, at the same price as they were in the time ot
plenty. Try them ; they are delicious, wholesome, and economical.

♦
Fitzgeralds’ Circus opened at Belmore
Park last Saturday to a city practically bereft of
its theatres. There are two or three novel fea-
tures in the show and a fair number of old ones.
The kicking pony January, and Silver King, a
quieter type of beast, are here as before. The
performing dogs have learned to walk kangaroo
fashion and to dress themselves, and are other-
wise estimable beasts. The Walhallas annoy
no one by pitching their hats on each other’s
heads; and Miss Katie Montgomery is, as before,
principal equestrienne in the show. The cycle whizz
is undoubtedly the second best item in the perform-
ance. Four cyclists ride a specially-constructed
track at an angle of 60 degrees, and the smash-up
that would ensue if Lycurgus shifted the hun-
dredth part of an inch too near Curdello, or
the foot of Summers grazed the right ear of
Shand, is interesting to speculate upon. Herr
Pagel, the main feature of the show, does not pre-
sent the top-heavy aspect of Sandow, and in his
Samson-like attire has altogether a better appear-
ance than his brother giant. He ties a horse to
his waist and mounts a ladder, supports a plat-
form with 18cwt. upon it, and, entering the lion’s
den, tosses that personage about in the air and
shows the audience his pretty teeth. After
which he folds his arms and looks abnormally
gentle. Gilleno, an excited-looking gentleman in
a red magician’s robe, plays tunes on various de-
funct animals and kitchen utensils, and by waving
an oblong box above his head produces the effect of
chimes. He discourses lively melodies on cabbages
and sad ones on ducks, though why a duck should
be sadder than a cabbage is not explained. His
anthem on a pair of bellows may also be com-
mended. Some persons, described as Flying
Meteors, move about the circus by means of roll-
ing globes and trapezes, and the exhibition of
high-jumping by Newhaven and Stockman rounds
off the show in a satisfactory manner.
* * * *
Several Sydney theatres, including the
Lyceum and the Criterion, farewelled last Satur-
day, Bland Holt finishing up with “The War of
Wealth,” Johnny Sheridan ended as he began
with “The Lady Slavey.” Johnny was given a
fold watch on this occasion, which is only right.
)uring the season John has been bestowing
watches, bangles and eulogistic speeches on various
members of his company. Mostly everyone in the
co. has got a gold watch or some other trophy.
Some of them, for their acting, ought to get six
months. The company expects to return in seven
weeks.
* * * «
At Sydney Palace “On and Off” concludes
with this week. It has proved a far better draw-
ing attraction than “The Wrong Mr. Wright,”
and should do well in the other capitals. “Are
You a Mason?” will introduce to the Sydney
public an entirely new English comedy co. at the
Palace next Saturday. Sydney likes new faces —
especially when they are pretty faces. Some of
the “ Mason ” co.’s girls are very pretty, and the
rest are beautiful. A brilliant first night and a
successful season may be therefore anticipated.
* * * * '
On Saturday many things are supposed to
happen in Sydney. The Royal re-opens with the
gorgeous “Dick Whittington,” in which the com-
bined gorgeousness of J. C. Williamson and his
old-time partner, George Musgrove, is to glare on
an astonished public. Frank Thornton is to per-
vade the Criterion on the same night with
“Charley’s Aunt,” and as Thornton has never yet
failed to strike it rich in Australia the probability
is that he won’t fail this time. The little, swift,
crinkled comedian who first really dawned on this
country when the eye of the Private Secretary
looked apologetically round the door, and
the attached voice asked if the goloshed
feet might come in, has never, since that
episode, lost his hold on Australia’s affections.
William Anderson also opens the Lyceum on
Saturday with a huge melodrama, “ A Woman of
Pleasure.” These, added to the circus, and the
Tivoli, and the fact that Williamson is staging a
new show at the Palace, and the yesterday’s
undigested Bun, are likely to make Sydney a busy
place on Saturday. At time of writing, how-
ever, most of the theatres are closed, and there is
little stirring socially, intellectually or politically
(save where all three parties are explaining that
they won at Tamwortn), and the note of expect-
ancy, which is said to be Australia’s dominant
characteristic, is more than usually in evidence.
* * * *
The “ Vesuvio Concert Co.” appearing at
Sydney Tivoli last Saturday, looked at first sight
a joke. A group of very plump Italians, dressed
in comic-opera fishing costume (and patent-leather
boots) —they strongly reminded the audience of
the swarthy Levantines who bring the plates
around in the temples of the immoral oyster and
the pernicious prawn. But the Italian gentlemen
in the fishing costumes (and patent-leather boots)
were not a joke. They took themselves with
extreme seriousness, especially an extra-plump
tenor whose killing glances searched every female
face from the gallery to the stalls; and, on the
whole, they deserved to be taken fairly seriously.
The instrumentalists are very good—especially a
violinist with the unhappy name of Strumillo.
The tenor has a very small but still a sweet voice,
a much-diluted Dani voice. The baritone has to
be taken more than seriously, for he is a very
solemn circumstance indeed. For Easter week
the Tivoli has prepared an almost entire change
of programme. The “first part ”isto be restored
with a Tennis Court scene ; Trevolo, a ventrilo-
quest from U.S.A., Rod da Perry, the alto, Seeley
and West with a musical turn, and H. C. Buckler,
baritone, will be among the new people, and Hill
and Sylvainy will shine as of yore.
* * * *■
The tent which Wirth Bros, pegged out
upon the lowlands, sou’-east of Prince’s Bridge,
contains seating accommodation for 4000 people,
but the space doesn’t exceed the Easter demand
for circus. Melbourne loves circus with a deep,
abiding affection, and the present show gives two
or three new knocks to the old popular weakness.
There are two rings at Wirths’—one for the
educated ’osses and the various human marvels of
the arena ; and a companion ring with iron rail-
ings round it, where three tigers kindly refrain
from chawing their sovereign lord, the King of
Animal Trainers. Tiger Kitty walks a tight-
rope, and contributes a beautiful jockey act, after
which her mates join her in a round of dissipa-
tion. The tigers are a bright entertainment.
A combination of elephant, camel, bear and
monkey is humorous mainly on account of the
weird, self-contained camel ; also the clowns have
fleeting moments of humor, especially the one de-
scribed as “ Continental.” The toilers who turn
somersaults and hang on to things by their heels
are only second-rate Marvels, whereas the gee-gee
section of the show is first-rate and not too long
drawn out. Most of the 30 turns on the programme
are necessarily brief, because the Howard Bros,
performance lasts a full half-hour. The Howards
puzzled a critical audience in the small hall at the
Athenaeum some days ago. Their display at the
Circus, where Andy, the roaming brother, can be
only faintly audible to James, the blind revealer,
is a more astounding achievement. When the
vociferous Howard, who sits in darkness, an-
nounces that his remote coadjutor is looking at a
shark’s tooth or a female baby, and, furthermore,
throws in the name of the baby as a guarantee of
good faith, the success of Wirths’ Circus seems
assured.
** * *
Three of the Melbourne theatres will be
closed on Thursday. On Saturday all five houses
enter into a violent competition, which is the soul
of business and the cause of grief. At Her
Majesty’s, the end of “The Emerald Isle” was
fixed for Monday, and some more “Geisha” is
now happening. On Saturday comes a revival of
“Ma Mie Rosette,” the dear girl who dreamed
that she went to the back door of King Henri’s
palace with a cargo of eggs and butter, and got
taken in and caressed by that sultry potentate.
It is understood that Carrie Moore will play
Rosette, whilst Thorley pursues the girls with
ardor, and Hugh Ward impersonates the remnants
of a war-stained colonel.
** * *
At the Princess’s the long expected pro-
duction of “A Midsummer Night's Dream”
comes to pass on Saturday. About 15 imported
artists will face an Australian audience for the
first time in this spectacular work of W. Shake-
speare, and many tons of machinery have been
imported to do honor to the deceased gentleman’s
genius. If Melbourne wants an impressive revival
of William’s fairy comedy the coming production
will doubtless fill the want. Meanwhile it looks
as though the mind of General Desire is not quite
made up.
*# * *
Bland Holt, with his co.. and army of
camp followers, returns to Melbourne this week
for the purpose of resuming business at the Royal
next Saturday. A whole year has passed since
the indefatigable Sultan of Melodrama spread
himself upon Australia’s largest stage, and he
intends to make full use of the floor-room on this
joyful occasion. “The Prodigal Daughter” was
ever an exciting horse show and a particular
feast of Brunton. This time the scenery promises
to be even more charmingly Bruntonic than
heretofore, also the preparations for the Grand
National Steeplechase are of an appalling descrip-
tion.
** * *
The Willoughby-Geach co., which received
its baptism of praise at Melbourne Bijou last
Easter, will come back to its starting point on
Saturday (11th) with the laurels of a 12 months’
tour twined around its brow. The particular
farcical comedy selected by George Willoughby
for his triumphal re-entrance is “ Mistakes Will
Happen.” Charles Arnold bought it in England
for the funniest of comedies, and the verdict of
Melbourne is expected to be uproarious.
** * *
Edward Lloyd makes his first Melbourne
appearance at the Town Hall on Saturday (11th),
and all citizens possessed of intelligent ideas about
the art of ballad singing will go out of their way
to hear Edward Lloyd sing. Age has not
withered his famous tenor voice, and the advt.
which describes Lloyd as the greatest living tenor
is speaking no more than truth. He will sing at
four ballad concerts, the last of them being dated
Friday, 17 th.
** * *
At Adelaide Tivoli, G. W. Hunter (of
London) shows himself a comedian of the most
E renounced London type. Most of his songs are
is own composition. Brownlow is going in for
action-songs, and works his audience up to
immense enthusiasm with “Gunga Din.” Two
new end-men, Cottier and Rocks, have replaced
Pope and Sayles, who are getting up a small
travelling co. of their own. Manager Pollock, of
the Royal, is back from the other States with a
long list of bookings, and the theatre opens this
Saturday with a programme that stretches
straight ahead to next February. Beaumont Read,
the veteran falsetto singer, is to take a Town Hall
farewell on Good Friday. It may be mentioned,
as a side issue, that the colored brother has been
running Adelaide this week. What with Taylor
at the Oval, being personally introduced to the
vice-regal party, and having a badge hung round
his neck by Lady Smith ; Irving, Sayles and
Charles Pope pretty nearly running the show at
the Tivoli (and Brownlow at the same house
assuring Gunga Din that “ You’re a better man
than I am ”), there seems hardly room for the
mere White Man to turn round.
** * *
The story of the Howard Bros, reminds
one of the broguey Irishman on “ The Emerald
Isle ” who states in a plain, commercial way that
he “ learned blind fiddlin’ ” from his father.
Howard pbre was a medical man with a leaning
towards wizardry, and he trained his little daugh-
ter in the art of “blind-seeing,” or whatever its
technical name may be. Dr. Howard and little
Louie were well-known entertainers long before
the Howard Bros, made their mark. They have
been at work on their secret means of communi-
cation ever since they were children, and they are
utterly dependent upon one another for their suc-
cess. The brothers may be said to smash up the
old conjuring-book stories as to how the “second
sight ” answers are obtained from a blindfolded
performer. It has been gravely stated that a
speaking tube runs under the stage into the
wizard’s chair and he gets his information that
way. The Howard seer has his chair on the cold
ground, and answers on the instant questions
which his brother is dropping—quite casual like—
from the far end of the circus.
A Warning.
N.S.W. Opposition candidate has won the Tam-
worth by-election through the splitting of the
“ other-side ” vote between a Government and a
Labor candidate. The electors evidently re-
garded the Government and Labor parties as two
souls with but a single loan, two hearts that bor-
row as one, and those who were not misled by the
Sydney daily papers to vote for the Cohen candr
date—the man whose election is to make the
getting of a loan easier—misled themselves by
supporting either the Government man or
the Labor man. If there were any electors in
Tamworth really seized of the necessities of
the present situation in N.S.W. they probably
did not vote at all, for it was not possible to
imagine that anyone’s success would be of value.
The Labor candidate, by the way, got a con-
temptuously small vote, and that should help to
impress upon his organisation the hopelessness of
continuing in its present hide-bound policy. Most
of the benefits that the Labor party promised the
industrial classes of the cities it has now secured
—direct taxation which wouldn’t tax them,
industrial arbitration, old age pensions, early
closing of shops and regulation of factories,
a Government minimum wage and day labor
system, and so on. Now the party must
move to fresh fields of reform if it is to
continue its existence. The two most pressing
(and in a measure co-related) reforms have to do
vvith the finances and with landlordism. There is
demanded now of a genuine Reform Party a
stoppage of the borrowing system and a destruc-
tion of the system by which companies and in-
dividuals—in many cases absentees —are allowed
to monopolise much of the best agricultural land
of the State, forcing the poor man who wishes “ to
go on the land ” out into the arid west. On
neither of these questions does the Labor Party
speak with anything like decision. If it were to
declare for a graduated land tax —falling with
special severity on big estates and with
overwhelming severity on the estates of
absentees —and for a higher income tax on
very big incomes, and a specially high income-tax
for absentees, there would, with proper economy,
be a quite sufficient revenue to allow of a complete
stoppage of borrowing. The graduated land-tax
would not alone benefit the Treasury directly but
would also force up the railway and customs
receipts. The Labor party must go to the country
next election, with a radically progressive plat-
form for the future, or it will fall. It cannot rely
on its past. A past is of no use in politics. To
stay on and be a mere borrowing party and a
party that heads unemployed deputations is of no
use either.

“ Scalpel ” writes : Touching “ Ooloo’s ”
“tales of horror” (B. 14/3/’O3). A Sydney doctor
once gave me particulars of the shuddersome case
in which one of Victoria’s leading surgeons, having
operated on a woman for uterine trouble, closed
up the wound leaving inside the patient a sponge
and a pair of bulldog forceps ! A fact almost as
strange as the strange story of the operator who
left a patient’s liver on the table and forgot to
replace it. I don’t think the Victorian surgeon
ever got his tools back, but he was an airy sort of
practitioner and never troubled about trifles.
“ T.D.” : There is not the slightest chance,
while the Australian Labor parties continue to see
no further than their noses, of any adoption of
The Bulletin’s suggestion that the wives of
imprisoned husbands should be provided for out
of the proceeds of work done by their worse halves
in gaol. A good deal of work used to be done in
the Vic. gaols, but the Trades’ Hall influence put
an end to that, and all that is done in Pentridge
now is the manufacture of a few articles to supply
interior wants. Even the official departments
have been restrained from availing themselves of
the good workmanship done by gaoled cabinet-
makers and others. It is impossible to convince
the average Labor member, or the man who wants
to be a Labor member, that work is wealth, and
that it is better to have a hard-working convict
supporting a well-fed family than a useless
prisoner and a charity-seeking wife and kids.
A few examples of how the N.S.W. Public
Service Board’s regulations work out:
A junior in the Professional Division applies for the
annual increase and is informed by the Board that there
is no provision made for increases to the Professional
Division—only for the Clerical. This is just after he has
passed his exam, to enable him to be promoted from the
Clerical to the Professional Division, so his hard study
and his success and his promotion only result in
stopping all further expansion in his salary. Another
officer in the Clerical Division is promoted to
the Professional and receives £l5O per annum
after 13 years’ service, and then stops at that salary,
while a junior clerk, who enters the service at £5O
a year, receives £l3O in three years without passing any
examination—not even the one which the regulations say
he must pass in order to get beyond £llO. Another clerk
passes an examination which, according to the regula-
tions, brings him a higher salary. He doesn’t get the
higher salary. On application he learns that the Board
is looking into the matter. That is six months ago, and
the Board is still looking. All this is the record of one
small office.
The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The N.S.W. Government has, so far won its
silly case (apparently undertaken out of sheer mean can
tankerousness) against the Australian Government and
got a decision that goods imported by .the State Govern
ments are not liable to duty (a decision which, it is to be
hoped, in the interests of common sense and of Australia’s
prosperity, will presently be reversed on appeal! ww
has it gained ? This is how things were— ’ 1
(1) The N.S.W. State Government paid the duty on its
imports to the Australian Government. (Where the
imports were goods paid for out of loans, it, out of its
natural and essential inability to go straight, paid the
duty out of loans.)
(2) The Australian Government handed back the duty
at the monthly division of the surplus, and
State Government put it into revenue. (This dodge of
transferring loan money to revenue was no part of the
Federal Customs system ; it arose out of the inability of
the N.S.W. Government to go straight if there was a
chance to go otherwise.)
And this is how things will be now if the disastrous
decision of N.S.W. Supreme Court holds good.
(L) The Australian Government will deduct from its
payments to N.S.W. the amount of the duty which it
wrongly refunded to N.S.W.—that will come out of
N.S.W. revenue.
(2) With this sum it will repay N.S.W. the amount it
wrongly charged, to N.S.W.—that will, in common
honesty, go back into the N.S.W. loan funds.
(3) The N.S.W. revenue, which already shows a large
deficit, will therefore show a larger deficit.
(4) N.S.W. pays all the law costs, for the Australian
Government can only pay by having so much less surplus
to refund to N.S.W.
(5) Finally, the alleged Protectionist Government of
N.S.W., if this decision holds good, is a successful (and if
the decision doesn’t hold good, an unsuccessful) traitor
to its alleged principles, a turncoat, a Judas Iscariot, and
an objectionable character generally.

The Union Trustee Co. (Melb.) has made no
more profit than usual :

The co. continues to distribute more than it earns;
though, evidently excusing this unhealthy feature, the
chairman has explained that it has in hand a sub-
stantial sum of as yet unaccrued commission on estates
in process of realisation. As usual, the profils derived
from the trustee business arid the dividends earned
by investments are not distinguished. Appar-
ently the business profits are very small indeed;
the investments alone ought to be returning at
least £2700, reckoning the return from loans on first
mortgage (£21,752 of the co.'s total investments of
£67,655) at an average of 5 per cent. Practically, there-
fore, it may be said that the co. makes all its profit out of
the investment of its own capital, mainly in as solid State
securities as can be got. Resides its ordinary reserves,
now very small, there is an Investment Fluctuation
Account, which is now also very low. Possibly it
is a significant fact, possibly it is only a curious one, that
of all the co.’s stock securities the only one which isn’t
worth less now than it was 12 months ago is a parcel of
brewery debentures.
Strictly speaking, the Union Trustee Co.’s
reserves are less than shown above. Hitherto the practice
has been to take in at current market prices the Vic. and
Queensland stock and debentures, which are held in terms
of the Co.’s incorporating Act. This year, however, while
the other stock investments are thus dealt with, these
two obligatory investments are taken in at par. Twelve
months ago they were included as worth £33,084 ; now
their par value (£32,400) is set down, although VictoriaD
3 per cents, stood at the end of Feb. at about 97£, and
the Queensland debentures were also at a discount.
These probably would have been written down, too,
as far as they ought to go if the Fluctuation Reserve
would have stood that much shock ; but it wouldn’t. If
the securities recover, the co. will come out all right; if
they don’t, it will have to take something out of profits
to strengthen the reserve for the purpose.

Much surprise has been expressed, officially and
otherwise, at the small value placed upon Daniel Cooper s
estate in N.S.W. Will the Stamp Commissioner insist
upon an official valuation ? He has done so in many
minor cases, and has now an opportunity of showing how
valuations are made for probate.

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, of Melbourne, reports
that there has been a great improvement in the general
outlook of the engineering business since the new year
started, and that his pipe making works in Melbourne
and Perth are working full-handed. Amongst the orders
now being executed at Footscray works is one for 7 miles
10 chains of 18in. diameter light steel spiral-riveted pipes
for the Victorian water supply ; and at Perth works a
contract is in progress for over 6 miles of lOjin. diameter
spiral-riveted pipes, flanged joints, for the Greenbushes
Tin-Mining Syndicate. About two miles of these pipes
are to stand a test pressure of 8001 b. to the square inch. •

* « » *
The balance-sheet of the Great Southern Con-
sols G.M. Co. (Vic.) for the last half-year shows that the
underground electric haulage plant, which was supplied
and erected about seven months ago by the Austral Otis
Engineering Coy., South Melbourne, sole representatives
of Ganz and Co. Ltd., Budapest, Hungary, has effected a
saving of 60 per cent, in manual labor required to operate
the mine. This excellent result, and the great satisfac-
tion the plant has given, has influenced the directors of
the Berry Consols Extended Company in placing an order
for a similar plant with the same firm. The plant will
consist of a generating station, three underground loco-
motives, and a complete electric lighting installation.
This order furnishes further evidence of the high esteem
in which Ganz’s apparatus is held in Australia.*

The Commonwealth Govt, will shortly consider
the matter of silver coinage. At present we do the
unprofitable business of minting J. Bull’s gold for him
while he does the profitable business of minting our silver
for us. As we have been doing the unprofitable business
for a long while, it wouldn’t be more than a fair thing if
now he let us do the profitable business for him, as well
as for ourselves. Just now, however, it is only proposed
to issue enough of the new coins for Australian
requirements ; and that, though still profitable, would
not be so gaudy as it looks. For it does look
gaudy. Of standard silver lib. troy, or of fine silver
11.10 z., makes 66 shillings ; and the market value of that
much silver is now about 225. This shows a profit of 200
per cent., which is even better than running a good
brewery. The Australian demand is, however, limited to
an average of about £llO,OOO worth of silver coin a year
of which about £20,000 worth is to replace worn-out
coins. Assuming that the latter have got so thin by
being so frequently dropped into the collection-plate that
only a third of them remains, it would still leave a market
for £30,000 worth of silver a year. The other £BO,OOO
ought to do something beyond cover expenses. Most
likely, as the Australian Parliament’s committee last year
reported, there would be £30,000 profit

Shaker : It has been pretty conclusively
proved that, among all the politicians of the Common-
wealth, not one is available for the position of State
Treasurer who can make ends meet. What’s the matter
with giving a N.Q. fossicker a trial at the billet ? Living
in districts where prices are ruinously high, he (at least
90 per cent, of him) does not average more than 15s a
week, out of which he has to pay for his tucker, provide
tent, tools, horses, and gear, pay for a miner’s ri«-ht—
and now the poll-tax ! He not only does it, but manages
to get picturesquely drunk occasionally as well. A man
who can do that ought to be worth'a trial, where the
getting of a financial quart out of a monetary pint-pot is
the thing wanted....L.O.C. : Can’t print that these 23
people have committed perjury, &c., on your bare asser-
tion. Nevertheless cordially agree that there is safety
in the Savings Bank.... A.G. : The S. Aus. Farmers’ Co
appears to have made a loss of £2749 for the year and
not £1749, as stated in its report. The extra thousand
is represented by a swift and silent (so far as the report
is concerned) transfer from the reserve fund.

On the spur of the moment the writer wishes he had been

and co. had a bad time once, while a good many other

result of this is that the addition made to the reserve is
the smallest since 1899. The Daid-up capital is £5B 333
so profits represent 45J per cent.

Just because N.S.W. municipal law, so far a 3
the rating of mines is concerned, may mean any of 13
different things, it has always been a “ soft snap” for the
lawyers. After about 20 decisions had been given it was
thought, though, that the law had been made complete as
to coal-mines ; but the lawyers are safe yet—a new point
has now arisen as to the right basis for taxing a mine
whose workings are partly outside the claimant munici-
pality.

The Queensland National Bank has put up
the rate for Queensland deposts to 4 and 4J per cent, for
money fixed for one and two year periods respectively.
The usual rate offered for 12 months’ deposits in Aus-
tralia was.s per cent in 1892 ; 4J in 1893 (the bank failure
year); 4J in 1894 ; 3J in 1895 ; and a humble 3 per cent
since then.
• * * •
Burra wong orchard (Molong, N.S.W.), one of
the largest, if not the largest, in the State, has been
closed down, with its cannery, in consequence of the
unsuitable climate, heavy freightage to market, and the
ever-increasing number of fruit-tree pests. The orchard
covered fully 800 acres. Nearly £50,000 had been ex-
pended on this “great natural industry” up to the time
or its abandonment. If the same amount had been put
into an iron fourtdry it might have paid better. Cater-
pillars don't eat iron.
• * * *
About the best scheme ever tried in Australia
t/O save a hospital from the eternal necessity of showing 1
its Lazarus sores, in order to win crumbs enough to keep
life in its body, is crumbling away at the Broken Hill
mines. With the concurrence of the work-people the
employers agreed to make a very small deduction from
all wages paid (it was shown that £d. in the £ would do)
themselves subsidising the total £ for £, while in the
ordinary course the State subsidised the gross total £ for
£. The arrangement was a brilliant success, simply be-
cause for years the hospital and its committee didn’t have
to cadge ; it was a greater success still because the
battered miner did not have his wounds aggravated
by the thought that Charity was dressing them and
feeding him. Squeezed by the fall in the price of lead
and silver, the big mining cos., however, jumped at the
chance to cut down their contribution ; and now, as
•j 1 ' e J’ I don’t guarantee a regular subsidy, the committee
has had to appeal to the ill-paid men to make up the
shortage, and the greasy old hat is passing round in the
bad old way.

Cobar Great Western people were within an
ace of losing their valuable property. It was “ reported,”
bad no valid excuse to offer for non-working,
tne Mines Department could have done nothing other
than forfeit the leases. At this critical juncture “ Jack”
u-S. eon caine t* 3 the rescue with a tribute proposal,
which was eagerly accepted. The conditions make him
Poetically the owner of the mine for three years, but
that does not trouble the nominal owners, as it gives
them another chance to show their patience in waiting
for a five-figure buyer. Common sense and practical
considerations point to the advisableness of an amalga-
mation with the Occidental.
* • • •
The workmen employed in the mines at Oobar
(N.S.W.), have made a demand for an increase of 3s. per
day all round—men and boys alike. The owners have
to meet the workers in conference ; failing
this the Arbitration Court will be appealed to. The pro-
posed increase means advances ranging from 20 to 40 per
cent., averaging about 30 per cent., and in the case of the
Dig copper mine would represent nearly £40,000 a year.
• * • *
, Queen h* ee mine, about 12 miles out of Cobar
(N.S.W.), since September last has shipped 161 tons of
ore, which has returned £1173 net, or £7 ss. per ton.
The parcels have varied from 20 to 37 per cent, copper,
the average being 28 per cent. Lode, so far as proved, is
small. Show owned by a local syndicate.
* * * *
Myall’s United mine (N.S.W.), which, a few
months since, gave promise of becoming successful, has
again “turned dog” on the patient shareholders. The rich
stone met with in bottom level was merely a patch. On
the strength of it extensive additions and alterations were
made to the plant. Recent returns have been so poor
that crushing has been discontinued, and over 200 miners
have been thrown out of employment.

1 E.H.M.” writes : Re selling tin. A rough
and ready way I used when selling the oxide was to
deduct £l5 per ton from the market value of metallic
tin, and divide the result by 100; this would give the
value of the oxide per unit, tin oxide being sold by the
unit and not by the ton. Thus, with metallic tin at £l4O,
/tlf Pr* ce P er un it is about £1 55., and 70 per cent, oxide
(the best stream tin) would give £B7 per ton to the
scratcher. Bischoff charges £4 per ton to smelt your tin,
t n? u your own market and take the risk of
a fall in price. The percentage your inquirer mentions
is nonsense, as tin oxide can only contain 78-67 per cent,
of tin. The usual percentage is 70 percent., though I
have sold it on the East Coast of Tasmania up to 73 and
even 74 per cent.
• * * *
Plant’s Day Dawn Deep mines—a 2000 odd feet
prospecting show—on the fringe of Charters Towers,
after trying to float on a gorgeous paid-up share scale,
has secured five months exemption. Probable destination
of proposition is London.
* • * •
Settlement of long-standing deadlock between
Mills United and Day Dawn Freehold Mines, Charters
Towers, has at last been arranged. Mills United co. was
the owner of the notorious “Grid-Iron” lease which
embraced the streets running across the Day Dawn Free-
hold claim. It wouldn’t work the streets itself, wouldn’t
let the Freehold company work them nor drive through
them. Now it has had to surrender, though previously
it refused advantageous offers. Reason : a Way-Leaves
Bill passed in session of 1901. It didn’t altogether compel
Mills to surrender, but would have been amended to that
end. Hence the giving way.
• * * *
One Caldwell has been pulling the legs of the
Cloncurry people (N.Q.), or else he has been badly misre-
presented. He is supposed to have applied for 3000 acres
of copper-bearing country, and, according to the “inter-
viewer” the “Syndicate primarily interested in the
scheme is the Rhodesia Co., of South Africa, of which Mr.
Caldwell himself, is a partner, and which has acquired
world-wide fame in association with such names as the
late Cecil Rhodes, Wernher, Beit and Co., J. B. Robinson,
and others. The capital of the co. is £200,000,000.” This
co., in Caldwell’s statement, is interested in copper mines
in Germany, Spain, and America, the profits of which in
1897 were £13,000,000. “All these mines are raising ore
from about 4000 ft. The mines require no shafts—simply
quarrying.” Quarrying to 4000 ft. ! Why doesn’t Caldwell
go to Broken Hill and show mining men there howto do it?
Or, better/undertake to do ithimself! Success would mean
more millions to him than Cloncurry is likely to produce.
“ Montana mines, Lake Superior, between 1893-9 paid over
£11,000,000 in divs.” The shifting of Montana mines to
Lake Superior is even a more interesting performance
than quarrying to i' Oft. It was with quite a nonchalant
air that Caldwell declared “we propose to spend at least
£4,000,000 at Cloncurry if we can obtain a concession for
a railway from Winton—this would be apart from the cost
of the line.” He has a unique proposal to submit to the
Queensland Government. “We are ready to build the
line as a speculation if the Government will give us fair
interest on our money without any concessions at all.”
Could anything be more magnanimous? Give us fair in-
terest on the cost of building a railway for our own
especial use, and we will not ask for the whole of
Queensland as a concession. Caldwell further talked
glibly about a combination of “my company” with
the Amalgamated Copper Trust of America. The
two co.’s would have a capital of £400,000,000, and
would be in a position to control the copper markets of
the world. Should the amalgamation be effected “we
will close down all the lower grade mines. The Americans
are anxious to secure Australian shows; they have a
representative at present in N.S.W.—General'Mackie.”
“ Our co. can turn out 40 per cent, ore from the Cloncurry
district to supply the whole world.’’ Is it any wonder that
the local paper had to hold over its report of a Sunday-
school picnic and omit the impounding notices to make
room for this “ valuable matter.” Quotations given are a
fair sample culled from a page “ interview.” The question
which has to be decided is whether Caldwell is a consum-
mate ass or an accomplished humorist. Tub Bulletin
votes for the last.
• * * •
According to a recent count Australia has still
684,000 cattle left in its North Gulf country. Rocklands
(Q ), running 35,000 head, claims more than any other
station; close up, on the 30,000 mark, are Lorraine,
Kamilaroi, Oanobie, Brunette (N.T.), Fort Constantine,
and Dalgonally.
* * * *
The old order of mining changes fast. Kal-
goorlie, whose methods are more up to date than those
of any other Australian goldfield, has fewer of the old
stamp-heads at work than either Coolgardie or Mur-
chison. Once, and not many years ago, gold extraction
meant the battery and nothing else ; now Westralia has,
besides batteries, 163 other mills, 1158 leaching vats, and
136 filter presses.
• • • •
W.A. mines during March distributed divs. of
£88,250 ; Great Boulder with £43,750, Oroya-Brown Hill
£40,500. Princess Royal £4OOO, being responsible for the
lot. Total divs. for first quarter of the year, £253,487,
paid by nine co.’s.
* * * *
Hannan’s Main Reef (W.A.), after undergoing
three reconstructions, and wasting eight years’ toil and
£30,000 cash on its wild-cat property at Kalgoorlie, has
flitted to Niagara. The new property, purchased from
Ryan Bros., for £3500 cash and 20,000 shares, consisted
originally of 36 acres, but 12 acres more have since been
acquired by pegging. A Fraser and Chalmers 10-head
battery, with power for 20-head, has been secured in ex-
change for 20,000 shares, so vendors of battery apparently
have faith in the show, or in the market. The property
is traversed by a north and south reef, on which several
shafts have been sunk, the deepest being 150 ft.
At the 140 ft. level the reef has been opened
up for a distance of 400 ft., and averages 4ft. wide
for this length. Prom this drive 300 tons were
mined, and 119 tons, taken as it came, yielded 115 oz.
at the Government battery. Taking this crushing as a
criterion, the reef averages loz. per ton, and theco. looks
as if it had got hold of a good thing. So far the mine is
only equipped with a whip pole and windlasses, and has
no water supply, so that the need for a battery at the
present stage is not very apparent. A winze sunk from
the 140 ft. level proves the water zone to be at 170 ft. 00.
has only £BOOO cash in hand, after calling up all available
capital. This won’t go very far in providing the mine
with a main shaft, and full surface equipment. Chair-
man, however, cheerfully buoys up shareholders’ drooping
spirits by informing them that the reserves opened up
are worth £40,000, of which half will be profit.
For the sake of the long-suffering shareholders it is to be
hoped that this sanguineness will not get a rude shock.
In spite of the experience obtained, directors of gold-
mines seem unable to grasp the fact that mining without
ample working capital is doomed to failure, or, at best,
can only achieve a minimum of success, no matter how
good the mine may be. This property, together with the
Pine Lodge, recently mentioned here, was originally
owned by the Challenge Gold Estates Prop., and was
abandoned as worthless, in 1900, after surface scratching.
Both properties were then acquired by prospectors, and
certainly look good enough for anything, if properly
handled.
* * * *
The Kookynie (W.A.), a recent Ballarat flota-
tion, has commenced operations on a promising show of
24 acres situated at Kookynie. There is a north and
south reef on the property, which has been opened up to
water level by working miners. Co. is in 25,000 shares of
10s. each, of which 5000 fully paid up were given in full
payment for the property. This proves the bona tides of
the vendors, as the shares are worthless if the property
is. Mine is in charge of F. W. Benness, who was long in
control of that defunct wild cat, the Brown Hill Central.
Property was acquired on Mr. Benness’ recommendation,
and, as he has voluntarily accepted the management,
and must be heartily sick of wild cats, he is evidently
satisfied with the prospects.
* * * *
Great Boulder Perseverance (W.A.) continues
to open out very consistently, and both the Lake View
and Perseverance lodes maintain their width and value
down to the seventh level. Shaft is now on its way to
the 1100 ft., or eighth level. So far as one can ascertain
the present output can be maintained for a long time
to come. Manager Nichols appears to enjoy a sinecure
billet, as no sooner had he returned from a six months’
trip round the world than, after a casual glance at the
mine, he cleared off to London to attend the annual
meeting. As he has the best staff on the field the mine is
not prejudiced by his absence. The splitting of the £1
shares into eight of 2s. fid. each appears to have been
favorably received by the market, as the first quote of
the splits came through as 325. 6d., equivalent to £l3 on
the old stock. 00. now consists of 1,500,000 shares of
2s. 6d. each nominal, of which 100,000 are held in reserve
for future contingencies. This places co. in a very strong
financial position, and also makes the shares much more
negotiable. Oo.’s next div. of Is. 3d. per new share is due
about April 10. The old heap of oxidised tailings is fast
disappearing and will shortly be exhausted. The tailings
from the sulphide plant are practically worthless, and are
being conveyed direct, overhead by electric tramway, to
the Great Boulder South lease.
* * • •
Parker’s Range, Southern Cross (W.A.), which
was hurriedly deserted on the announcement of the
discovery of Coolgardie, is again attracting the attention
of prospectors and speculators. The deepest point
worked in the old days was only 100 ft., yet about
100,000 oz. of gold were won. A large number of gold-
bearing lodes, reefs and quartz leaders outcrop over a
distance of 14 miles. A large block of leases has recently
been pegged, among others 72 acres on account of Frank
Gardner. As mining facilities have been greatly im-
proved since the mines were last worked, there seems
little doubt that the field will shortly add materially to
the State’s gold output. Fifteen head of stamps are at
work, and more are on order. Recent crushings have
ranged from |oz. to 3oz. per ton for fairly large parcels.
Government is so satisfied with the outlook that it is
making the old dam, capable of holding B,ooo,ooogals. f
watertight, and this will ensure an ample supply of water
for the field.
* * * *
Hainault, Kalgoorlie (W.A.) is now running 20
head of stamps, and, in future, the monthly output will
be increased to about 2000 tons of lOdwt. ore. With the
10-head mill running, the total local costs have averaged
265. a ton, and with the increased output it is anticipated
that the same figure will cover all costs both locally and
in London. This will leave a profit of about 10s. per ton,
or, say, £lOOO monthly, or sufficient to pay 12 per cent,
on the issued capital, which should raise the shares to
par. Shareholders have had a long and weary wait of
eight years, but the end of the long lane appears to be at
last in sight. Work is now in progress in all the seven
levels opened up, even in the three upper levels, on which
the mine was formerly classified as a wild-cat.
• * * •
From the time the first of them began to talk,
down to the end of Feb. the Westralian State batteries
have saved 141,2400 z. gold (worth £526,284) from 113,200
tons; and the three cyanide plants, lately started, have
added £32,542 to this output. The whole 14 works now
in operation showed a nominal profit of £350 for March.
* * • *
State mining-engineer Montgomery’s report on
the remote Phillips River (W.A.) field is available. It
doesn’t glow. “ There are only four mines that have yet
passed the preliminary prospecting stage, and in three of
these there is an urgent want of further development to
put them in a position to maintain their output.” Most
of the properties, though “ very likely and promising
prospects,” are still “ speculative ventures” ; and as the
public will not come in on these terms, all that can be
done is to help the prospectors to cheaply realise on the
ore which they can raise with their own primitive appli-
ances while they are further opening up their shows. If,
however, the present excessive costs cannot somehow be
reduced, all that will be possible will be to pick out the
eyes of the field and then abandon it.
* *
Fraser’s South Extended, Southern Cross
(W. A.), is, after a dreary wait of seven years, at last being
properly handled. Joseph Bridge, vendor of the Windsor
Consols, who was badly left with his vendor’s shares (they
were intentionally rendered unnegotiable on ’Change,
because no application for a quote was made) is now
consulting-engineer to the co. Bridge has advocated a
vigorous policy of development, and proposes to double the
battery. The pay chute has already been opened up for
a length of fully 200 ft., and averages quite half-an-ounce.
In the past mere surface scratchings and pig-rootings were
indulged in, and the mine is practically a virgin property
in the sulphide zone. If properly handled, co. should be
a steady producer and div.-payer on a moderate scale-
say of about 10 per cent.
* * * *
Queen Margaret (W.A.) has tired of its more or
less fruitless efforts in that State. The Bulong property
is looking so poor that all hands have been retrenched,
and the mine is now a scene of desolation. The South
Gippsland property has totally belied expectations, and
the option which expires on the 11th instant will probably
not be exercised.
» * * *
London papers have been printing columns
about “ the banket reefs of Westralia,” all owned, ifr
seems, by Morgan’s British Exploration of Australia, Ltd.,
the modest J. Bull concern with a share capital of half-a-
million. The banket reefs are, the reports say, 70 miles
south-east of Marble Bar—nine of them, parallel, and
from 2ft. to 7ft. thick. Only one, the office gives out,
has been tested to any extent; it' runs 9dwt. Half-a-
million tons of this grade stone have been exposed, which
can be made to. pay at sdwt. To explain why it has
made so little stir it is said that there have been water
difficulties, but it is added that these have now been got
over.

* * * *
Geologist Brown’s report on Weinnecke’s Re-
ward mine, Artlunga, confirms the condemnation by
Paul Northey and Captain Pleitner. Mr. Brown says
that the reefs are barren, and what gold there is is con-
tained in ironstone veins. These are showing in decom-
posed gneiss, and he expresses a doubt as to their
“ living ”in the unaltered rock. What has become of
Chewings’ mountain, where the gold was so thick that
there was an insufficiency of mullock to cover it? This
fiasco is about the nastiest knock that Adelaide has ever
had.
• • • •
A strangely casual mining venture is now being
carried on in the vicinity of Drysdale (Vic.), where, some
years ago, a family of spiritualists, in consequence of a
vision by one of its members, saw gold in a field on the
sea shore. The father, two sons, and a son-in-law have
since been mining in the intervals of onion-growing and
farming, and have now reached a great depth in blue sea
sand without success. But the spirits still urge them to
persevere, and they continue to dig with an enthusiasm
worthy of better direction.
• • • *
The Broken Hill Proprietary mine appears to
have gone back to the three-shift system as a regular thing.
It is, whichever way you look at it, a change for the worse.
The night shift (midnight till 8 a.m.) is, and probably
always will be, an abomination. A stuffy stope or drive,
in which work goes on day and night, never has a chance
to get really free from powder-fumes ; and when, on top
of that, a man has to worX by night and attempt to sleep
in a tin humpy by day, a Barrier summer has for him
most of the terrors of Hades.
* • • •
There is joy in North Queensland because the
revolutionary row at Monte Video has been settled.
Monte Video, the gate of communication between the
Transpacific co’s dredge and the outer world, is open
again. Latest clean-ups are 105 oz. for six days’ work
and 550 z. for four days. If any belligerent Don Miguel
del Antimacassar bags that gold on its way to the bank,
there will be a record outburst of profanity from the
Far Far North, which, in spite of Leonta, can still swear.
“ Wanderer ” writes :
I have struck Natal. There are restrictions on the
entry of “free Indians” here, but thousands come in
under indentures to work for five years, after which they
are “ free.” The result is that in the country towns the
small tradesmen, formerly Europeans, have been dis-
placed by Arabs, and even in the cities of Durban and
Maritzburg the same process is going on. The land, too,
is passing so rapidly into Asiatic hands that it is esti-
mated that within ten years they will own two-thirds of
the colony. In Durban, a few weeks ago, an important
sale of city lots was held, and Asiatics bought everything,
bidding up to £60,000 in one instance. When in Durban
I saw an eviction of a white family by the Asiatic land-
lord, who owned a large number of villas. It is generally
recognised that there is now no hope of saving Natal
from passing into '"the hands of the Asiatics. Kruger
would have_ wholly saved the Transvaal from the coolies
by prohibition, but the British Government demanded
their admission as British subjects. Still Kruger limited
the curse by not allowing Asiatics to trade or live outside
locations at Johannesburg, but now they have the run of
the whole country, and the small tradesmen are praying :
Save us from the fate of Natal.” Any Australian poli-
tician who attempts to open the door to the Asiatic
should be sent to Natal for a month or hanged. The
Asiatics in the Commonwealth should be given eight
months’ notice to quit, as was done in the Orange Free
State.

“P.X.”: At Newcastle (N.S.W.), the
other day, six people guessed within a pound of
the prize bullock’s weight—79llb. Sometimes,
no doubt, these competitions are a fraud, and
sometimes mere luck guides the winner. But it
can’t always be like that, for it’s some men’s
bread-and-butter to be able to tell to a thin steak
or a chop how much a live beast will pan out.
Same months ago the head of a firm, which
handles the meat for at least 10,000 people, died
suddenly, and it fell to another man to carry on
the concern—another man who knew nothing of
buying, wherein lies the secret, or most of the
secret, of successful butchering. Day by day,
therefore, he has run his eye over every beast m
the yard, “ guessed ” its weight and noted it, and
later on compared the guess with the actual cold-
meat result. After over four months of this he
is now rarely more than a sausage or so out, and
he has almost got confidence enough in his judg-
ment to buy.
A lady named Smith wrote to the Mel
bourne Age expressing her gratitude for its
recent article on the Barmaid question, which
represented the average Hebe as a decoy who
lures young men into drunkenness and “fre-
quently goes down with her prey.” Also the
Smith lady stated that there are more female
licensees than men in the Melbourne district, and
she suggested that the police allow undue latitude
to the Sex. Evidently the lady named Smith has
given a lot of earnest thought to the ‘ ‘ decoy evil ”
from the point of view of an outsider knowing
only a little about it. She has yet to learn that
the majority of Hebe’s regular visitors are
married men suffering from a surfeit of the female
society they get at home, which is to say they are
not very young. The average hopeful young man
is decoyed away from the bar and preserved from
whisky dangers by some sweet illusion or other,
and it is not until his illusion has married him
that he takes to drink, if he ever takes to it at all.
The number of men dragged down by barmaids is
a mere trifle compared with the number of women
in poorer districts who become regular customers
at the pub because there is another woman behind
the bar to keep them in countenance. We hear
too much about the Hebe who attracts men and
incites them to take an extra lemon-squash. Let
us now deplore the fact that she practically en-
courages her own sex to come and get partially
drunk on the premises.

A love story, as told by the male criminal :
I “ mashed ” her at a moonlight concert on the Mel-
bourne Cricket Ground, and found that we had both come
from the same suburb. That was a coincidence, and
made conversation easy. She was rich and lived with
her widowed mother, she said. I was wealthy, and
manager of a bank, I said. We often met after, and once
my watch was “ spouted ” to provide a night at the
theatre. I was never allowed to see her right home,
always left her at the street corner. Mother might be
waiting at the front, she said. Some nights she couldn’t
come out. Mother didn’t like her going about much, she
said. Some nights I couldn’t come out. Had to stay
late at the bank, I said. One day my boss said, “ Weigh
out two cwt. of coal and take it to Mrs. Smith’s—the
second double-fronted house on the right, in Charles-st.”
I did. Was told at the house that the servant would
show me where to put it. The servant came out. It was
She 1 We never spoke then, nor since.
Thursday Island has just had its annual
count of heads, and the mixture is found to be
rather worse than usual. Of 1515 people, 736 are
Europeans ; the rest, running down the scale, are
Japanese, Chinese, Filippinos, Malays, Polynesians
and Cingalese. There are also 105 who are nothing
in particular; this class is increasing more rapidly
than any other. When a young man’s four grand-
parents are respectively Malay, Filipino, Anglo-
Chow and the son of a half-bred Kanaka girl by
marriage with a Dago seaman from Greece, and
the young man himself is mashing a mulatto girl
whose father was Portuguese, the despairing
census official puts him down as “unspecified.”

The collapse of the Jingo madness in
Maoriland is well illustrated by the tameness
which characterises the attempt to boom the
newly-formed guild to look after the graves of the
Maorilanders who died in South Africa, and keep
the weeds and the goats off them. The average
citizen is woefully heedless about the whole busi-
ness, and it bids fair to be left solely in the hands
D f a few nice kind ladies and a bishop or two,
with a stodgy city councillor thrown in here and
there.

There is a wide-
spread and strongly-sup-
norted movement among
•• Anglicans ” in Aus-
tralia to abolish the title of “ Church of England ”
and substitute “Episcopal Church of Australia,”
or some such name. A “broad ’’ episcopal church
with a broad name, instead of the present narrow
and ridiculously local one, would probably attract
many adherents. “The Church of England’’ is
an absurd ecclesiastical title—especially in these
days when it is mostly run by Scotchmen.
* * • *
The course of true love in Australia em-
bodies, among others, three notable possibilities.
First, that the young man will shoot the girl
before marriage ; second, that he will marry her
and leave her at the church door, after which she
will take in washing for three years while quali-
fying for a divorce ; third, that he will marry her
and take her to live in a back street, where, in
after years, he will slay her with an axe in a moment
of excitement arising through inability to pay the
rent. Australia has seen several examples of
tragedies I. and 111. within the last few weeks
(tragedy 11. is always with us). The young man
Dillon, who started to thin out Mary Jones
and her family at Miller’s Point, Sydney,
last week, was an ordinary sample of
tragedy I. According to his own statement,
he heard that Mary Jones was going to
meet another man, and thereafter the trouble
commenced. The Australian young man may
not take marriage seriously, but he takes love
very seriously, and even a rumor of the possible
defection of Mary Jones is apt to lead to hysteria
and all manner of woe. As a rule he buys a
cheap revolver at the ironmonger’s, and it won’t
go off when he points it at his true love, so he hits
Mary Jones on the head with the butt end, and
then the erratic weapon recovers its senses just in
time to enable him to fire promiscuously at the
assembling public, Mary having got away by this
time, screaming, but not much damaged. Then
in most cases he misses the public, and the police
gather him in. This isn’t always the way of
things—sometimes he hits the public by accident;
but in a general way the cheap young man with
the cheap gun makes far more row than real
damage.
One of Melb. Argus correspondents, in
the discussion on population and preventives,
crowded so much hard truth into his letter that it
was a wonder the paper published it. Said he :
I have a family of nine, and an income of £2 10s. a
week, and I tell you candidly that if I could live my time
over again I would not get married at all. What pleasure
has my wife or I? We have nothing but the bare neces-
saries of life, and our clothing is scant. . . . My neighbor,
Blank, is a laborer, and I am a carpenter in full work.
Yet he and his wife have more comforts than me—be has
only one little girl. ... lam not saving a penny. The
country will have to keep me when I am no longer able
to work, and so it should, for the country alone will reap
the benefits of my large family.

The military trouble in S.A. arises from
the fact that when the Crow-eater contingents
were at the Federal shivoos in Sydney and Mel-
bourne in 1901, they learned that the militia in
those cities received more money than they did
for the same work, parade and frill. They have
been brooding over the twopence or so ever since,
and until the pay is levelled up, it is not likely
that amateur lighting men will be plentiful in
S.A. again. The Australian Parliament hasn’t
had time to regulate military matters yet, and
the old Crow-eater pay was low simply because
the Crow-eater land is poor. It was hoped that
patriotism would more than counterbalance the
twopence; but it didn’t.
• * • *
This is the story of a “ lady-help ’’ who
had been in a Christian family :
My employer was a clergyman. His family was large
and my wages were low. It was a case of working all day
and half the night and putting up with the poorest of
food. Still I was better off than the actual “servant."
She was a small scrap of humanity who, for various
reasons, was glad to be paid tenpence per week and pro-
vided with food and clothing of a sort, in return for 16
hours’ work a day. But we were expected to find
consolation in the extreme piety which permeated
the household, from its clerical head and his wife
down to the youngest of the numerous children.
Being only human, I occasionally lost my temper,
under the treble trial of too much work, too much snuffle
and too little food. On such occasions I was not
threatened with dismissal, but the family was solemnly
gathered together and I was prayed for. The clergyman
would beseech the Lord to look down upon my manifold
imperfections, to forget that they continued in spite of
the blessed comfort I enjoyed of association with a truly
Christian family, and to pardon me and to grant me
grace to more gratefully recognise the great kindness of
my mistress. Then they would sing a hymn, “ A little
talk with Jesus makes it right again,” and I was ex-
pected to go back to my work with a chastened spirit
and renewed strength. When I “ gave notice,” the
family prayed most earnestly for a change of my heart.
But the efficacy of prayer was not proved, for I left. I’m
now with godless people ; but there is generally roast
meat for dinner, and pudding, and the work is light.

I KISSED THE COOK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I kissed the cook —ah me, she was divine !
Cheeks peachy, dark brown eyes, lips red as wine ;
Long apron, with a how,
A cap as white as snow—
By far too tempting, so I kissed the cook.
I kissed the cook—poor helpless little lass ;
The chance was good, I could not let it pass.
Her hands were in the dough ;
She dare not spoil, you know,
My Sunday suit, and so I kissed the cook.
I kissed the cook, with lingering kiss and long,
And even now I hardly think ’twas wrong,
For, just ’tween you and me,
The cook’s my wife, is she,
So I’d a right, you see, to kiss the cook.
BRISBANB.
E. WEST.
MIDSUMMER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The torches of the bee-balm light,
The gullies dense with briar ;
The yellow primrose streaks the night
With its illusive fire ;
But, ah ! the rose of queenly head
Lies drooped and dead!
The beds within the garden gleam
With red nasturtium gold ;
The poppy-cups invite to dream
The rainbow dreams of old ;
Yet, sweet, there is a shadowy bliss
We two do miss 1
Still, tho’ our hearts may not forget
How suns once sang and shone,
Let us have done with vain regret,
And still we’ll journey on,
Treading the harvest-colored land
Hand clasped in hand !
MAGGIE SULLIYAN.

The despised women of Victoria are help-
lessly frantic about the eighth rebuff which the
House of Rich Landlords has given to their
suffrage claims. It was seriously proposed that
they should demonstrate in the gallery of the
House upon the report on the bi cameral confer-
ence , but they decided not to lower themselves
to the level of the old women inside the bar. The
latest idea is to copy some dissenting sects in
England and refuse to pay taxes ordered by a body
in which they have no representation. Yet there
is a far better remedy than that, and a more
accessible one. A really strong Union of Female
Workers could do the whole business, and the
homely essential Biddy could wipe the floor with
the House of Landlords and all its gauds. The
Union could withdraw all the domestics from the
houseof every M.L.C. who voted against Woman’s
Suffrage and keep them out till the Bill was passed.
It could withdraw alldomestichelpfrom every hotei
or boarding-house that took him in ; call out the
cook and other assistance from the house that
entertained him ; order the waitress not to see
him at the restaurant table ; get the affiliated
laundress to make his shirts limp, and the
affiliated barmaid to look the other way when lie
wanted a drink, and the charwoman to leave his
palatial home unswept and unwashed. Next
session the ukase should go forth that Woman’s
Suffrage and One-Woman-One-Vote must become
law before 1 p.m. on a certain day. And on that
day, at that hour exactly, if the measure wasn’t
passed, the M.L.C. ’s waiting-maid would drop the
lunch upside down on the carpet, his cook would
capsize the pot on the floor, his nurse-girl would
hand in his infant at the police-station as a lost
child, his parlor-maid would spill the valuable
statuette she was dusting, and female aid and
comfort would leave him in one act.

The movement started 12 months ago,
under enormously influential patronage, to raise a
fund with which to found 10 girls’ scholarships to
be allotted at Sydney University annual matricu-
lation examination—the scholarships being in-
tended to perpetuate and keep green the memory
of the late Queen Victoria—seems to have shared
the fate of other projects started with that object.
Judged on a cash basis the interest taken in the
departed monarch is a very small quantity—almost
**■ vanished quantity.

Tumufc (N.S.W.) has been illustrating how
hard a cow can kick when it likes. There was at
ttie start of the performance just the ordinary row
over the ownership of the beast, just the ordinary
friendly-neighbor s prosecution for cattle-stealing
just the ordinary swearing that it was Brown’s
cow all right, and the ordinary swearing that it
was Smith’s. The tired P.M., probably having
no chance to toss for it, passed the trouble on to a
jury ; and after thinking over it for a long while
the jury said it didn’t know, and acquitted its
neighbor as in the day of evil it hoped to be
acquitted itself. Then Brown, who had been the
accused in the first case, sued Smith to get
damages for illegal detention of cow and loss of
character ; and the whole trouble was raked up
again. (Meantime the cow, becoming lonely in
the police-yard, Providence had sent it a calf.)
Pugh teen witnesses again swore that it was
Brown s cow, and 17 that it was Smith’s ; and the
jury thought that the swearing was equal enough
to justify it in disagreeing some more. Brown’s
cow and Smith’s cow factions were beginning to
form themselves, when, something after midnight,
Brown and Smith simultaneously had visions of
the bills that the cow was costing ; so they led
one another outside and shook hands, and
reckoned it up that they were already over £4OO
out of pocket, and sacked their lawyers, and
agreed to give the cow to the local hospital. And
now Ihe Bulletin is waiting to hear that the
cow has pleuro or something, and that it has cost
the hospital 7s. fid. to have the carcase carted out
to the back of the township and burned.
All _4 he trouble over that Tumut cow, and
the difficulty of getting a jury to convict in
c t ases of disputed cow-identification, recalls some-
thing that happened in a town away out in the
dust at a time when cattle changed hands oftener
than receipts. There had been six or eight cases
at the sessions, and all looked pretty clear—any-
way to the Judge, who, failing to get a convic-
tion, made each charge to the jury stronger than
the one before. Last came a youngster ; and the
J udge changed his manner, and nearly melted.
I lie last thing the jurymen heard as they filed out
was ; ‘ And so young too ; poor lad, poor lad ! ”
Here s our chance,’ the foreman whispered.
If we find the young ’un guilty, the old chap’ll
only give him about six months, and we’ll win
back the district’s reputation.” So thev filed in.
‘‘Guilty.” ’Sonah was lying in wait for it!
Seven years,” he snapped as soon as he decently
could. Then the jury looked along its beard, and
felt that it had fallen in.

Sydney’s very human and likeable parson,
W. Carr Smith, is in trouble with some of the
Sabbatarians for making this remark—
• lf L he were a young layman he would spend his Sundays
in the following manner : Stay in bed an hour later than
usual; attend a celebration of the Eucharist at 9 ; break-
fast about 10 : then loaf ; read or write letters till’lunch •
after dinner take recreation, perhaps boating, cycling or
pay a few calls j after that attend a bright evensong
where there would be a short sermon if possible • thus
beginning and ending the day worshipping God.
Yet surely that is a sensible, a reverent, and a
godly Sunday for the young Christian. But there
still survives in Sydney—by some curious stay of
the processes of mental evolution—a weep-and-
howl section of Christians, whose God is a God of
gloom and terror only to be worshipped in fear
and trembling in a purgatorial tin conventicle, to
Whom fresh air and rational recreation and a clear
wholesome body are things accursed. It is possible
to understand Calvin in a dark Swiss valley in an
age when sanitation was unknown. But Calvinism
in bright, sunny Australia is curiously out of
place. Perhaps it will fade away when the sewer-
age systems have been perfected and food-adul-
teration stopped.
* * * .
An Australian Chief Justice on matrimonial
matters (duringa recent breach of promise ease):
As far as the law is concerned a man must take the
amount of affection that a woman can give He may
look for the warmth of Sapho and find the chill kiss of
Diana, but he cannot complain afterwards. The woman
says. Such as I was you sought me j such as I was you
were willing to marry me.” We deal with a contract of
marriage as we deal with a contract for the sale of
potatoes or anything else. (Laughter.) A woman tells a
man she does not love him, but he says, “I will make
you.” He knows the thing he is bargaining for, and that
is the thing he has to take.
Dr. Gresswell lately astonished his fellow
members of Melb. Board of Health by sticking a
piece of common piping into the lovely Corpora-
tion tip at West Melbourne and holding a lighted
match at the other end, when he got a steady
flame seven inches long—natural gas. Then he
decanted some of the effluvium off into cylinders
and therewith. kept four lamps burning for a
while, clearly illuminating the darkened board-
room. This is the sort of atmosphere the surround-
ing citizens breathe, and the responsible city
councillors are to be invited to excuse themselves.
But they are more likely to lease their beautiful
tip as a “concession” to some lighting shindykit
and then start to make another.
* • * •
Marie Corelli, writing of the domestic
servant difficulty in London, says that good
servants are plentiful, but most of them won’t
take situations on account of the characters of
their would-be mistresses, who are “unblushingly
indecent and unspeakably vulgar.” “I could
name dozens of cases,” she declares, “ where girls,
on inquiry, have actually declined lucrative
situations and contented themselves with work at
lower wages rather than be known as ‘in service ’
with certain distinguished ladies.” Mary Jane,
in short, is afraid of losing her good character by
mixing with London’s Very Best People, and
judging by recent divorce proceedings she has
good reason to be.
• • * *
Melb. Age, which has begun to worry up
the barmaid question, affords evidences of the
fact that it has gone no further than the gospel-
cwm-temperance organisations for its information.
Different from the case of a Sydney daily when
agitating for the abolition of the Hebe. It sent
out two of its best and most worldly-wise writers
to personally investigate the mysteries of the pri-
vate bar, gave them weeks to go round, and pro-
vided them with much money for expenses.
One of those pressmen (the other is dead) still
alludes to the job as the one great compensation
for years of arduous and joyless newspaper experi-
ence, and their articles certainly had a great deal
more weight, and more hiccup, than a whole
mountain of impersonal “statistics” and other
information prepared by pallid young men who
could never trust themselves within cooee of a
pretty barmaid without feeling tempted to “fall.”

Canowindra Star (N.S.W.) reports :
A team of 20 Chinese are at present engaged suckering
8000 acres on the Glen Gariff estate between Cargo and
Orange ; 3s. per acre is the price given ; estimated by
Europeans to be a splendidly paying one. But the
Europeans were not given a chance to compete for the
work.
It is a mystery why some employers prefer Chinese
labor when it is not a question of economy, unless
it is that they enjoy the servility or the smell of
the alien. Very possibly it is the last; some
people have a taste for high game and lofty
oysters; and a depraved liking for a Chinese flavor
about a place would go quite naturally with that
weakness.

Melbourne efforts to run a “ simultaneous
mission,” without Yankee showmen Torrey and
Geil, were a dreary frost. “ Bleeds that Won the
Empire ” Fitchett therefore waxes bitter in his
religious weekly :
The missioners, it might almost be said, have to take
their own audience of Christian sympathisers with them
—the Yarra bank crowd passes them by. A semi-lunatic
orator on Home Rule, and Mr. Fleming’s lurid and equally
lunatic discourses on anarchy [mark the “Christian”
tone] gather large audiences.
Rather rashly Fitchett prints two photos, showing
the small “ Christian ” gathering and Fleming’s
crowd of some hundreds. Perhaps, after all, the
“ Lurid Lunatic ” is more in sympathy with the
fishers of Galilee than the superior Fitchett and
Co.

Brutal remark by “Nym.” :
Clean necks are the order of the day since the new
style of hair-gear, which consists of brushing the hair to
a bob on the crown of the head, became fashionable in
those circles where young women gather.
This is the sort of society item in which
N.Q. papers revel. The Mackay Mercury records
the marriage of
Hannah Mickey, daughter of Jimmy Mickey, formerly
of Gala (S. Sea Islands) with Mr. Henry Mie, also of S. 8.
parentage but Queensland native. . . . The bride was
given away by her father and was attended by two little
Kanaka maidens>as bridesmaids. .. . The bride’s costume
consisted of white merveilleux, trimmed with torchon
lace, bridal veil and orange blossoms. She carried a
handsome bouquet. . . . The bridal couple left amid
showers of rice for Te Kowai.
If it were not for Kanaka weddings, Chinese
gambling-den raids and Polynesian outrages, the
Northern Press would have little left to write
about.
* * * «
A feature in the Melbourne landscape
which suggests the first faint breath of Boom is
the number of red-tiled, brick villas that have
sprung up of late. These are mostly planked
down in patches, a dozen or so in a patch, each
in its half-acre of ground. They are all speculator-
built, and run from A4OO to each ; and
they sell well, though the business is already over-
done. Also, as they face all ways and no two are
exactly alike, they are a pleasing variant on the
ghastly terraces of brick or weatherboard houses,
with iron roofs and chimneys like rows of pins,
and handkerchief “gardens’’ with amende
creepers stuck therein.
* * * *
The habit in Australia of a women being
a lady—always a lady and never anything but a
lady—reached its climax, surely, the other day
when that loud and untamed King Billy paper,
the Protestant Standard, printed this :
_ Long years ago Russia cut off Rome, and now Spain,
trance, Austria, Portugal, indeed every nation of the
earth over which Rome held sway are busily engaged ex-
pelling the Scarlet Lady.
The Scarlet Lady! We had hoped that, no
matter who else became a perfect lady, at any rate
the Scarlet Woman was safe. But no; though
all the unpleasant adjectives in the dictionary may
be applied to that symbolic personage, site is
nevertheless a lady, a real lady.
