Measles having broken out at Fiji, the
Wesleyan deputation that was to have followed
on the tracks of Cardinal Moran to the coral
strand has been warned not to come. And pos-
sibly for the same reason the Cardinal will aban-
don his visit. Then all will be peace and measles.
Measles are much better visitors than a Wesleyan
deputation and a Cardinal.
* * • *
The habit of being found dead seems to be
getting even more common than usual nowadays,
hvery morning the daily papers have two or
three paragraphs detailing how a couple of lovers
strolled into a quiet spot and were just about to
commence rubbing faces when Angelina drew
attention to a gentleman lying down. Generally
the gentleman was dead. Sometimes a man just
wanders in, casual like, to an empty house and
protests against the evil influences of the proper-
tied class by dying on the premises and making
the place hard to let. Nothing makes a place so
hard to let as the fact that a stranger cut his
throat on the premises.
AUTUMN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I made a grave where leaves lay sere.
All Love ! All Hope lie buried here 1
(It was the waning of the year.)
All Hope. All Love. Slow, slow the sun
Sank to his setting. One by one
The small birds hushed, the day was done.
My tears fell fast upon the ground,
Where Love and Hope lay sleeping sound ;
Kind leaves crept down and hid the mound.
The Darkness came and brooded low ;
Across the grave, with wailing woe,
A moaning breeze didst come and go.
I made a grave where leaves lay sere~
All, all my heart lies buried here.
(It was the waning of the year.)
DOROTHY MO.
THE LOVERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ambition thus to Love :
“ Thou wert my queen—
My own—till Future’s glamor fell between.
Thy love to prove ;
Give back my vow ;
Too mean art thou to reign beside me now."
Ambition thus to Love,
When years had rolled :
“ Vain, foolish were my vision hopes of old.
O Queen above,
Come I too late ?
Forgive—be happy with thy meaner mate."
M.L.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, April 7, 1903.
My Dear Arini.—
Resurrection season always brings a budding of
the dry twigs of society. Gov. -General and Lady
Tennyson’s cards are out for a dance at the Mac-
quarie-street palace, and those cards make a
“light blue lane of early dawn” (Tennyson) out
bf the long hot summer night of dullness. May be
a lane on which few can skip, but it will give some
shops some trade, and some cabmen a few fares.
Besides; it leads into the Agricultural Show
Ground, arid runs out to the A.J.C. races, and
winds up tb the Town Hall portals and balls.
The Tennysbns started to grind the social mill
last week by attending a naval and military tattoo
at Victoria Barracks in aid of the drought fund.
Some seven thousand silent, unapplausive persons
sat in circular darkness and listened to bands,
Single and massed, and gazed on the scarlet array
on the green of the barrack cricket-ground. The
lobster heap was lighted by tongues of orange
flame from torches, a cluster of acetylene gas
lamps shed their intolerable radiance over it, and
a half-faced moon looked from overhead, and, if
the men in that satellite found the show more
amusing than we did, they must be easily pleased.
Concerning colors : Did you note the afterglow
of last Sunday’s sunset ? The unclouded west was
one mighty blush of dusky flame, as if the Great
White Throne itself was on fire, and out of the
dull rose blazed the planet of love, that beautiful
hussy, Venus, flashing diamond rays. It must
have inspired our sunset poet, especially if he saw
it through the great grave gums, to do another
Fantasy.
But to return to high society: the Tennysons
went to St. Ignatius’ regatta and watched “the
measured pulse of racing oars” (I’ll let the Gov.-
General see that there’s one copy of Papa’s poems
in Australia) and looked over the college, which
commands such an unsurpassed view of Hunter’s
Hill. The river swarmed with small craft, and
every R.C. of note or notelessness was there. The
Gov.-General was dressed as the skipper of the
launch Premier in very tight uniform. Lady T.,
in a holland frock and blue sailor hat, brought her
three sons—such jolly little fellows, in white
raiment.
Liedertafel held its 122nd concert last week,
under the baton of its new conductor, Arundel
Orchard, a young man with a cleft in his chin,
deep-socketed eyes, pince-nez, and an I-shall-be-
obeyed manner of waving his stick. He is a
quiet conductor ; his gestures don’t suggest that
he is standing on an ant-hill, and that some of the
owners of the soil are in his coat-sleeves. Amy
Murphy, a young Maorilander, made her first
local appearance as a singer, and as both appear-
ance and voice are fresh and cultivated, the
audience intimated by its hearty applause and
encores that it will be glad to see her (and her
greenstone pendant) when occasion offers.
Hope Mayne, who left Sydney some nine
months ago to “try” London, has secured an
engagement to go to Africa with Mr. Sass, for-
merly of the Brough 00. Mr. and Mrs. Thomas,
who used to be with the Arnold co., also join the
new combine. Miss Mayne will play Olivia in
“Sweet Nell,” Lucy in “Lady Huntworth’s
Experiment,” and the r6le Mrs. Brough had in
“ The Manoeuvres of Jane.” Inez Bensusan has
deserted the inkpots and ice of New York, and
when last heard from was at Monte Carlo with
her brother Edgar. In his “ Impressions ” given
to a local paper, “Willie” Beattie says he was
told by the Harmsworths (when he submitted
some articles) that “four Australians had over-
stocked the market. ” Four Australians had over-
stocked London with its five millions, and its
annexes with their 25 millions !
Mark Hambourg is on his way to Australia
from the United States, where the serious critics
were unanimous in the opinion that his place in
the musical arena is on the shoulders of most
other pianists* The serio-comic criticisms make
the best reading for persons who are not walking
symphonies or concertos. “He tore up and down
the keyboard and grabbed great chunks of
melody,” says one authority, “all the while
bobbing up and down on the piano-stool like an
animated mop.” “ Hambourg’s mannerisms con-
sist of his walk and his hair. He is the first piano
soloist ever heard in this city who did not
insist on moving the piano other-end-round after
the stage hands had worked on it for 15 minutes,
but that little omission of genius may be forgiven
him. Small, and almost frail, with stooping
shoulders, he comes upon the stage with a swing
and a rush that remind one of a belated office-boy
hurrying workward at the last moment, with a
fire-me-if-you-can-but -1 - hurried -at - the - last ex-
pression that is very convincing.”
“Ferresford Waltz,” by Adeline Parry, is on
the market, and ready for light fantastic toes and
any other kind of toe that feels inclined to invest.
Its composer is the wife of the heavy-weight
young gentleman who acts as hon. sec. to the
Liedertafel, and no doubt State Premier See, to
whom the composition is dedicated, will find it
one of those irresistible waltzes that first catch
the ear and then curl round the heart, till, on a
sudden, they pervade the legs and make them
gyrate recklessly on a slippery surface.
A Scotch thistle in holy orders (Campbell is his
name) has been saying in London that the late
Hector Macdonald “was very sensitive and that
his unpopularity in Ceylon was due to the fact
that he did not give parties.” It sounds weak.
Fighting Mac with his strong barking voice as a
party-giver, and doing Dolly-dialogues on the
lawn with smart married women, and then
taking a racquet at tennis with a fashionable
curate and the Governor’s daughter ! Dr. Camp-
bell says he warned the General of a jealous feeling
in regard to him, but does not report Mac.’s reply.
Perhaps he retorted that in Ceylon (on the
authority of Bishop Heber, who wanted a rhyme
for isle) “only man is vile,” and that for all their
jealousies he wasn’t going to bang saxpences away
on jungle teas. Perhaps he just swore, and
took up a book on tactics. When at Darley’s
garden party in Sydney he turned his back on the
fawning ladies to talk tactics with another military
man. Mac. was just the kind of soldier to glory
in the unpopularity that comes of no ping-pong,
bridge, or champagne cup.
Concerning the lack of mention of a Lady
Macdonald. It seems at Sydney, at the Highland
gathering in his horror, the General returned
thanks on behalf of his wife and family.
Primate Saumarez Smith’s eldest daughter, who
accompanied her father to London, is to be mar-
ried to Rev. Mr. Griffiths after Lent. The fianc4
was chaplain to the Primate when in Sydney.
He has secured a country living outside the Great
Smoke.
Two big fascinating bundles of accordeon-
pleated chiffon, spindrift lace, narrow applique
ribbon, sprays of pink rosebuds and bunches of
violets have held large street audiences for the
last week at a George-st. draper’s window. None
knew what they were, and many went inside to
ask. “Opera muffs” was the reply. They are
Midsummer-night’s-dream things to look at, but
Titania and her whole court might house in one of
them.
In an English seed catalogue I notice that the
blue-gum is recommended to florists as a pot-plant
for green houses ! And another species of euca-
lyptus—a Tasmanian one (which grows in its
native land to a height of three or four hundred
feet) is advertised as “forming a very elegant
bush, or small tree, almost hardy.”
A returned Durbarite says of the great Salma-
gundy that it was shockingly mismanaged. Train
service so disorganised that people were stranded
in Delhi for a fortnight after the curtain had rung
down. Lord Curzon’s camp cost for feeding and
sleeping £3 per day—use of carriage, £4 per day.
Fourteen days at Delhi cost this Durbarite’s party
of four £300. One native potentate, finding that
the terms for himself and retinue would eat up his
State’s revenues for three years or so, bought a
plot of ground and hastily ran up a series of
bungalows, and so saved his fortune.
What a noisy city Sydney is. The roar and
whirr of the electric trams is a big improvement
on the awful rattle of the ’busses over the serrated
blocks in the olden days, but it is still an awful
noise. There seems to be no law to make the
drivers of vehicles oil their wheels, and George-
street is sometimes filled with the shrieks and
groans of the dry axle. In the residential streets,
the hawker, the bottle-oh man, the fiend who
sells stale prawns, the howling of the tied-up dog,
and all the other nuisances, bawl and shout the
livelong day, and at night cabs rattle past,
cats hold concerts, children scream and howl,
and all these make the darkness more dreadful
than the daylight. Some day the silent city will
be evolved, where the vehicles will glide noise-
lessly by, and the people pass softly to and fro,
while birds whistle and chortle in the shade trees
that grow along the dreamy streets. In that city
life will be worth living, and the only nuisance
will be the dead organ grinders hanging on the
lamp posts at the corners. But there will be com-
pensations about that nuisance.
A travelling Australian recently met Ada
Rehan,. the American actress. She was with the
Marquis of Ripon’s family, who are her staunchest
friends. Ada is now a middle-aged woman, with
white hair. But, as she is very rich, she can
afford the white hair and the years.
On the evening of Easter Monday Miss Mulli-
gan’s farewell concert will eventuate in Sydney
Town Hall. The huge programme is to be given
by a cloud of first-class artists, and is under vice-
regal patronage. The proverb that a prophet is
without honor in his own country does not apply
to this grand-niece of Vincent Wallace, for in
Manly, where she lives, the local hall was crowded
night after night to hear her in “Pinafore” and
the “Pirates of Penzance 1 ” and the village has
evidently made up its mind to be present at this
farewell concert, as it has arranged for a special
steamer to take it home after the show. The box
plan is at Paling’s, and the function is under the
management of Mr. Robert Kohblanck, by per-
mission of autocrat J. C. Williamson. The little
singer will leave, under the wing of her mamma,
for Europe about the 25th, and will go straight to
Paris for tuition under Marchesi.
A bazaar in aid of the Drought Relief (Mayor’s
Fund) was held at “Kioto,” Mr. T. Playfair’s
place at Waverley, last week. Enough money
was realised to lighten some selector’s load and
buy him seed wheat, if not to restock his sheep-
paddock. The fine big trees and far-reaching
garden of “Kioto” were illuminated and trade
seemed brisk. Serving damsels at the stalls wore
huge Distinguished Service orders of green ribbon
and lace aprons, with pockets quite big enough to
hold one chocolate-cream.
Dr. Richard Steer Bowker, M.L.O., who died
at his house at Darling Point, Sydney, last week,
at the age of 88, had been in practice in N.S.W.
for close upon 66 years. He was a skilful surgeon
and oculist. Four of his six sons follow their
father’s profession. Mrs. Tom Rolin, the
youngest of his nine children, is a Sydney beauty.
For Miss Rose Scott, the woman-suffragist leader
and lecturer, the late Dr. Bowker had a great
regard. All the same, the last time he took his
seat in the Council was to try to defeat the
Woman Suffrage Bill.
Melba is back in Sydney, staying with the
State Governor’s family.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Prof. Peterson is about again after a serious
lilr.css. But the picturesque professor is not
likely to enjoy really robust health as long as the
struggle for the prosperity of the Conservatorium
goes on, and with such a masterly rival school as
Marshall Hall’s that struggle must go on It was
surmised, long ago, that Prof. Peterson was facing
a position he didn’t fully understand when he
accepted the Ormond Chair of Music. At the
time Melbourne was in a ferment from old-woman-
newspaper interpretation of Marshall Hall’s in-
different and harmless ‘ poetry,’ and the Scotch-
byterian clans cried aloud for a righteous brother
with a poetic taste for Moody and Sanky
to come and wrest the baton from ‘ this brilliant
blasphemist. ’ So Prof. Peterson came with his
pink cheeks and unblemished record, and settled
down happily to the work of starting afresh a
fumigated and Christian Conservatorium. Then
the fickle public, including many of the scan-
dalised Scotchbyterians, did a somersault and
landed over at the doors of the ex-professor they
had tried to hound down. Every week Marshall
Hall’s Academy receives fresh recruits, and grows
in power. The Ormond professor finds it difficult
to keep the orthodox Conservatorium even
numerically strong. It will never be musically
productive.
“ One of the most moneyed bachelors Mel-
bourne knows, tiring of the monotony of rude
health and society life, developed a fashionable
complaint—something royal, like appendicitis—
and was laid up for repairs in a private hospital,
where the nurses are given to prettiness, and the
sawbones is famed for the sharpness of his wit
and his lancet. The sick man had a good time,
got better slowly, and was so indifferent to the
outside world that he refused to see visiting
angels of his smart circle, and let the nurses enjoy
the exotics sent to his bed-side. When convales-
cence—an interesting stage in a * smart ’ hospital
—could be feigned no longer, and the petted Dives
had to take up his kit and walk, the nurses wept,
and were only comforted when he promised to
catch the next fashionable complaint.
“The anxiety of Melbourne’s surplus girls to
sample the fun of life in the nursing r6le ! They
wait in hundreds for admittance to don the sober
uniform of the sisterhood, even though premiums
be exacted. The romantic patting of Romeo’s or
Don Juan’s pillow looms up all the while, and the
initial stages of ward-work —disgusting in many
aspects—awake no aesthetic qualms.
“ Nowadays, girls who want to ‘go in for
writing,’ do a course of hospital nursing, by way
of seeing life and accumulating ‘copy.’ Certainly
nurses do get a few eye-openers. Scribe knows of
one who had to comb and plait pigtails for two
Chinaman patients every morning. She says she
found Ah Sam and Co. the cleanest, most grate-
ful, and most modest patients in the wards. As
far as modesty goes, sick men, say nurses, are not
wanting in delicacy of feeling or manner, but sick
gentlemen sometimes seem to suppose that because
a girl is a nurse, small reserves, not to say decen-
cies, would be wasted on her.
“ A wistful male musician contributes his ex-
perience of musical and business dealings with a
notable star. When the sea was calm and life
abroad dull, there arose a war rumble, and they
guessed madam was rousing on someone. ‘I
think she used to do it for the fun of the
thing. But it made one feel horribly unsafe,’
and the young man gives a shiver. ‘Really, she
was wonderfully nice to me. I don’t know why,’
winds up the coy tootler, as if he (the rogue) knew
well, but wasn’t going to tell. Probably madam
scorned to show fight to so sad a foe. As she
mentioned to a Toorak chum, ‘ I’ll mammy him,
or he’ll sink us with tears.
“ A breezy Melb. surgeon scored brilliantly the
other day, in a case where a young wife wanted
permission to sit and sigh all day by the side of
her sick spouse in the private hospital, where he lay
under treatment. The surgeon gently hinted that
this was strictly contrary to rules. The jealous dame
persisted, saying she would give her darling all the
care and attention possible. Then the man of the
lancet was annoyed and hatched a plot. “ Nurse,”
he called out later, as the wife sat at her vigil,
“ Get so and so and so and so (naming all sorts of
unpleasant instruments) and treat the patient.
Be very careful and very thorough.” The devoted
wife took the alarm. “ Doctor, not in my
presence. Wait, for just five minutes and I’ll be
gone.” And she was.
“ There was a big display of woman’s private
or drawing-room leg, a while back, at a South
Yarra afternoon corroboree. During lunch, the
talk had turned from scandal to matters of weight
and dress-measures, and a controversy arose as to
the figure at which one and another turned the
scales. Finally, one started up with ‘You really
don’t believe I’m twelve stone? I’m as hard as
nails. Why, look at that for a leg.’ When the
laughter had subsided, other contentious legs were
unveiled, and there was at length the spectacle of
a dozen fashionable woman with skirts hoisted
above their knees in a fierce contest for the fattest
calf.
“ Not Lord Hawke’s, but the other man’s
cricketers left Melbourne unhonored and unwept
by Tuesday’s Oroya. They looked a solitary set
when some male Melbourne sympathisers left and
they lined up to the deck-rails watching the
kiss-throwing to luckier brethren on board. A full
but not over-interesting passenger list included
Mrs. and Miss Sargood. The leave-taking of a
daughter of Israel was the moistest thing about
the scene. Her ‘ fader ’ kissed her many times,
then ‘ mudder ’ slobbered her hugely, and a dozen
' brudders ’ rained kisses on her wet cheeks.
After that the female friends—all of the imposing
nose order—took her to their ample bosoms and
hugged her warmly. Hebrew kinship is even
hotter and more demonstrative than the Hibernian
brother love.
“Miss Vida Goldstein wishes to take the place
of the late Henry Parkes as the Women’s Moses.
Here is the style of her law-giving: ‘Australian
women are going to use their vote, first and fore-
most, for the best men. Men who are known to
be immoral, or unscrupulous, or self-seeking, or
un-public-spirited, will not receive the support
of the women. I admit their choice will be
extremely limited.’
“Women visiting Wirths’ Circus these times
waste no affection on the ‘Marvellous Howards.’
As the blindfold partner rattles forth minute de-
scriptions of curios, &c., almost faster than his
mate can collect them from the audience, petti-
coat wearers agree sotto voce that, fake or no fake,
it would be ‘ simply horrid ’ to meet such a seer
at close quarters, not to speak of drawing one of
his sort in the matrimonial lottery. Picture him
penetrating all the small disguises and shams dear
to feminine nature and mentally noting the shreds
and patches of the sex’s physical make-up—the
artificial tooth, the pinned-on ‘switch,’ the pneu-
matic hip-yoke !
“Should a girl be bent upon marrying into Saw-
dust circles, I should strongly advise her to set
her affections upon the “ Strong Man ” of a troupe,
provided, of course, he’s still unappropriated.
Since the days of Samson these brawny heroes
have been as wax in feminine hands and seem
born to be hen-pecked. There’s one professional
Hercules I used to know who is tied absolutely to the
apron-strings of his scrap of a wife. This lady
did not hesitate to trade on her lord’s reputation
for strength. Said she to the landlord of a tiny
out-back inn: ‘My ’usband is too proud to sleep
in a back room. Give us your swellest, or ’e’ll do
for you.’ ‘ Who might the party be ? ’ queried the
landlord. ‘Z , the Emperor of Strength,’
snapped the wife. Landlord grew gushing at once
and laid his establishment at the feet of poor
Biceps, who stood by, mild as milk, and only
anxious to find a corner wherein to snore in peace.
“ A common sight is that of President Henry
Wrixon’s daughter, dressed as sombrely as a
convent postulant, passing along to some asylum
or other, on a pilgrimage of mercy. She is one of
the most enthusiastic and gentlest “slummers”
of Melbourne.
“ The first she to win a Vic. law degree is that
astute Scotch girl—Miss Floss Greig. The ‘ Floss ’
is annoying, but that’s her look out. Two of her
sisters ply the lancet in a busy quarter of Mel-
bourne. The Greigs are Dundee lasses, and have
the national characteristics of foresight, common
sense, endurance, and steady perseverance.
“ In the appointment of Public Service officers
to the new Appeal Board, it is found that the
feminine vote goes mainly to the wearer of the
sportiest tie, the fanciest vest and the brightest
tan shoes. Even the crusty she-clerks—long-
service record-makers —are seriously inclined to
favor the jaunty males, the smilers of the Service.
The male who cocks his hat to the side, and
brightens up visibly when even a sober ‘ lustre ’
skirt whisks into sight may be a lamentable trifler
and a humbug, but he has evidently a corner in
his heart for the sex, and is held to be the best
battler for her interests.

“ The brief skirt is upon us. In Collins-street
these mornings, when the fairest do the rounds of
shopping and the massage parlors, the short skirt
is to be seen and the back curves are erased. The
smart trip along, hands swinging by the side, the
tail-less dress hanging in demure folds like the fat
about a holy man’s neck.
“Bendigo isn’t beaten yet for prodigies in the
vocal line. The latest is a Miss Eva Laughton,
who is shortly to have a preliminary canter on the
professional boards. Her voice is alleged to be
sweet and soul-stirring.
“The Purity Brigade is extending its inquiries
into the quarters of the barmaid. The scandal of
‘ spooning bars ’ crept into large paper type the
other day. Members of the Purity Brigade want
to know— ‘ If hundreds of these swell “spooning
bars” are in existence, who supports them?’
They intend to ask their husbands.
“Another She-Club looms on the horizon. This
time, ‘ nice ’ people are understood to barrack for
it, and Dames Forrest, Turner, and Madden stand
in trust for its respectability of purpose. The
Western dame is at the head of the movement,
having for her model the Katta-Katta Club of
Perth. A fine suggestive name that!
“ What changes have come over the Women’s
Hospital Catmittee ! No longer a sanctuary for
idle purrers, nor a distraction for ambitious
fashionables, it jogs along, faintly reported, made
up now of shrewd, intelligent women. There is
not much rustling of silk linings in the Catmittee
rooms these days, but neither is there any knitting
of socks, nor sharpening of private axes.”
“ The Victorian Chief Justicess is establishing
a firm friendship with the new bishopess. The
Waxellency leaves all religious matters in the
hands of his sweet-looking dame, hence the high
Anglican tone of his household. Meanwhile he
himself gives a shoulder to Jew and Gentile,
Salvationist and Jesuit, by affable aphorisms
on special occasions. Periodically it is stated that
he is no longer a member of the R.C. faith, and
periodically there is a denial of the statement.

That is how Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills cure all stomach and bowel complaints like cholera, dysentery,
diarrhoea, dyspepsia, windiness, and indigestion.
JUNEE MAN CURED.— Mr. William Perrie, of Old Junee, an expert shearing machinist, well-known in the
Riverina and Lafchlan, says : “In June, 1902,1 began to suffer terribly from cholera and weakening diarrhoea. My
stomach was so upset that every meal brought on indigestion, heartburn and sour gases. A dead, heavy weight lay across
my chest. Fits of wind and internal pains kept me awake for hours. My nerves became unstrung, and the terrible
diarrhoea continued to drain away my strength. Seeing Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills recommended in the Sydney Mail, I
began taking them. They enriched my blood and braced up my nerves from the very first. In a day or two the diarrhoea
lessened and then stopped. In a few weeks I was a strong, hearty man again.”

PERSONAL ITEMS.
It is feared that Dr. Bevan, the cherubic
Melbourne parson, intends to publish a “ History
of Australia, or something in that way.
It was Home Secretary J. F. Gr. Foxton
who was mainly responsible for the comprehensive
and admirable Queensland Local Authorities Act.
The threatened lawsuit, W. P. Crick v.
Tom Slattery, will be interesting from a police-
court point of view. Both are sons of policemen.
Speaker Holder is so fond of politics that
he took the chair in a model Parliament at St.
Peter s (an Adelaide suburb) last week. The place
is not in his constituency, either.
Edwin Smith, president of S.A. Cricketing
Association and a score of other organisations
was 72 on Monday, April 6. He made his money
by beer, but lost much of it by drought.

Sergeant Hanley, one of the warders at Yatala Labor

The Supreme Court of Victoria only last
week decided to strike off the roll of barristers
Joseph Henry Grey, the Geelong Gent. One who
did almost the biggest swindle in Australian his-
tory some years ago.
Little Sammy Way completes his 67th
year on April 11. He showed the cosmopolitan
character of his desire to be agreeable to all sorts
and conditions of men by calling for three cheers
for Major Taylor on the Adelaide Oval the other
day.
A few old semi-forgottens to be met any
day in Melbourne streets : Abe Hicken, Jimmy
Munro, Mat Davies, Bryan O’Loghlen, and
Murray Ross, who built the enormous white
elephant of the beetroot sugar factory, at Ross-
town.
Judge Burnside, of Westralia, made what
is surely a unique commencement of his judicial
career. To doll up in the odious millinery of the
black cap and sentence six people to death, and
within a fortnight to find the Full Court, on
appeal, agreeing that there was no substantial
evidence on which five of them could be convicted,
is, at least, unusual.

Charters Towers Miner published the following
hurricane item from the Lower Burdekin :
“ Kelly’s hotel lost the roof. Mr. Ching Do’s shop
was blown down.” The white man is just
“Kelly.” The yellow man is alluded to rever-
ently as Mr. Cock-a-Doodle-Do, or whatever his
appelation may be.
Jenkins Iscariot, who has just completed
his 25th year in S. Australia, where he landed a
full Amurrikan citizen, has held office for 10 years
and 3 months ; which makes a record, beating
even Kingston’s. Only his book-canvassing train-
ing has made this possible. Like Jones (the man
that What Happened To), he learned then to
offer them playing-cards if he couldn’t do business
in prayer-books.

As Amy Castles led her tuneful sister to
the breach of public life, so Premier John See
leads brother David. David has started on a

A prominent JS.S.W. politician who was
fond of distributing photographs of “me and the
gallant soldier So-and-so ” doesn’t send them
around now, and he recognises the great truth
that a man should never be photographed in any
company except that of his dog.
Whether it had anything to do with what
happened to him in the end or not, Hector
Macdonald did have a detestation of “society.”
In Australia once, bored by the effusive attentions
of some haw-haw officahs, he begged his hosts to
introduce him to “ somebody less distinguished.”
That discredited and astoundingly cheeky
person, Captain Miles, whose jobbery lost him his
seat in the Assembly and his portfolio in the
Braddon Ministry, and brought that Ministry to
the dust, was a candidate at the Tasmanian elec-
tion of last week. The stench of his Strahan
Marine Board doings was too recent —still he got
234 votes.
The Bulletin regrets to hear a rumor
that some godless person lately prigged four of
Cardinal Moran’s nightshirts. If he had only
five, this must leave him rather short. And, by
the way, it is said that no really orthodox ecclesi-
astic of the R.C. faith wears pyjamas. There is
thought to be something frivolous, if not actually
immodest, about pyjamas.
Current rumor in London hints that
Whitaker Wright, of the great London and
Globe smash, relied for immunity from proceed-
ings on the that he had been “uncle” to
King Ned. Certainly proceedings went after
Wright on feet of lead. The London and Globe
swindle looks almost as ancient now as Achan’s
prigging of the Babylonish garment.

What a healthy occupation is kinging 1
Queen Alexandra has gone on a visit to her father
the King of Denmark, who is still reigning gaily
at the age of 85. And even he isn’t the oldest of
European potentates. Adolphus, Duke of Luxem-
bourg (if he hasn’t died within the last few weeks
without The Bulletin noticing it), is 86. Frederick
Bill, of Mecklenburg-Strelitz, is close up with a
record of 83, and Francis Joseph of Austria is 73.
“F. ”: The deceased Colonel Thornton was
a restless being all his days. He was an Imperial
captain at Wynyard Square, Paddington and
Hobart. He jolted off as farmer ; turned stock
buyer; became grazier ; jolted again, and sought
a billet in the civil service; became coroner,
became police magistrate; and when he abandoned
these lines turned to land-speculating, house-
building, and even at 80 was ready to entertain
any new line of enterprise that promised well.
The death of “ Mint Sauce ” Jones (Vic.)
recalls an assault case in which he figured at
Fitzroy when M.L.A., in ’66. Writing for a
country newspaper, he said something disagree-
able about J. Dennistoun Woods, also a member
of the Assembly. Woods and De Courcy Ireland,
M.L.A., son of the Q.C., took on the job of
assaulting Jones. That large, voluble tailor fought
several rounds with very little luck, but he had
both Ireland and Woods fined at Fitzroy police
court.
The last instalment of the “National
Purse ” has just been clutched at by Seddon, and
in acknowledging it the modest man spoke for
two hours, concluding:
He had dropped across a few lines of Pope’s which he
thought described himself, and, though he seldom dropped
into poetry, he would repeat them :
“ Statesman, yet friend, to truth of soul sincere ;
In action faithful and in honor clear ;
Who broke no promise, served no private end ;
Who gained no title, and who lost no friend.”
Died in Adelaide last week A. T. Hodg-
son, aged 73, one of the oldest booksellers in
Australia. He was in business at Ballarat at the
time of the Eureka trouble ; was afterwards at
Castlemaine, and had been in S. A. for 20 years.
Reputed to be possessed of means, he lived
entirely by himself, slept under the counter of his
little shop, and was frugal to the verge of starva-
tion. Hair, face, and clothes seemed to have aged
to the same color, and he was seldom seen to speak
to anyone except a customer. He fainted one day
from weakness, was carried to the hospital and
died. He published a history of the Kelly gang,
many reciters and other cheap books.

“ Bushie ” speaks irreverently of a great
man :
At Temora (N.S.W.), t’other day, heard the Owe’Sulli
van for the first time. Imagine a stodgy, common-place
person with a thick neck and a cropped head, and a voice
like a file on a wood-saw. That's the Owe ! His oratory
is of the argumentative type, and his argument is mostly
abuse of his opponents, whom he denounces as “ boun-
ders,” “ malicious liars,” “ calamity howlers,” “ misery-
mongers,” “ idiotic incapables,” etc. The Owe is forceful
and not very convincing, and as I listened I formed a
mental picture of what the Athenian “ bounder ” Oleon
was like.
Charles Pharazyn, who committed suicide
while in London, was one of Maoriland’s boss
landowners, and an absentee at that. Naturally
he was a virulent enemy of Liberal land laws, and,
according to him, the land and income tax did im-
mense harm to Maoriland by preventing the
accumulation of foreign debt in the land. Pharazyn
was an immense believer in foreign debt. Two
years ago he visited Maoriland, when heannounced
himself a convert to the Seddon policy, especially
its gory Imperialism. Ungenerous critics hinted
that the purchase by the Government at satis-
factory prices of some of the Pharazyn land had a
good deal to do with the “conversion.”
All Fools’ Day recalls a western practical
joke. “Benny” Lee, police-magistrate of
Bathurst, who at one time represented Maitland
in the Assembly, and once had a big “scrap ” on
the floor of the House with David Buchanan,
lived on the Vale-road. He was never popular,
and on one Fools’ Day his house was besieged by
vanmen, carriers’ and tradesmen’s carts of every
description. Some had come to remove Mr.
Lee’s effects to a new residence ; requisites of
almost every description were delivered, to the
bewilderment of the household ; even Sawyer, the
undertaker, was brought out in his best funeral
strut. “Giraffe” Taylor was then in Bathurst,
and got the credit of this casual mirthfulness.
Governor M‘Gregor, formerly of Papua,
retires from the colonial service
when his term at White Man’s
Grave (Lagos) expires. He never
had a rosy time. 111-health dogged
his youth, and when he dropped
medicine in Glasgow and entered
the Government service he only
got the jobs that men with high
social influence wouldn’t look at,
being himself a man with no
, N high social influence. He did
his best to get to Tasmania
after his 10 years in Papua,
but that chanceof renewing
his vigor was denied him.
He longed for a few years ,
in N.S.W. or even Queens-
land, but here again he was
refused, and he left the
purgatory of Papua for
the Sheol of Western
Africa. Still he has almost
managed to last out his
term and earn his pension.

Dr. Bowker, who “ went over ” on Friday,
aged 88 years, was perhaps the oldest medical
practitioner in N.S.W., his London diploma being
dated 1836, sixty-seven years ago. He came to
Australia in the late Forties and settled at New-
castle, where he resided until 1873. In the
latter year he removed to Sydney. Dr. Bowker
was not a member of the first Parliament under
Responsible Government, as has been stated. He
was in the second Parliament, which assembled in
March, 1858, and was dissolved in April, 1859,
under the Electoral Reform Act. He remained
out of Parliament until 1877, when he represented
Newcastle; in 1887 he was appointed to the
Legislative Council. He was buried in the
little cemetery at Paterson, where also lie the
remains of Speaker Arnold, who was drowned in
the flood of 1875 while endeavoring to save six-
penn’orth of pumpkins. Dr. Bowker has left four
sons in the medical profession.
Some men are never satisfied, as these
extracts from a letter addressed to the editor of
The Bulletin by C. G. Gurr, of Adelaide, show :
Your item of news concerning myself has got such an
amount of inaccuracy crammed into it as to make it
worth preserving as the ’orrid example to set before
young reporters. (1) My first start in life was in the
office of Pasquin ; the solicitor’s office came later. (2) I
never left auctioneering to become a land agent. (3) Nor
a mining secretary. (4) Never returned to auctioneering
again. (5) The paper I edit is not named the Webkly
Record, but rejoices in the title of the Weekly News.
Finally the gross inaccuracy running over and dripping
from the four corners of the paragraph is the suggestion
that lam changeable in my occupations. For the past 21
years I have been in the same business in the same pre-
mises in the same street of the same city, which certainly
says something for my “ sticking ” qualities. ... I hope
to visit you and take you down at your favorite game of
bowls, say on the Waverley Green.
In reply whereunto the editor of The Bulletin
desires to say that he was never on Waverley
bowling green in his life, and doesn’t know a bowl
from a giraffe.
The late William Jones, of Footscray (Vic.),
having died intestate, an early claimant obtained
letters of administration, but had hardly got them
fixed up before another claimant began an action
to revoke the said letters. Whilst these two were
having it out with much profit to the legal sharks,
a third person transpired with a claim that he was
the only surviving cousin or something of the
deceased. Before a worried court could dis-
entangle this three-cornered confusion, the Eng-
lish mail arrived and announced a score or more
new claimants. The court, finding such a plenti-
ful crop of claimants without any undue publicity
being given outside, ordered that advertisements
should be inserted in papers all over the world,
logically opining that if no advertisement brought
scores of claimants, some advertisement would
bring some more. When the ten thousand new
claimants accrue from this, and have been in-
dividually and collectively proven first cousins to
deceased, the court will probably rule that the
costs are to come out of the estate, after which,
in all likelihood, there will be not much left to
argue about.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors. —ln the case of ethnological and
natural history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience ; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific
value.
ON THE LAND.—LOOKING HIS DIFFICULTIES FULL IN THE FACE.
D- Harum ” : Re 00100 (21/3/’O3) on
horses that have died through swallowing sand,
knives, glass, scrap-iron, etc., have heard of such
things often, but never to the extent of Ooloo’s 75
pounds of miscellany. Once saw the skeleton of
a horse on the Wallaway Plains (S. A.). On the
ground where the stomach had rested were some
two dozen smooth pebbles which one was forced
to conclude the poor animal had swallowed some
time or other, for there were no other pebbles of
the kind anywhere around.
*** . *
“ W.F.H.” : Recently visited a Northern
cordial factory, where the men were unable to
bottle on account of the great number of bees and
ants which haunted the place. Proprietor stated
that the insects, being unable to find enough
nourishment owing to the drought, visited his
factory in myriads to devour his syrup. In many
cases the ravenous bees “ put away ” such a quan-
tity of syrup that they became too dazed to get
away, and could only “flop ’’ on the ground,
where the ants attacked them, tore them asunder,
and took them home in pieces for future con-
sumption.
Not only does the Australian squatter
discourage population by refusing to employ
people burdened with “encumbrances,” but he
also does his best in Western Queensland to starve
out the existing population of single shearers and
rouseabouts. At Milo station, near Adavale (Q.),
for example, all the shearing this year is. being
done by Young and Co., of Sydney, who import
all their men from Sydney. These imported men
are to get a run of sheds in a district where
hundreds of Queensland shearers and rouse-
abouts are living in a state verging on starvation.
* * • *
Australia has a few wet spots. Listen to
this :
Geraldton reports that the Johnstone River is in flood,
and, anticipating bad weather, the Kuranda, Gowrie,
and Ellen remained at the wharfs last night, but left
early this morning. The traffic between .Geraldton and
outside districts has been stopped owing to swollen
creeks. The rainfall for the past four days was 14 inches
29 points, and the total to date since the beginning of the
wet season is nearly 103 inches. It is still raining heavily.

The loose-limbed person who keeps the
Drovers’ Rest will now be wise to have a
magnifying-glass under the bar. One day, not
long ago, he served a black brother in whom,
somewhere or other, as the result of an acci-
dent in a former generation, there was a fair
whitish streak. He was charged with supply-
ing an aborigine; but Judge Prynnge, now
says that, in acquitting him, the way-back “ beak ”
was, for once in a way, right. But perhaps, on
further consideration, the lord of the Drovers’
Rest needn’t buy the instrument before alluded
to. It might be enough if he hired one for a
time; for presently, when all the very old ones of
the tribes have died out, he will be fairly safe in
taking it for granted that all the rest have the
saving white streak somewhere. The present
decision gives the law against the liquoring of
aborigines a pretty hard blow ; in a few years, if
it stands, it will about knock it out altogether.

Under the philanthropic guise of helping
the drought-stricken person out back, a section of
the squatter tribe conceives it a favorable oppor-
tunity to get some necessary work done on the
cheap— sweatingly cheap, that is. Two of them
empowered a Queanbeyan (N.S.W.), solicitor,
t’other day, to gather the local unemployed
together and offer them, with the humane inten-
tion of alleviating distress, scrub-cutting at from
ninepence to half-crown an acre !

The last male aborigine of Tenterfield
(N.S.W.) district, who was locally known as
“ Jacky from the Mole,” died a lonely and miser-
able death on 9th March last. The body had
been lying four days in the gunyah when the
police visited the camp. “Why you not come
before ?” his gin reproached. She is crippled and
nearly blind, and the poor old thing had then
been two days without water. She is all that is
left of a once numerous tribe,

“Shadow” on beer-drinking record (8.,
21/3/’03): In September, 1899, while in Dubbo
(N.S.W.), bet a prominent man in that town a
new hat he couldn’t drink 15 pints of beer in 15
ra i nu tes ; loser to pay for beer also. I lost. He
swallowed 16 in the stipulated time. Since then
I have found that drinking one pint per minute
has ceased to be a feat, and is merely an incident,
It may have been a record once, but once isn’t
often.

accounted for 2000, one cartridge often being re-

gully, and foxes, suspended in masses from every
branch, covered an area of about a mile square,

“H.S." writes concerning aboriginal mas-
sage :
A black columnar smoke has suddenly shot skywards—
in a little while to be answered by a dozen others. It is
an aboriginal “ wireless telegraph,” bearing to the blacks
far and near the news of the stranding of a whale. This
means days and days of lusty gorging. Men, women,
children rally to the spot, to dig with their tomahawks
into the huge carcase, which is soon a human hive with
continuous streams of glistening bodies passing in and
out. In, to gorge anew, and out, to seek fresh air and
roll upon the ground.
After a few days the whole neighborhood smells
“high,” but still the gorge goes on, until “massage”
has to be resorted to ; the blacks rolling each other over
and over, and kneading each other’s distended stomachs
with all their power. I have seen men, possessed of three
or four wives, stretched flat on their backs whilst their
spouses played “ follow my leader ” over hubby’s body
until relief came, and enabled him to once more tackle
the feast. The same man is often robustly massaged
many times over before the great “larder” has become
too gatney.
Astounding what an amount of solid food a blackfellow
can Btuff into him. I have known a hungry nig to con-
sume a turkey of about ten pounds weight, entrails and
all—leaving only the feathers.
* • * •
“ Amiens ” : The grasshopper-plague in
the Green Ponds (Tas.) district is so bad that in
many places along the Jordan there is scarcely a
vestige of grass. The insects eat off the tops of
turnips and other root crops, and, where the roots
are exposed, eat right down into these also. Fowls
get a fine feed, but the eggs of the grasshopper-
eating fowl become too strong to be pleasant. The
only check to the pest is when the wet weather
drowns out the young.
• • * *
The very latest idea in rain-production,
put forward in the daily papers, is to send up
balloons and scatter fine dust on the clouds,
thereby starting the process of condensation.
Well, any of the drought centres from Hay to
Hungerford will supply the dust, if the man with
the idea will supply the balloon and the clouds.
He had been a red-headed jackaroo but
had evoluted to a perfect “ wayback ” and
attained management of a small sta-
tion. Later on he was made a J.P.,
and, having been duly sworn-in,
swaggered about town rejoicing in his
new dignity. Very, very anxious
was he to “sit on” somebody—but
no luck —not even a drunken carrier
in town. Presently Bullocky Jack
came along, and, taking out a sove-
reign, the J.P. said : “ Here, take
this, and get yourself run in. I’ll
pay the fine.” Half-an-hour
afterwards the new magistrate
sentenced Jack for “drunk and
disorderly ” to two hours in the
lock-up or two bob fine. “I’ll
pay it for you this time, my
man ; but you must be more
careful in future.” That night
Bullocky Jack slept the sleep of
the really drunk, and a pleased
and proud J.P. wrote to his
people about the arduous duties
he had to perform as magistrate.
Mallee and Wimmera fanners still keep
finding by sad experience, that sending their
horses down south, even on a free pass, was
not altogether the bunch of violets they expected
it would be. To the men from the drought and
sand all grass looked like life, and horses were
hurried on to unsuitable pasture in sheep and
cattle country with disastrous results. One man
took down a team of eight, expecting to have
them in good condition for the cropping season,
The then value of the team was £l6O. He
recently returned with the remnants—three en-
feebled beasts (about £3O worth). Ihe others are
atnaas
horse had grown accustomed toother diet, and,
when he was grassed recklessly, the shock was too
much for him.

“ Back-Pew ” : The yarn (8.,
June 14) of the good Melbourne
maiden-lady who didn’t know what
“tight” meant recalls another,
handicapped by being nearly
true. To work among the
foreign portion of his flock,
a very stern and pious
R.O. bishop of an in-
land N.S.W. diocese
imported aßussian
priest trained in
10 languages at
Rome. Hi s
English he
had got from
text books,
and soknew
no slang and
few collo-
quialisms.
Now it hap-
pened that
when he ar-
rived he was
put under the
wing of a senior
priest, a rollick-
ing Irishman,
who complained
that the polyglot’s
language was too
starched for back-
country use in sum-
mer, and so under-
took to teach him
English as the Austra-
lian talks it. And as he
reckoned that about the first (and the second and
all through till the last) job he would be put
on to, would be to collect funds for “the poor
sisters” and “ the little orphans” and the others,
they began with money—“ s quid,” “ half-a-quid,”
“ caser/’ “ sprat,” and the rest. The Polyglot was
sent out after coin in the next few days; and when
he counted it up before his Lordship that night, that
stern and pious father was horrified to be told
that his new priest had got “a quid from Mr.
Patrick Maloney,” and a “ rock ” from somebody
else, and so on right down to Mrs. M‘Sweeney’s
children, who had sent a sprat apiece for the
little “sisters.” There was a transfer from that
parish soon afterwards.
* * * *
Commenting on the fact that out of one
shipment of pigs from Sydney nine suffered
from pneumonia, and the rest had “dread-
ful bad coughs,” a Bellinger (N.S.W.) magis-
trate remarked, Wednesday before last, on
the remarkable fact that “although there was
a large staff of salaried inspectors in Sydney,
such a number of palpably diseased pigs
should have been allowed to pass through the
saleyards.” That recalls the case in which a
Sydney butcher, on being asked to give his
opinion upon a lot of meat seized by an inspector,
said, “So far as I can judge this meat” is
certainly not fit for human food; but I don’t
see anything to prevent its being used for making
sausages.” Beyond doubt the present system
of inspecting meat is almost useless. Putting
a mark on a condemned carcase is not enough, as
a venturesome tradesman could still sell “prime
joints ” off it and throw the mark in as an un-
charged-for “extra.” In the country many per-
sons still do what is termed a “ killing business ”
in more senses than one. “Why didn’t your
father send over that side of bacon that he pro-
mised ?” asked a Western “cookie” as he met his
neighbor’s son. ‘‘Oh ! ” was the artless reply,
“dad isn’t goin’ to kill that pig he spoke about
now. It’s gettin’ better, an’ Mr. Mooney, th’
hoss doctor, says it didn’t have cancer at all. But
there’s another cove lookin’ a bit multy, so I
s’pose he’ll kill it.”

"HOOSHTAH!”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was at a Goldfields Mission,
In the land of Sand and Sin,
That a wandering apostle
Asked the Groper miners in,
And each pagan, as he entered,
Wore a deprecating grin.
Notwithstanding their reluctance
And their “ Oome-t’-look-on ” air,
They all listened, with attention,
To the parson’s lengthy prayer.
And they mixed up the responses
In a way that made him glare.
The proceedings went so gaily
That M’Ginn said he could feel
“Sorter saved f’r ever feelin’ 1”
Over him begin to steal.
But he withered when the parson
Said : “ Dear friends, now we will kneel! ”
In the pub. they never did it,
And it put them ill at ease
To be asked, in tones so solemn,
Just to go down on their knees ;
And the parson, as he waited,
Waved his wipe and feigned to sneeze.
Second after weary second
Ticked away, but still they stood,
Like sheep waiting for a leader,
And that parson, kind and good,
Gazed expectantly around him,
With a smile that was of wood.
Of a sudden, ’midst that awful
Breathless stillness of the dead,
Came the voice of Camel Hogan,
As he slightly raised his head,
“ Can’t ye divils understand there ?
“ Hooshtah —hooshtah !” Hogan said.
Then these pagans there assembled,
Heeding not the parson’s frown,
When they heard that camel order,
Which is Afghan for “ kneel down 1”
Sank to worship their Creator,
With a thud that woke the town.

23 HUNTER STREET.
A select circle of old-timers,
t’other siders, and assorted liars, was
discussing the drought in a Boulder
bar (W.A.) when Pigweed Jimmy
rose with his tenth beer in his hand
and spoke: “Yes, ez you chaps sez,
this drought is pooty bad, but it’s
nothin’ to th’ drought as kem along
when I was drovin’ a flock o’ sheep
for ole Tim Crossland. They was
feedin’ on pig-weed roots, as they
alius holds a little moisture. But
bimeby th’ bloomin’ jura bucks gets
cunnin’, and’ goes a nosin’ round on
their own. One day, when they’d been
tearin’ up th’ weeds I sees the’ color o’
th’ yaller stuff, but before I could get
inter Coolgardie to sell th’ sheep th’
talent got the ‘ office ’ an’ rushed the
ground. I sold th’ sheep—a new
chum buys th’ lot. A couple o’ days
after that I was down at Bayley’s,
an’ I sees this cove humpin’ a lot o’
sheep’s ’eads in a bag. 4 Ullo ! ’ sez
I, 4 wot’s the game now ? 4 Oh,’ sez
the boss o’ th’ crushin’ machine, who
was standin’ by, 4 we’re crushin’
sheep’s ’eads for this ’ere gent, an’ an
all right sort o’ game it is, too.’
‘As how?’ says I. 4 As this way,’
sez ’e. 4 Them sheep was drivin’
across good gold-bearin’ country, an’
in rootin’ up th’ pig-weed they gits
their teeth filled with fine gold. Th’
last lot we crushed went soz. to th’
’ead. ‘Well, I’m d d!’ sez I.
‘Like enough,’ sez ’e,’ ‘anyone
oughter be d d fer lettin’ such a
good thing go by them.’ ”
• • * *
“ What I like about the ‘ Wild
Colonial Boy,’” says a well-known
commercial traveller, “is his don’t
care-a-damn-ness. Driving along up
north lately, I stopped at a cottage
door to inquire about the road ahead.
A small baby orawled out from some-
where and began to play 4 London
Bridge’ under my horse’s legs. The
horse didn’t mind it at first, but finally,
when the child had tangled itself up with
its hoofs a couple of times, began to look
round to see what was the matter. Then
it started stamping to shake the flies off,
and, getting scared, I called out to a ragged
10-year-old boy at the cottage door to take the
little one away before it got hurt. The lad never
budged an inch, but, swaying carelessly against
the wall, drawled out: 1 Take the blanky kid
away yuself. It don’t b’long to us !’ I took it
away.”

A Queensland publican has two daughters
—one of them a cripple ; and not long ago a young
fellow who was “struck” on the other sister
tackled the old man on the subject. Suitor : “ I
want to marry your daughter. Will you give her
tome?” Oldman: “I will, Jack. She’s a fine
girl is Sarah, and will make a good wife.”
Suitor: “ But it isn’t Sarah I want. She’s a
cripple !” Old Man : “ Hang it, man, what does
that matter ? You don’t want her for racing pur-
poses, do you ?”
AT THE SIGN OF THE “ KING’S HEAD”
SALE OF STAMPS AT HOTELS.
The Rev. J. H. Cain moved, at the recent sittings of the Methodist Conference, “That this
conference regrets to find that licenses for the sale of stamps have been issued to hotels licensed
for the sale of alcoholic liquors, and believing that such action is against the best interests of
the community, and will lead many into needless temptation, urges the Commonwealth postal
authorities to cancel as soon as possible those licenses already issued, and not to issue any more
in the future.”— Melbourne ARGUS.
MODERN METRODIST DANIEL : “ Young man, beware! This is no innocent pub
where they sell merely the harmless, necessary booze. In an unguarded
moment you may be tempted to buy the soul-damning postage stamp; and,
moreover, the female with whom you are now communing may be a — a — a — a
post-mistress.”
“ Glenara” : The Yic. cockie is a curious
animal. He will buy £2OO or £3OO worth of
machinery, but he won’t spend £lO on a shed to
cover it while it is idle. He will erect miles of
good substantial fence, but a couple of saplings
and a bit of acacia-brush have to serve for a gate.
He will practically ruin a £2O horse by feeding
him on straw ehaiff in order to save a few pounds’
worth of hay and oats. And lie will go to a sale
for the sake of the free drinks and buy a cart-load
of rubbish not worth carrying home.
MIS, ANNETTE KELLERMANN, AUSTRALIA’S
CHAMPION LADY SWIMMER.
Photo, by the Swiss Studios, Sydney and Melbourne.
In the 60’s, the Victorian Government
offered a reward for the discovery of a satisfactory
snake-poison antidote, and allowed dogs to be
experimented on in Melbourne gaol. A confident
swagman came along from Queensland and had a
trial. It was found that a bitten dog let alone
lived 45 minutes ; if the swagman’s antidote was
applied it died in 20 minutes. The nostrum was
compounded of decomposed snakes’ bodies !

It has been found by experience that the
N.S.W. coast farmers are too tired to go to the
State experimental farms to pick up wrinkles, so
the new plan is being tried of carrying the
wrinkles round to the farms—the officers are to
go on circuit and pump in information where they
can.

Be drought losses. In 1898 the Leichhardt
East'Marsupial Board (Dawson River, Q.) levied

year the number is under 43,000.

Australian far-
mer in common
hospitality. The
present genera-

Australians are
as sordid and

generous and
open - handed.
These remarks
are based on a
wide experience
gained before the
drought set in.
A more deplor-
able trait of the
average farmer
is hi 3 ignorance
and filthy habits.
In very few

a bath be found,
such a thing
being regarded
by the cockie as
one of the super-
fluous luxuries of
the “ toff.” This
remark applies
even to localities
where water is
abundant. I was
once sitting in a
farmer’s eating-
room. It was a
hot evening, and
all were perspir-

what a smell!” remarked pater-familias, jocu-
larly. Said a girl of 16, “It’s Annie—she’s jest

she heard the remark quite unconcernedly.

A Rockhampton (Q.) authority, speaking
on the question of water supply, estimates that
there are 200,000 dead cattle in the Fitzroy River.
That doesn’t make the Fitzroy much of a prospect
to fall back upon in the event of a water shortage
whatever it may be worth as soup.

Spondulix : Your theories about the inevitableness of the
N.S.W. public debt are sheer rot. N.S.W. has up to now
received a trifle over £50,000,000 of land revenue. If the
State had been content to pay the ordinary expenses of
Government out of taxation, as quite a number of coun-
tries do, that money would have supplied the means to
build all the State-owned public works now existing or to
extinguish the debt incurred in building them. The
revenue derived from public works (which wouldn’t then
be absorbed by interest) would be in the nature of com-
pound interest on this £50,000,000, and this compound
interest process would have supplied a sum equal to the
whole present debt. A long-continued disinclination to
pay taxes was all the trouble Dugald Mac. : “ Man-

Eastern : That dictionary definition is absurdly inade-

“Top”: Have been told by a skipper of
20 years’ coasting experience that the Cardinal’s
Palace at Manly (Sydney), when lighted up at
night, is a considerable source of danger to
mariners marking for Port Jackson. The South
Head light does not show far on a drizzly or hazy
night, which makes it necessary to keep near the
land. On such nights bands or strata of haze, of
varying density, are continually passing, the light
from his Eminence’s humble but cheerful home
showing at intervals between these successive
bands, and giving the impression of a revolving
light; also the tower looks not unlike a lighthouse.
One night not long ago the haze lifted just in time
to prevent my captain running his vessel ashore
beneath the Cardinal’s modest and secluded
humpy.
Miss Annette Kellermann, now appearing
at Melbourne Aquarium as a mermaid in a tank,
may take credit for having introduced swimming-
culture to the local feminines. In Sydney she had
the title of champion lady swimmer and diver; in
Melbourne, where the ordinary girls’ notion of
water-exercises is confined to “bobbing up and
down like this,” Miss K. begins to be known as a
revelation. She gave her first show at Brighton
some three months ago, and since then she has
astonished the natives with her performances at
sundry aquatic carnivals. Hence the engagement
of interesting Annette (and her small brother) by
the Melb. Exhibition trustees. The trustees are
supposed to constitute an Educational Entertain-
ment department, and the mermaid in the tank is
about the most attractive object lesson they have
ever submitted to notice during their long career
of un-usefulness.
Said that the Krupp (Germany) libel pro-
ceedings against the Berlin socialist paper
Vorwarts were dropped because its editor had
absolute proof from the Prussian income-tax
records that, during the last two years of his life,
Krupp had loaned the Kaiser 100,000,000 marks.
Another hard-up king.
The annual appeal now being made
throughout all parts of the Commonwealth for
the British and Foreign Bible Society, recalls the
fact that this society, now so keen after the
widow’s mite and the tradesmen’s copper, was
able to spend £2365 on an elaborately got-up and
bejewelled Bible to present to Edward VII. on
his Coronation last year. The writer used to
subscribe to this society, feeling that a few tracts
for the heathen might be beneficial, and at the
least might serve as curl-papers for Mrs.
Heathen’s hair, but, after this exhibition of ser-
vile extravagance, never again !
An obscure English vicar, Rev. W. Nixon,
of Robert Town, Yorkshire, has suddenly thrust
himself under the notice of a scandalized world
by writing, in his parish magazine, of the deified
Dr. Parker, of London City Temple, like this :
“ Dr. Parker is supposed, by most people, to have died
the death common to all men, but if we are to believe the
inscription that his admiring friends placed on his coffin
iio such thing as death took place in his case. The
inscription reads thus: ‘Joseph Parker, D.D., Minister
of the City Temple, born 9th April, 1830, ascended 28th
November, 1902.’ Henceforth, therefore, 28th November
will be a red-letter day in the calendar. It will be
marked as the day of the Ascension of Dr. Parker.
The Ascension of our Lord Jesus Christ will have a rival
in that of Dr. Parker. Indeed, Dr. Parker’s ascension
will, in a way, put Christ’s quite into the shade, because
whilst Christ both died and was buried, Dr. Parker
apparently escaped those degradations, and went straight
to heaven. What was put in the coffin, or what use
there was for a coffin at all in this case, or why even a
formal funeral service was gone through, we are at a loss
to know. The whole thing seems most absurd and
irreverent.”^
THE LATE REV. CHARLES CLARKE.
Photo, by Eden George, Sydney.
The late Charles Clarke was a handsome
apostle of 35 when he broke away from pulpit
restraints and took to lecturing around Austra-
lasia on his own. Perhaps he lived to regret that
R. S. Smythe had enticed him away from his
Baptist flock in Melbourne. Physically a strong
man, Rev. Clarke might have enjoyed a plump
stipend and all the comforts of salvation for
another 20 years had he laid strictly to his
Book. A parson of his constitution can endure
comfortably through a long old age; but Clarke,
the lecturer, was cut down by a stroke of
paralysis at 65.
“ Cripps Clarke ” has a theory :
Some good may result from the Victorian Lands Office
scandal if the moral thereof is applied. Since the woeful
day when I “ settled on the land ” under a sheet of bark,
much study of the land administration of N.S.W.
has made me a strong advocate for one or other of the
following innovations: (1) Minister for Lands to be a
stone-deaf, dumb and blind bachelor, with no relations,
and a salary of £lO,OOO per annum. (2) Frankly admit
the enormous possibilities of the billet by calling tenders
for the privilege of filling it; highest tenderer not to be
harrassed by Royal Commissions of Inquiry. (3) Run
the show with three commissioners, free of political
influence. It is more difficult to “ approach ” three men
than one, and to lessen the chance of the commissioners
agreeing too readily to extensions of leases, exchanges,
withdrawals from selection and other blocks to settle-
ment, the trio should be carefully assorted. A promising
start might be made by the appointment, say, of Cardinal
Moran, Dill Macky and Jos. Symes ; then, whatever per-
suasion an applicant belonged to, his application would
stand a fair chance of being dealt with solely upon its
merits. Any little unpleasantness that might arise
during discussion could be smoothed out by the appoint-
ment of John Norton as honorary referee, with power to
swear at all three Ministers.

At Poverty Point.
WHAT IS COMING .
The deputation asks N.S.W. Premier See to have the grass and trees around Sydney Harbor
burnt, and the rocks dynamited every week , so that the sheltered spots where man now sinfully
hugs his (or the other man's) best girl may no longer lead the Sunday public astray.
To call Charles Frohman the greatest theatrical man-
ager that ever lived is far from big enough. Gus Harris
and Edwardes are minnows to him. He leases eleven
New York theatres, with seventy in other parts of
America, and four in London, besides being con-
cerned in enterprises in Paris and Berlin. Further,
he is the head of the combine of all the principal
American managers. This colossus is a little clean-
shaven man. He never was an actor, but has the
judgment of an Augustin Daly in picking plays and
supervising rehearsals. Frohman began without any
capital. He was the treasurer of Haverly’s minstrel
company, in America and London. Then he launched a
New York theatrical agency. His next step was plank-
ing his bottom dollar on a play named “ Shenandoah,”
which made a hit.
“ Cockaigne ” : Composer Planquette was at starvation
point when he made his hit with “ Cloches de Corneville.”
Produced at the Folies Dramatiques it was a dismal frost.
Then it suddenly caught on at Bordeaux. Revived at
the Folies it ran a year. The London production (1878)
was under the most depressing auspices. As with
“Genevieve de Brabant,” all hands predicted failure.
The last rehearsal was an awful smudge. Shiel Barry, as
Gaspard, could not emit a scrap of voice. His over-
whelming success in the performance is historical. It
was for him as Jefferson’s “ Rip Van Winkle,” Sothern’s
“Dundreary,” Emmet’s “ Fritz,” or Jennie Lee’s “ Jo.”
“J.F.D.”: Waiting for some reverend howler to open
up a crusade against the mutoscope. In “ Sylvia un-
dressing,” “ Peeping Tom,” “ Who owned the Corset,”
etc., the spectator who has invested a penny is just
thinking he is going to see something shocking when the
light goes, and the penny, which has been acting as a
joint in the circuit, falls down into the cash box with a
sound like a fat chuckle. “ Great is the art of finishing.”
Why doesn’t some parson arise and denounce the machine
for leaving off in this villainous manner ?
Under the heading “Bullet Catching” in Weekly
Telegraph (London), 24/l/’O3, appears the following: —
“ A more tragic case was that of a murder brought about
by the bullet trick. It occurred about 15 years ago in a
then unsettle)! portion of Australia. A conjuror was
announced to give an entertainment one evening, the
great turn being the bullet trick. The bullet was selected
and marked by one of the rough men of the audience,
dropped into the pistol, and extracted by the ramrod in
the usual way. The audience waited breathlessly while
one of their number, a dead shot, took aim and fired.
When the smoke cleared away the magician was seen
smiling, with the marked bullet between his teeth. Just
as he was bowing in acknowledgment of the applause a
man jumped on the stage and pointed a pistol at the con-
juror’s head, ‘lf you are so good at catching bullets,
catch this,’ he cried as he pulled the trigger. The man
fell dead.” [This happened in “an unsettled part of
Australia,” so it ought to be quite easy to locate the
episode.]
“ N.”: The lately-deceased Augustus Hare, in his prosy
Autobiography, says that there have been painful and
incredible circumstances in his life, but forbears to dig
them up. The English newspapers are now doing so.
He had a brother Francis, and a sister Esmeralda, the
heroine of About’s romance of “ Tolla,” which is founded
on her being cruelly jilted by a young member of the
Italian nobility. Hare’s widowed mother was very rich,
and lived in Rome. Her brother was the swindler Sir
John Dean Paul, who robbed her of half her fortune.
She was robbed of the other half by Parker, her London
solicitor. This blow broke her heart, and she died.
Esmeralda, however, recovered £20,000 of the money.
Then brother Francis, a scallywag, came on the scene.
Mrs. and Miss Hare had become devout Catholics, and
Francis pretended conversion also. He had dissipated a
large fortune of his own, but Esmeralda trusted him, and
he made away with all her £20,000. _ Within about a year
of getting it she died, under suspicious circumstances,
that looked like poisoning. She said to a nun who
attended her, “ Francis has been the death of me.
Augustus Hare made statements to the effect that his
brother had killed his sister. Francis thereupon com-
menced a libel action against him, and it came into
Court, at Guildford. The Judge refused to try it. A
settlement was effected, on Augustus explaining that he
only alluded to Francis’ bad investments. It is supposed
that Esmeralda poisoned herself. All the parties to the
affair had been dead a long time before Augustus passed
away. Sir John Dean Paul married his gaoler’s daughter.
“ J.A.C.” : It is stated, on excellent authority, that
Signor Buzzi, once well-known here in Italian opera, has
abandoned “the pomps and vanities of this wicked
world,” and joined the Carthusian Brotherhood in Rome.
Little G. D. Meudell, the figure merchant who used to
help “ lead ” the great Vie. agitation for cutting down
public servants’ screws, lately devoted his evenings
to the harmless pleasure of trying to tangle up calcu-
lator Inaudi. After spending a day evolving a beautiful
problem, he would cart his friends round to Melb. Opera
House, and fire off the puzzler. When Inaudi came out pat
with his answer, the ex-reformer would exclaim, “ You’re
quite right, sir,” and clap hysterically. Then the gallery
boys gazed down in admiration at Meudell, and concluded
that he was Treasurer Turner with his “ mo.” dyed.
“ J.A.C.” : “ S.T.S.” (Bulletin 28/3/’O3) has courteously
undertaken to put me right re my statement that the
late Kate Vaughan was “ without a rival in her pourtrayal
of the London street-arab.” I may have been wrong, but,
if so, then so was the entire London press. Her “con-
stant associate, Nellie Farren,” eminent though she was
as a Priestess of the Sacred Lamp of Burlesque, had to
play second fiddle to K.V. in that respect. Back in the
’7o’s I had numerous opportunities of witnessing the per-
formances of both these ladies, and I am not “ talking
through my hat.” Further, “S.T.S.” remarks:—“The
dainty K.V. could not have looked like a street boy to
save her life.” It is not necessary for one to “ look like
a street boy ” to give a burlesque imitation of one.
Possibly “ S.T.S.” has never heard of the gentleman who
gave an imitation of a hard-boiled egg, although he did
not at all resemble that vegetable.
“ Melb.” : If you want something new, go and see Miss
Kellermann, at the Melbourne Aquarium. I have just
been, and had no idea that aught so daring could be
attempted- A finely-developed woman, she disdains
tights, except a very close-fitting black body shape.
Miss K. is a rich mahogany brown, through her habit
of classic sea-bathing. She is not as hard as nails, but
all splendid muscle. First she gave a diving and swim-
ming show in water the level of the floor, then a remark-
able exhibition in a glass tank, 60ft. by 20ft., within
a few inches of the spectators. No wet brown girl that
I have ever before seen impressed me so much.
Madame Adelina Patti was 60 years of age on Feb. 10th.
In Melbourne General Cemetery, in close proximity to
a number of Thespians, there is a marble memorial over
the grave of Ellen Mortyn which bears the following in-
scription : “ Here lie the mortal remains of the gifted
Ellen Mortyn. This simple tribute to her worth was sub-
scribed for at a public benefit in the Royal Princess’
Theatre, Melbourne, by the Amateur Benevolent Comedian
Club, August 31,1861. J. M. Forde, Honorary Secretary.”
The memorial cost £5O.
Wilson Barrett and Hall Caine have been waging a
newspaper correspondence or controversy concerning a
second dramatisation of “The Manxman,” which was not
anything of an improvement on the play that Barrett
staged in Australia. The controversy brought no satis-
faction to Hall Caine, but it served as a capital advertise-
ment for Barrett. Perhaps they are business partners
quarrelling in the interests of the firm.
The various U.S. religious denominations propose to
unite to reproduce the Holy City in all important
details on a 10-acre plot at St. Louis' World’s Fair of 1904.
The cost is estimated at £445,000, and the profits, from
sale of stock, &c,, at £945,000. Apart from the fact that
you can’t get much of a Jerusalem into 10 acres, or build
much Jerusalem for £445,000, there is something pathetic
about the hope of the religious denominations that they
may make £500,000 beer-money by guying the birthplace
of their faith.
“The man they cannot hang,” was the sensation of an
English touring circus for many months. Daniel
Lambert, the man in question, used to be “turned
off,” taking a 15ft. drop every night, and people
crowded to see the performance. They were at last
satisfied; one night Lambert failed to catch the noose
with his hand as he fell and the man was banged in
earnest. Probably the audience was then happy.
“ J.F.D.” : Re calculating feats. Knew a man who had
a calculating crow. Taught him by tying a piece of cotton
round his leg, and making him nod his head every time
the string was pulled. The crow picked up the nodding
business in about three months, and the owner exhibited
him as a calculator, the crow doing (apparently) multi-
plication and division sums by nodding his head, each nod
representing one. The owner pulled the cotton, which
travelled from his trousers pocket down the leg of his
trousers along the floor to the crow’s perch. “ Had a bigj
crowd one night,” he said, “ and fairly beat em, adding up;
seven columns of figures. Started him on another. FirstS
figure two ; I gave two pulls, but he wouldn’t nod.|
Another two, no nod. Then he grabbed the cotton in hist
beak and gave the whole show away. Found when II
examined him afterwards that the cotton had slipped!
into a cut on his leg. No wonder he struck.” I
The noble Earl of Yarmouth, who gained notoriety out
here by his remarkable exhibition of skirt-dancing, is
about to marry an American heiress, a Miss Alice Thaw.
He is himself a frost. A “courtesy” earl (really much
the same as a commoner) who is hard-up and wears
skirts, and takes his more or less bogus title from a town
that is full of red herrings, isn’t much of a catch.
“ J. A. 0.” : J. H. Edwards, the victim of the lamentable
tramway accident on 24th March last, was employed by
Bland Holt as what might be termed equestrian director,
and trainer of the various animals employed by B.H. in his
productions. Edwards was a fearless horseman, and had
in his time been a Master of Fox-hounds. In one of
Bland’s military screams Edwards had to gallop a horse
up a steep “ rake,” then across a bridge into the “ flies,”
a height of about 30ft. When he reached the top of the
“rake” one night the horse stumbled,and both rolled
down together to the stage. That the rider escaped being
crushed was miraculous but Edwards just mounted and
rode up again. The audience took it for part of the show,
and when he didn’t roll down with his horse next night it
had a half inclination to demand its money back.
The story of a Maoriland warrior :
George Whitmore, M.L.O. and Major-General, who
died in Napier (M.L.), March 16th, was a man who
lived a stirring life in the wild times of empire-
building. He was born in the island fortress of Malta,
away back in 1829, while his father was a major in the
Royal Engineers. The year 1846 found him fighting
Kaffirs in South Africa, and two years later the sharp,
little, rat-like officer was with Harry Smith when the
Boer laager was burst at Boomplats, and he helped to
chase President Pretorius for nearly 20 miles after the
Boer rout. More Kaffir wars followed, and Whitmore
was in almost every battle. Thence he went to the
(\rimea, and served under both British and Turkish flags,
and when there was no more fighting in Europe he went
on V> India. He came to Maoriland as military secretary
to General Duncan Cameron, who was in command of the
Imperial troops who were suppressing a Maori “rebellion.”
A year later he went on the land in the Hawkes Bay dis-
trict, wnhfe his home has been ever since. A band of
Hau-haus yame down by Petane and skirted round
Napier, andNnally took up a position in a fortified pah at
Omaranui. TNere they were attacked at day-break by
Whitmore with 200 militia, and what were not shot were
captured. Whitmore was the first to scramble over the
palisade, and saw Hie wife of the fighting chief belaboring
him with a rail beisause he wouldn’t get up and fight.
Later on that chief was found to have 40 odd bullet holes
through him, and the bellicose wahine fell down and wept
when she found she had been walloping a dead husband.
About the time the Franco-Prussian war was commencing
W’hitmore was chasing Te Kooti over the hills and down
the gullies in the most break-neck hunt ever Maoriland
saw ; butTe Kooti was a great goer and kept in front.
In January, 1869, Whitmore stormed Te Kooti’s fortress
at Ngatipa; it was situated on a wooded hill 2500 feet
high, and was taken after a five days siege. As usual Te
Kooti got away, and Colonel Whitmore went back to the
West Coast and let out his disgust on Tito Kowaru’s
crowd, which had been doing pretty well as it liked
between Kai Iwi and Waitara. By this time Te Kooti was
once more on the riot in the Uriwera Hills, and the little
Colonel came back to have another go at him, but just as
the same old hunt was commencing there was a change
of Ministry, and the Fox Government at once removed
the Colonel from his command. He was Colonial Secre-
tary under Premier George Grey, whose Ministry lasted
two years—lB77-79. Later on he was a member of the
transitory Stout-Vogel Ministry which lasted 12 days
in August, 1884. When the Infidel and the Jew came
back to power in September, 1884 (Atkinson’s five-days
Government intervening between the two Stout-Vogel
Governments) Whitmore became Commander of the
Forces and Commissioner of the Armed Constabulary,
and was promoted to the rank of Major-General. He was
a member of the Legislative Council from 1863 till his
death a few weeks ago. He tried his hand at many
things, including matrimony, but at none of them was he
so successful as in the art of war. Ilis business enter-
prises were unsuccessful, and his marriage, sad to say,
unhappy. About three years ago he suffered a paralytic
stroke. He only returned from England, where he bad
gone to publish a history of the Maori Wars, about a
month before his death.
“ J.F.D.” : Had a case at a suburban
court, a few weeks back, where I wished to obtain
an order for possession of premises occupied by a
“lydy” of loose virtue. The case was called,
but no defendant appearing the magistrate put it
back till the afternoon. Called again (no defend-
ant), he postponed it till the next day. Next day
(no defendant) he refused to make an order be-
cause I had not told the “lydy” the case was
postponed, and ordered me through his clerk to
acquaint her and endeavor to bring her to court
next morning. I went down and tried to cajole
her up, and she promised to attend. The beak’s
typewriter next morning (lady not putting in an
appearance) informed me that his Wash up could
not make an order without the appearance of de-
fendant, he having postponed the case when there
was no appearance of defendant in the first in-
stance. Next day I gave her five shillings to
come to court, and on her consenting to go in
seven days I got an order. But what is the legal
method of ejecting a lydy of loose character ?
