The visit of Admiral Fanshawe’s squadron
of neutral-tinted battle-ships to Maoriland re-
sulted in the usual quantity of heart-burn and
abject toadying. The man who gave the Admira’
the list of notables in Wellington was evidently a
humorist. F instance, the Mayor and some of
the councillors were invited to a function on the
flagship ; but the balance of the city fathers were
overlooked. The Times staff went abroad, even
to the printer’s devil; while the Evening Post
staff couldn’t raise an “invite” amongst them.
Consequence—the Post gravely informed its
readers that a children’s party was proceeding on
His Majesty’s man-o’-war. Dick Seddon sailed
out in his private steamer (Maoriland really owns
it) and took with him any chance friend he met in
the street. These persons he introduced indivi-
dually to the Admiral to the mortification of some
of the Very Best People who were only guests of
the junior officers. And now the disappointed
spinsters of Wellington look with hungry eyes on
the lonely harbor and wonder why there isn’t a
hole in it where the squadron was.

New Australian verb—“to bulwinkle” the
Customs. A relation of the old Queensland verb
“ to bulcook” the electoral rolls.
The new Tasmanian Assembly, so bad has
been the smash, contains only one man (Nicholas
Brown) who has had experience as a Minister,
and he, being Speaker, and aged 65, and none too
brilliant, scarcely counts.
The latest phase of co-operation is the
clubbing together of bunches of N.S.W. legis-
lators to employ and pay a common secretary.
“ Half-a-crown each would get us a good man,”
said the organiser of one bunch.

ruthers says of O’S. : “If you do, I’ll tell some-
thmg. O’S.: “I didn’t mean I would tell
anything.” Carruthers responds: “Then if he
doesn’t mean anything, I don’t mean anything
either.” J s ’

Fegan was taken into the See Cabinet principally

hit the ceiling.

Commonwealth Government proposed to take any
steps to disqualify or retire or eject or freeze out
alleged Senator Reid, was neat but not gaudy •
“It is hardly necessary for the Federal Govern-
ment to interfere when the probability is that the
ordinary results will accrue.”
The suicide of Mrs. White, at Brighton
(Vic.), last week, after 66 years of married life,
ought to be a solemn warning to Raven Shiels and
the rest of the Irvine-Bent gang to go slowly with
the reduction of the Old Age Pension. Had
Victoria been run by a decent, civilised Govern-
ment, the woman who is now dead would not have
been driven to such dreadful extremities. It
isn t necessary to cut anyone’s throat to be a mur-
derer.

year. The Mcßillion gang should look
at Canada, which, with about a million
or so more population, has
now 15 huge iron and steel
mills going, and is add-
ing to the number.
THE POINT OF INTEREST.
THE STRANGER (finishing his hair-raising anecdote): “At that very moment a thunderbolt
hurtled down and dashed the glass from my hand.”
BILIJIM (with awakened interest): “ Wa-was there any beer in it?”
Victoria has about six times as much rail-
way as it had in 1873. But in 1873 the list of killed
and injured consisted of one person (and he was
only injured); the year ended June, 1902, the
killed and injured numbered 878. For the last
few years the list has been mounting up gaily:
1895, 324 ; 1896, 361; 1897, 553 ; 1898, 451; 1899,
436 ; 1900, 576 ; 1901, 665; 1902, 878.
Queensland Premier Philp is very sure as
to his tenure of office. He told a deputation at
Rockhampton that he had no intention of
resigning or gomg to the country; if the electors
did not like his policy they could turn his party
out-at the next general elections That is two
years off and greater men than Philp and stronger
parties than his have disappeared in less than two
years.
There is much tribulation amongst big
landowners in Victoria over filling in their new
income-tax forms. Land assessed for land-tax at
20s. per acre won’t look well bringing in from 10s.
to 12s. 6d. an acre rent. Civil servants in the tax
department have a unique chance to raise Cain
and come out even with the reformers.

Fitzpatrick, and John Haynes are sure of port-

inkpot” will have more than a due share of the
billets. Carruthers himself was connected with a
little Rockdale paper some years ago when Bouci-
oault, of Gympie (Q.), tried to wake up the
suburbs.

been a shocking drought, he says, and Crick has

Sydney D. T. seems disposed to crawl
down on the White Australia question and adopt
the Argus view in regard to the kanaka. Extract
from leader, April Ist:
The mixed aliens cannot be dispersed and, being here,
as a matter of sensible precaution should be encouraged
to get work and attend to it. There remain the kanakas,
immeasurably the most useful of all the colored peoples
within our borders, who, by the irony of circumstances,
are the only section to be deported.
A correspondent from Maoriland :
I have read “Theata’s” contribution (B. 14/3/’O3) about
the squire blight in Victoria. We have the remnants of
the same disease in this allegedly democratic State. One
can see the trail of the squattocracy in many ways. For
instance, Balfour, Prime Minister of England, owns a
huge block of fine land somewhere in the Wairarapa, and
now that the Government talks of resuming it for closer
settlement, sundry contemptible newspapers whine about
“ driving capital out of the country,” etc.; and one penny
rag tells its readers that Balfour is a “ good landlord,”
because he has just sent out a cheque for £25 to help
build a church.
Some recent prosecutions of soft-goods
importers by the Customs are the result of infor-
mation supplied by a straight-going firm. A cer-
tain agent sought orders from Messrs. So-and-So
on the strength of his alleged success in getting
goods through the Customs on false descriptions.
He gave the names of other firms for whom he
had worked the swindle and who had thus been
able to undersell their honest rivals. Then So-
and-So, for their own protection, passed these
particulars along to the Department.

There have been lately, in Fremantle gaol,
under sentence of death, no less than seven
prisoners, and there is an eighth on the goldfields
—a record, it is believed, even for Westralia. And
there is not one of the eight who might not easily
have been shut out of the country under any
decently-administered law restricting undesirable
alien immigration.
At Moruya (N.S.W.) an old-age pensioner
sued for £lB5, money lent, and got a verdict. The
case is said to illustrate how the pension system is
abused. But it doesn t necessarily do anything
of the sort; it may merely illustrate how a man
may be driven to apply for an old-age pension
through the difficulty, as the law now stands, in
collecting ,£lB5 justly due to him.
Although the majority of the S.A. farmers
last season reaped a good harvest and obtained
high prices for their produce, there was a large
number who got no crops at all. For their
assistance £30,000 has been distributed, and 1310
cases were relieved. Next year they hope to pay
it all back. A similar hope has been in their
hearts season after season for eight or ten years,
but at the close of each season seed has had to be
asked for again.
Woodend (Vic.) wants a new post-office,
and a publican offers a free site next his beer-
pump and opposite the railway station, while a
land speculator wants to sell a “ more moral ” site
for £350. A public meeting resolved that the free
site be accepted. Thereupon two Federal poli-
ticians rushed to Melbourne to protest in the
interests of Boodle and the rights of land specu-
lators, and P.M.G. Drake, instead of throwing
them out, listened and promised a referendum.
The enforcing of the regulations regarding
temporary employment in N.S.W. public service
is producing a new class to be known as the nine-
months man. A temporary clerk is nowadays
only appointed for a
short term, and the
“temporary’’ hand who
lasts for 20 years in one
department will soon
be extinct. The term
of the appointment is
usually three months
with a quarterly re-
newal, but the Board
cannot approve of the
retention of the appoin-
tee for more than nine
consecutive months, and
a year has to elapse
before he can be re-
instated on the tem-
porary staff. Certain
men with a “claim on
the Govt.” get re-ap-
pointed again and
again. For nine months
they strut round full-
blown public servants
at 10s. a day. Then
they disappear for a
time. Punctually, when
their term of banish-
ment is up, they bob
up from goodness knows where, and a billet
is generally waiting in the Rabbit Protection
Department or the Rabbit Destruction Office
or the Rabbit Consideration Branch. What
they do in the interregnum no one knows (or asks).
But they can be depended upon to turn up and to
enjoy, like the remittance man, their period of
prosperity.
The Westralian jury’s vigorous kicking
against the Executive’s failure to spare the neck
of a man who had been recommended to mercy
availed at the last moment; the hanging has been
commuted to 20 years’ gaol. This particular
man’s life may not be worth much ; but the main-
tenance of the principle that a jury’s “ recommen-
dation ” should be taken absolutely as part of its
verdict is worth a good deal. But the jury
should be allowed to say not only that it recom-
mends mercy, but also how much mercy it recom-
mends.
In Victoria’s present hard-up period it is
a pleasant reflection that its selectors owe it over
£400,000 for arrears. This isn’t so much the poor,
embarrassed selector as the big landowner who has
frozen dozens of selectors out by purchase or fore-
closure of mortgage, and who truculently dares the
spineless Government to make him pay his just
debts. There are cases, for instance, of 30 selec-
tions being held by one man, who owes £2OOO or
so of arrears, and off-handedly tells the State to go
to Sheol. And instead of selling him up it goes
to Sheol.
During the discussion on the Railway
Commissioners Bill in the Victorian Assembly, it
was stated that the interest on the East Natimuk
to Noradjuha line amounted to £3 a day, and the
line earned 4s. Interest on the Beeac to Colac
line came to £5 a day, and the takings 12s. And
one station was instanced where the average daily
income was less than one shilling. This discus-
sion ought to be interesting reading to incoming
Commissioner Tait. With Tommy Bent and lines
like these to manage, his life is not going to be a
big joy.
N.S.W. Public Works Act provides that
no work, the cost of which is to exceed £20,000, is
to be undertaken unless favorably reported upon
by the Public Works Committee of Parliament.
The Act—intended to limit Ministerial power—
can be evaded by letting a work in sections, the
cost of none of which reaches £20,000. Car-
ruthers, when Minister for Education, built the
Technical College in that way; and Varney
Parkes —of the same political gang—the post-office
telephone tunnels. The N.S.W. Freetrade crowd
was always peculiar in its methods when in power.
During his Queensland tour Senator
Glassey pledged himself to advocate a weighty
absentee-tax (which is good) the proceeds to be set
aside to pay Federal Old Age Pensions (which is
also good). And then Senator Glassey might have
done some more good by pledging himself to a
heavy special tax or series of taxes, proceeds to go
into a Public Works Fund so as to make loans
unnecessary, which is even more essential than old
age pensions. But he didn’t. Glassey, in fact,
seems willing to be a cheerful borrower and help
to beggar posterity, so long as we have a good time
ourselves.
Balfour has a great opportunity now that
he is about to buy out the Irish landlords ; but, of
course, he’ll neglect it. Instead of selling the
land to the tenants, to be paid for in instalments,
why not make the State the landlord, leasing out
its acres ? His plan only staves off the evil day.

There will be few remonstrances if the
Barton Government take early steps to set up a
Federal Statistician s department. Strange as it
may appear, many of the fiscal bubbles that
politicians blow are generated in one or other
of the handbooks the States separately produce.
Different bases are adopted, and giddy super-
structures of argument are the consequence. On
a multitude of grounds the Commonwealth should
have its own Statistician.
Bent’s latest idea is to offer the scorned
railway weeding contracts—which nobody seems
able to do cheaper than the Department— to the
Victorian unemployed, in small sections, pre-
sumably at sweating rates. “They will make
more than they will lounging round the street
corners, at any rate,” says Bent. Apparently the
permanent-way men who usually do this work are
to lounge about the corners instead. Nobody
seems to have any idea of meeting the unemployed
difficulty by creating new work at making the
goods that are now imported. Every fool’s
remedy is to take Smith’s job from him and give
it to Jones.

A N.S.W. daily paper correspondent
points with unconscious satire the true position of
the Tax-Dodging crowd, in giving his idea of re-
form :
No borrowing.
Economy, reduction of members.
Reduction of taxation.
Alterations of the Federal bond in such a way as to
enable the Commonwealth to reduce their taxation.
No borrowing and less taxation make up a plat-
form that should attract any voter, yet it could be
improved upon. Why not add that a quart of
beer should henceforth be a gallon, and be sold at
the price of a pint, with an increase in brewers’
profits.
That quaint N.S.W. politician Eden
George has addressed to Premier See a letter
weighing about in which he casts off his
allegiance. In the beginning of the letter he
speaks of large State matters, and reproaches See
for his extravagance and ineptitude, and then
slides into a bitter curse about a friend or
acquaintance whose property was resumed and
his rent allegedly raised £1 per week—though the
Ministerial reply makes it look much more as if
the friend’s rent was reduced. Apparently Mr.
George stood everything till his friend’s rent was
in question. Then the camel snapped in the
middle, carrot-wise.
During the last five months' (since the
abrupt close of last session made the thing pos-
sible) there have been 4000 garnishee orders in the
Victorian Railway Department. Commissioner
Fitzpatrick and Accountant Kent declare that
over 90 per cent, of these are got by money-
lenders— “ the tradesmen don’t garnishee.” Bent
still hangs back with his departmental loan bank
scheme, “lest some thriftless, betting employees
should take advantage of it.” A Usury Bill, such
as was introduced by Isaacs, M.P., long ago,
would put a stop to this bloodsucking, but that is
the sort of legislation the “House of Review”
always ponders over for several years.
Under the Q. Divisional Boards Act
owners and occupiers of land who have paid their
rates not later than seven days before the date of
nomination are entitled to have their names on
the voters’ list for the election. Bishop O’Quinn
died 20 years ago, and his name still appears on a
Brisbane suburban voters’ list. The other day
Judge Real wanted to know how the dead ecole-
aiastic contrived to pay his rates seven days before
nomination day and how the local authority
managed to serve him with the rate-notice. In
Queensland a property owner can remain on a
voters’ list 20 years after his death. An unpro-
pertied citizen can scarcely keep on the electoral
roll while he is alive.
The Bulletin (7/2/’O3), when there was
a sound of loud timbrels over alleged Senator
Reid’s election:
There is practical unanimity among Men who Have and
a very-widely-prevailing notion among Men who Have
Not, and can never hope to Have, that the work of
governing the country is in good hands when a wealthy
draper or a more than ordinarily predatory stock-exchange
speculator condescends to go into politics. Such men, it
is reasoned, are likely to do well for the community,
because they have done well for themselves at the expense
of the community. But—apart from the fact that it is
only when tired of doing well for himself and anxious to
gratify some social or other vanity that the rich man u
willing to go into politics with the dregs of his brain-
power—is a graduation at the school of predatory
commercialism the best preparation for public life ?
And that was before Robert Reid’s commercialism

from Anaemia, Weakness, and Debility. I had two

of age, and tried everything that I thought would do her

of mine who was taking Webber’s VITADATIO for
Hydatids advised me to give her the same medicine,
which I did, and after a course of VITADATIO (which I

Launceston, the northern capital of the
politically-dull Flyspeck, astonished the mainland
Laborites who have been across helping the local
men. Among the things which the city owns and
runs are electric lighting works, abattoirs, water-
works, and baths; and it helps its municipal
workmen to get homes of their own, and provides
them with an old-age pension when they can t
look after the dust-cart any more.
“ Alba ” on heroes :
What egotists these generals are ! Napoleon wept over
his own domestic troubles, but dined heavily within sight
of the shambles at Jena. Probably Caesar was violently
affected when his wife’s corn was bad. Years ago writer
was very close to Kitchener at Aldershot, and in his eyes
were the shadows of two continents ; but when he ripped
out an order his jaw moved like the door of a pawnshop
safe. But some day he may weep, and then the gods will
lean from the beer-bars in heaven and listen to his tears
falling. Although differing from Kitchener in many
respects yet the late Hector Macdonald was of the sharp,
semi-brutal stuff that goes with the first-class fighter.
With Kitchener for his chief he smiled upon the horrors
of the River War. To be truthful, nothing disturbed him
or Kitchener—wine, the tears of women, the burning of
homesteads, the crying of little children, were all ob-
served with soldierly indifference. And yet there are
times when a tear may move like a mountain from a
man’s eye. When Lb Matin, with its bitter disregard for
heroes, stoned Hector Macdonald for his alleged sin, he
permitted his blinding tears to engulph him—tears for
himself. Such splendid egotism is grief in plaster of
Paris ; it is imperishable until it gets wet.

Sydney Telegraph on the British Secre-
tary for War:
Mr. Brodrick is a type of Minister not well known to
Australians. He comes of the “governing class,” and
he became a Minister and remains a Minister by virtue of
the support of the “ governing classes.” He is an
indifferent speaker, an unattractive personality, and a
bad administrator. These three disqualifications would
make short work of him in an Australian Legislature.
Here he will have to go eventually as a member of the
Ministry, but only to be translated to another and equally
important position, say, as Viceroy of India. In any case,
he will go to the House of Lords, where most of the
political failures go. . . . In any self-governing colony
Mr. Brodrick would have had a short political shrift. His
survival in political life in England is solely due to the
fact that he is the nephew of Somebody, the brother of
Somebody, and the son-in-law of Somebody. All these
Somebodies are representative of the classes, who deem
it to be their pre-emptive right to govern the masses.
The worst of it is, the masses think so, too.

JAYNE’S EXPECTORANT
CURES THE. WORSTCOUGHJ.
Business, Robbery, etc.
“Petramos ” : A friend in the business telis me
that the credence property buyers in S.A. place in the
prophecies of weather prophet Giddons is extraordinary,
A man comes along presumably with the intention of
buying an orchard “ somewhere near the city.” After a
bit he thinks that after all it’s not quite what he wants.
Gradually he works round and says—“ I suppose you
haven’t got anything like a small farm somewhere about
the middle North ?” Fancy anyone, this time last year,
wanting to buy a farm in the middle North of S.A.
• * * •
“ J.F.D.” : Another item on the “ struggle for
a crust” calendar. Recently applied for a position
advertised in S.M. Herald, and three days afterwards I
was sent for by the advertiser. He informed me that he
had one hundred and thirteen applications for the posi-
tion. I evidently passed inspection, as he detailed my
duties, and, as a final remark, informed me the wages
would be £1 ss. per week. I accepted and gripped tne
lever. It was a change-stall and tobacconist’s business
combined, and I opened up at 5 a.m., receiving assist-
ance from 7to 9. I ran it till 2 p.m., when I was
relieved, but only to come back and relieve my assistant
between 5 and 7 p.m., when the rush was great. Sun-
days the same, and holidays were unknown —in fact, on
those occasions, the work was doubled. As I was respon-
sible for the cash, I had on my own account to slip back
on Saturday nights and help during a few hours’ rush.
After three weeks of it I left. I figured out that I had
worked 80 hours per week, which averaged 3Jd. per hour.
1 thought my abilities were thrown away at that, so I
took a turn at selling wild-rabbit.
* • « •
Successful London action againsta Yankee assess-
ment insurance ofiice to recover premiums paid on a policy
—the “ proposal ’’ for which was gained by tricky and mis-
leading statements on the part of the canvasser—recalls
an Australian incident of a little while ago, when a
smart eanvasser obtained in Sydney and Melbourne
several big “ proposals,” hooking his victims with the
bait of a local directorship or something of that sort. In
some cases the premiums were recovered by actions at
law. The too-clever canvasser afterwards went to
London, calculating that he was wasting his talents in
such a punctilious country as this.
* * » *
Victoria’s ■ rabbit-export trade rapidly ap-
proaches a “ great natural industry.” Trappers are now
forming an association 5000 strong ; butter-export firms
are making that staple a secondary item, bunny coming
first; last year Victoria sent away 6,000,000 rabbits ; this
year the total should be at least 10,000,000. Already the
landowner, as usual, begins to take the cream of the
profits, many trappers now paying heavily for the right
to trap over lands where, a few years back, the owners
had to pay to keep bunny down. Possibly, in another
couple of decades sheep-breeding will have been displaced
by bunny-rearing; and the Britisher of the future, living
mostly on rabbits, will squat on his haunches and wear
cocked ears and a scared expression.
• • • •
From the Perth R.C. Bishop’s “Lenten
Pastoral,” amid the notices as to days when his flock
must not eat meat:
“ For a considerable time it has been a matter of great
solicitude and anxiety to Us to have none but the purest
and most reliable wines used in the Holy Sacrifice of the
Mass. It is, therefore, a great relief to Our anxiety to be
able to recommend Santa Rosa Wines as pure and reliable
for the use of the Altar, and, for the preservation of uni-
formity, We hereby notify the Venerable Clergy that no
other wine has episcopal sanction for use in the Holy
Sacrifice of the Mass throughout this Diocese. —M. Gib-
net, Bishop of Perth. The Palace, Perth, 21st February,
1903."
Which beats anything The B. has ever yet heard of, even
from Yankeeland, in the way of advertisement.
* • • •
Isaac paid £5 to see an old joke over again
t’other day. A man was in the habit of pawning a
diamond ring with the hooked-nose one regularly about
the middle of the month, and redeeming at the Ist. This
went on until the ring became an institution. This
month the pawner was very stiff, and got an extra £1 on
the ring. Not coming as usual at first of month to
redeem it, Isaac became suspicious and tested the stone.
It was only paste. He had had the jewel through his
hands so often that he did not bother to test it that time.
Thus it is that the Hebrew who goes too often to the well
is broken at last.

Writes “Victorian Country S.M., P.M., L.T.0.,
Dog Man,” etc. Income Tax in Victoria is only a farce
and is evaded wholesale by our wealthiest class, the well-
to-do farmers. As an instance: My town boasts three
pubs, one taxed ; three stores, one taxed ; and scores of
wealthy farmers within three miles who have had splendid
seasons, record crops and best prices known of for years.
And how many income-tax forms do you think I issued
last year? Well, only three. Yet mine is the only office
for miles round where income-tax forms could be
obtained. One particularly well-off cockie, a J.P. etc.,
sold, to my personal knowledge, close on £6OO worth of
produce in six months, and he obtained from me an
exempt form declaring his taxable income to be nil. I
know what I write, as all his produce passed through my
hands. I also know prices ruling at'the time. I am an
officer at £146 per year, with wife and five kids, and have
to part up my pound of flesh to the Income lax, while
around me are scores of bloated humbugs who lf
things don’t soon alter in Victoria there will be GORE.
“ Junior Clerk ” writes :
All things considered, the civil-servant of the future
Should be a somewhat useful creature. A new regulation
made by N.S.W. Public Service Board provides test exam-
inations for promotion to the higher grades of the clerical
division. The curriculum of study includes public finance
and accounts, land laws and administration, the princi-
ples of national education, law and the administration of
justice, mining and agriculture, State charities, asylums,
old-age pensions, industrial legislation, principles of
commerce and commercial law, history and law of the
constitution, political economy and economic history,
international law and diplomacy, actuarial science and
theory of statistics, English literature, expansion of the
British Empire (of course), modern history, political
science and history of political theones soc o ogy,
French, German, law and mathematics (the latter
ing logarithms, trigonometry, analytical geometry, differ-
ential and integral calculus, statics and dynamics).
Altogether about 196 text-books, numerous Actsi of Par-
liament (as well as books required for certain unn ersitv
coursesmid the bar examinations) are recommended for
study what is the use of it all so far as concerns
three-fourths of New South Wales. The cm -servant
out in the Far West should be examined in asylums, old-
age pensions, dust-storms, artesian wells and bad whisky.
Other knowledge is superfluous.
The Most Valuable Diamonds.
It is difficult to account for the great price of
diamonds. To be sure, they are beautiful stones ;
but their virtue lies entirely in their power to
ornament. All but about one in every fifty
thousand of us live our lives without ever owning
a diamond, and don’t seem much the worse for
the privation. But ’tis not so with diamonds of
anotner sort —black diamonds, coals. Stop his
coal supply the man becomes paralysed—his
steamers lie idly in the harbour, his factory wheels
cease to revolve, he starves, shivers, and soon
becomes an object of pity. Take every other sort
of diamond out of the world, you will wound much
vanity and extinguish a very small industry. The
difference is great. Black diamonds for us, and
plenty of them, we say. The pity of it is that
they cannot be obtained under conditions more
healthful. “ But every trade has its drawbacks
and disadvantages, ” says Mr. Joseph Watson, of
Harris-street, Glebe, near Newcastle, N.S.W.,
when writing on September 26th, 1902. “Pit
asthma, or miners’ complaint, as it is often called,
is undoubtedly the worst evil peculiar to my
work, coal mining, and few diseases are responsible
for more suffering. But for Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup it would long ago have ended my
career. Pit asthma is an affection of the lungs
brought on through breathing the vitiated air in
pit working, which is moist and hot. Strangely
enough, those miners who are most robust and
healthy are the very ones most prone to contract
this disease. I have worked for more than
half a century in various collieries, both in the
‘old country ’ (of which I am a native) and here in
Australia; and frequently in the pits, where
danger is always present, I have seen comrades
killed, crushed, or crippled. However, it has
always been my good fortune to escape injury.
But some years ago I fell a victim to pit asthma.
I spent money liberally on doctors and medicines ;
but the complaint increased upon me, and
frequently I was unable to work for days together.
Violent paroxysms of coughing would rack my
chest until I was quite exhausted. Sometimes 1
would feel a little better, but oftener a great deal
worse. While in this state I was recommended to
try Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup. I was not
very sanguine about it, as I had suffered so many
disappointments in respect of other medicines.
But the first few doses distinctly benefited me
My complaint had a firm hold of me, and it took
several bottles to cure me ; but the Syrup did cun
me and an occasional dose keeps me in good run
ning order.”
THE GOLD THAT IS IN THE REPORT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There are fairy tales borrowed from Mammon,
That tell us of magical gold,
Unearthed by the witchcraft and gammon
They practised in ages of old ;
But visions of wealth in a coffer
Are nothing to wealth of the sort
That a golden prospectus may offer—
That gold that is in the report.
The lamp of Aladdin was mighty,
But “over an ounce to the ton,”
In paragraphs airy and flighty,
Looks solid and easily won.
We learn there’s a leader to follow,
And stuff to put through the retort,
And in “ grand indications ” we wallow,
The ones that are in the report.
Another wild cat—does it matter ?
A picturesque lie has its worth,
And luck may the biggest fool flatter,
And gild the round rim of the earth.
So down to the office we travel
To pay up the calls that are short.
O, if only the rock and the gravel
Had the wealth that is in the report 1
M. O'M.

BUCHANAN WHISKY.
A Wet Skin Caused Trouble.
How often have we heard it said, “Take off
your wet clothes, or you will catch your death of
cold.” That is all very well if one is at home, and
close handy to something dry to put on. Mr.
John A. Phypers, of 173, Pitt-street, Redfern,
Sydney, was far from home, when one day he was
drenched to the skin. In this condition he had
to remain all day. The consequence was he had a
frightful attack of influenza, which left him with
rheumatic fever before it had done with him. “ I
was under the doctor for months,” he says. “He
eased my condition for a time, but the old
rheumatic pains came back, and for five years I
suffered such agony that the pain nearly drove me
mad. Everything I thought of I tried. The
affected parts were rubbed with oils and liniments,
poultices were applied, and I dosed myself with
various drugs and medicines, but all to no purpose.
In fact I seemed to be growing worse. At last I
heard of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills. Before
I had taken a single bottle the rheumatic pains
began to leave me. I continued with them and I
was soon able to be up and about. The pains
became less acute, until they finally vanished
altogether. Besides completely curing me of
rheumatism, Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills have
thoroughly improved my general condition, and
to-day I am in better health than I have ever
been. ’’ Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are a perfect
blood purifier, and a positive cure for biliousness,
indigestion, constipation, liver and kidney
troubles, piles pimples and blotches, and for
female ailments. Put up in amber bottles and
he full name bio wn thereon.*

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM! TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance
is solicited
The lath and plaster pavilion at Centennial
Park (Sydney), wherein the Australian Common-
wealth formally commenced its existence, is now
on its last wobble, and threatens to topple over at
any moment. . The structure, erected temporarily
for a temporary purpose, with a hazy idea of re-
producing it in more lasting material later on,
has been neglected till it is now about to give up
the ghost. From its associations, the weather-
beaten wreck should find a warm corner in the
memory of all patriotic Australians, but the pro-
posal to make a stone or brick duplicate of it,
and to try and shift the emotions and associations
from the original place to the duplicate, is pain-
fully thin and artificial.

BREWERY and OFFICES: DOWLING ST., REDFERN, SYDNEY.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Clarendon Handicap at Hawkesbury, Satur-
day, was a complete boil-over and gave Mo an absolute
“ skinner." Those who saw Heyday win at Newcastle
earlier in the week voted him a “ moral ” ; it was only a
matter of putting coin down and picking it up again with
the odds added, they said. Heyday started 6 to 4 against,
and others weightily invested upon were Capriform,
Skein Dhu, Hello, and W.F. Unthought-of Lady Grafton
(unbacked by owner or anyone else, and about whom, in
a field of eight, twenties were plentiful) made a ridicu-
lously easy win by a length which might have been materi-
ally added to had that been necessary. This, too, with
the mare fresh from a spell. Truly the bejewelled are
the pampered of fortune.
* * * •
Aged Mozart —Unknown gelding Jew Boy, who
easily won Park Stakes at Hawkesbury meet, Saturday,
from a big field, should shape profitably in much better
company when wanted. Jew Boy had previously notched
successes in northern N.S.W.—among them a double at
Narrabri. A protest that the horse was prepared by an
unlicensed trainer came too late. After this, Welter,
wherein he was weighted lib. above the minimum, looked
good for Jew Boy, but he failed to shape, possibly
through fear of that objection cropping up again.
* * • •
“Oxboro” Autumn Handicap (l|m. and
100yds.), once among the bigattractions of the Continent,
hadn’t on Saturday last the fetching power of an ordinary
every-day suburban weekly big thing. A field of
moderates lined up—B of 13 entrants putting in an appear-
ance. Fungus, who never did anything to justify the
fact, was made favorite—3 to 1 against. Silver Forest
and Vocalist were also well backed. Outsider Skein Dhu
cut the work out, and was once eight lengths to the good,
but Silver Forest came along at the right end and won
fairly comfortably from Vocalist. Warrior 11. (last year’s
easy gatherer of same event) finished back of the lot with
Grafnell. Silver Forest belongs to bookmaker Beales,
who has only had him a few months. Nag’s previous
owner, intent upon visiting S’Africa, tried hard to get rid
of S.F. and one or two others at auction, but failed until
Beales came along.
• • « •
Oxenham’s Acrasia looks like giving Sydney
Cup, Easter Monday, a big shaking, judged by most
reliable of all lines —public form. Last time out (at
Rosehill, Saturday week) she shaped capitally, and will
have 181 b. less to carry in the Cup ; besides, distance is
her forte, considering the stable connections thought she
couldn’t lose Australian Cup and the great race she ran
in last Melbourne Cup. Northern N.S W. crack Postillion
must also be reckoned with, as, quite ready, he is a
dangerous customer always ; and, despite Abundance’s
failure to down Great Scot inside a fortnight ago, he
should be handy, even though the load (9st.) allotted him
is a thumping one for a 3yr.-old. Bewitcher (6.7) is
fancied in some quarters, and doing well on the track.
Patronage, trained away from the metropolis, is some-
what of a mystery, but marked “ fit” he’d have troops of
followers. Summed up, Acrasia or Postillion take writer’s
fancy most, with some Doncaster Handicap failure to
shake them up.
* * <* *
Money keeps coining for Australia for Doncaster
Handicap but, to time of writing, stable hasn’t a cent on
and price shortens as day (this Saturday) of race ap-
proaches. Most punters are on tenterhooks as to whether
they’ll get a run for their coin, but its next door to a
certainty the nag will be “after it,” as such prizes are
not available often and, further, Australia is not likely to
be so well in any future engagement; and she’d perhaps
have to carry another stone if the handicapper had his
way now. Next best fancied, The Idler, looks worth an
investment, especially when one remembers how he
pushed Clean Sweep at w.f.a. just after latter
won Melbourne Cup, and fact that he beat The
Victory and Flagship in Adelaide. Provided trainer
Williams brings his charge to the post fit and well—the
Victorian will make things sultry for the best. Marvel
Loch’s success at recent Rosehill meet was full of merit,
and, having done some good work since, she must be
given a short price chance, especially if backing comes
from the right quarter, for Argentum, who pushed Aus-
tralia to a head in the Rosehill Welter, is in the same
stable, and should supply a “line.” Of the others.it
seems Rose Petal and, possibly, Postillion (if that backing
of the Northern colt last week was inspired), are the best.

* * * *
The fact that Tattersall’s sweep on the Doncas-
ter has filled with 25,000 subs., and another on the Sydney
Cup is filling fast, proves that people who want to gamble
will gamble in spite of postal Ministers or parsons.
» • * *
Federal Postmaster-General announces that the
Postal law, which prohibits a fair gamble like Tatt.’s
sweep, is not to be extended to church lotteries, Eight-
hours Day sweeps and such like, wherein the speculator
is always “ taken down ” more or less—sometimes to the
extent of getting a pennyworth of gamble at the expen-
diture of a shilling. The principle of the Postal system
seems to be that gambling under fair conditions is not
allowed, but gamble-cwm-swindle is legal, if not abso-
lutely beautiful.
* • * •
A Charters Towers racing starter was recently
fined £7 7s. cost of new set of teeth, 10s. 6d. dentist’s fee,
£1 for lost time, and costs, for dealing out stoush to a
racing patron who boohooed over a bad start or some-
thing of the kind. They always were more lively than
dignified up North.
• • • *
A. O. Whitington, secretary of Adelaide Racing
Club, is only 46, and once patented an improved totali-
sator. He has been with the club ever since the rocky
times previous to 1888. His father is reputed to have
imported the first thoroughbreds into S.A.
• * * *
“Le Chasse ” predicts: “Punters who pin their
faith to Wakeful can rest assured that the marvellous
mare will not sport silk at the A.J.C. Autumn meeting.
Luckily she belongs to an owner who does not require to
race to balance his corn-bill, and, as she has now been in
training for over two seasons and paid her way most
handsomely, Mr. Macdonald rightly thinks she has earned
a holiday, especially as he has a well-grounded idea she
may be able to annex some of the good things going dur-
ing the Randwick Spring meeting in September. To run
Wakeful through the coming Randwick meeting would
necessitate part of her holiday being taken during the
early part of winter, and that would spoil a deal of her
chance of success during the Spring season. Therefore,
just at present, Wakeful is “ off,” but when she comes
again the stable will require to be very wide awake that
expects to down Wakeful.

“X. 0.”: Re Charters Towers Ikey Mo sup-
pression pars, in Bulletin. Pity the Queensland police
or Government don’t complete the job by suppressing
Mo. altogether. Mo. still flourishes at bike and athletic
sports, pony races, and in shops. The C.T. Pony Club
has a tote permit, and has no excuse for allowing Mo. to
bellow on its premises—still he bellows. The Govern-
ment refuses the League of Wheelmen a tote permit—
very foolish proceeding for a Government that wants
money baddy—so the bike people have to give Mo. per-
mission to bet. As to the betting shops—one zealous
Charters Towers bobby, if given instructions to that
effect, would soon settle them. For a Government that
hunted Tattersall out of the State, that of Queensland
is very tender in dealing with Mo., but then it is
very much parson-ridden, and the parsons have made so
much money telling people how Mo. came out of Egypt
that they feel tender towards him. Heaven knows how
many thousand Christians make their living preaching
about Mo.
• • • •
Liverpool Grand National Steeplechase this
year was remarkable for two things—the marvellous
manner in which the veteran’chasers finished, and the cruel
luck that befell Ambush 11., which fell at the last fence
while leading, but winded. And this after his Royal owner
had travelled to Aintree to lead the wished-for winner
back to the weighing-yard. The real winner, Drumcree,
is no chicken, as he has been racing five seasons (running
second to Grudon in the Grand National two years ago) ;
the second horse, Detail, is seven years old ; while the
third, Manifesto, is 15 years old, and not only
won the Grand National of 1897 and 1899, but
ran third last year when Shamrock Lass, lOst. lib. , won,
Matthew, 9.12, being second. The old warrior, Manifesto,
had then to carry 12.8. He ran five times last year, and
though he failed to win, he ran into a place every time.
He started ’chasing in 1892, and has been going ever
since. Manifesto has many wins to his credit, one of the
best being the Lancashire Handicap Steeplechase of
£2170. His first try for a Grand National was made in
1895, when he ran fourth. The Grand National has now
been an annual event at Aintree for 65 years, and the
entrance fee is 25 sovs., the length of the course being
4 miles 856 yards. This year the “ National ” was worth
2525 sovs., including a trophy valued at 100 sovs.—a nice
little pile which, had he won it, would have enabled
King Ned to keep the wolf from the door till the end of
winter. But all the best “lepping” horses are Irish.
Another injustice to England.
» * * *
Recent Westralian Goldfields’ meeting saw
crack jock R. Trewartha up on 13 firsts in 17 starts.
* * * *
For a considerable time the bookmaker has
defied the efforts of the Maoriland racing clubs to expel
him from the racecourse. To settle the point the Wel-
lington club took the matter into court, and the Chief
Justice has decided that “ Ikey” can be expelled, since,
“ under its lease, the club has the right to frame regula-
tions to prevent bookmakers from carrying on their
calling.” There is much weeping and wailing now !
• • * *
Will of recently-deceased Sydney jockey, Frank
Kuhn, was sworn at £6449, and consists mostly of a life
policy (£500) house and land in suburb of Kensing-
ton, near Randwick racecourse, and a saddlery business
in Pitt-st., Sydney. In the circumstances it isn’t to be
wondered at that the disqualification imposed by the
Y.R.C. never worried Kuhn much.
• * * *
The last cricket match of the season gave
another showing-up to the bowling strength of Warner’s
team. To his big total of 553, S.A. replied with 304
Claxton, 88 ; Hill and Jennings, both over 50. Thomp-
son’s fast yorkers did all the damage, and he took 9 for
p 5, all the other bowlers being scored off at a tremendous
sace. In the follow-on he seemed ineffective, and the
score mounted to 454—the colt Gehrs, 100; Hack,
90; Hill, 73. Going in to make 206 to win, the
Englishmen were all out in 2£ hours for 108, amid
tremendous excitement. Dowson, top scorer, was run-
out, and the other nine wickets fell to Hay’s fast
medium stuff, six being bowled. Burnip, Fane, and
Taylor fell to the hat-trick, Warner and Bosanquet to
consecutive balls. The sensation was increased because
Hay only got into the team as emergency man, and also
lives in the district where the match was played. The
Unley Oval provided a splendid wicket and a rough out-
field, and the result was quick scoring throughout, 1419
runs being made in under four days. Stanning (of the
spectacles) was a good deal knocked about on the rough
ground, and, said Warner after the match, deserved the
V.C. for standing up to the ball; but he added that they
had not had a better wicket in Australia.
Albert Trott’s performance in the S.A. match
was 0 wickets for 78, and 2 for 101; scores of 4 and 8.
Hay and Travers bowled unchanged through the English
second innings, although the latter never took a wicket.
Record ? Newland only gave away 1 bye in 661 runs.
• • * *
Eye-opener of recent England-S.A. match was
wonderful bowling of Hay, who bagged 9 for 67, and pos-
sibly might have had the other (Dowson’s) but for
his being run out. Performance is an exceptional one,
and recalled Collins’ dismissal of the same side on last day
of its match against Victoria. Hay has played elector-
ate 'cricket with Sturt Club for years, and only two weeks
before going through the Englishmen secured 7 for 29.
Merit of the feat, however, is materially depreciated by
the fact that on first and second days of match, when
wicket was easy, his work had no sting and was erratic.
At best, Hay is only a medium-pace man and may pro-
bably do nothing extraordinary against bats accustomed
to fast wickets.

Auckland Weekly News : “ Westland 22 v. English 11.
Currie quite surprised the Englishmen, proving himself a
regular stonewaller. ... He carried his bat out, after

doesn’t say if they cheered then.—J. Byrnes.
* • • *
“Stumped” says: All great men have their
own pet superstitions or little fads, and great cricketers
are no exception to this rule. The great Grace not only
has a weakness for wearing silk shirts—a weakness shared
by Ranji, who also loves white, and in the field often
looks like a large stick of liquorice rolled up in a table-
cloth—but W.G. also has a funny way of cocking up his
left heel (a sign of nervous trepidation) while waiting for
the ball to arrive. A. J. Begbie, the Harrow champion,
stands at the wickets with his legs very wide apart, and
he also has a queer trick of patting the ground with his
bat, exactly three times, while facing the bowler. This
is done “ for luck.” But the Eton boys used to kill a lot
of Begbie’s luck by piping out at the critical moment,
with each pat, “ One, two, three— hit! ” Lord Hawke’s
little fad is to wear his cap back to front; Gunn places
his foot to the exact inch, and then draws up his left foot
to the proper position ; Shrewsbury likes to play a couple
of little pat balls by way of a preliminary. All agree
that it is a fatal omen to drop your hat while walking to
the wickets, but writer thinks the worst omen of all is to
let a straight ball pass you when you get there.
• • • •
The veteran of Maoriland cricket is Richard
Nelmes, who recently top-scored in a Waikouaiti v.
Dunback fixture, notching 28. He took part in the All
England XI. v. Otago XXII. match in Dunedin in ’64—
the first English team to visit Dunedin—and, although
now about 70 years old, is as nimble as many of the
young ’uns in the field. Like George Giffen, his cricket-
ing 3tar never seems to wane.
Concluding rounds of Melbourne pennant cricket
matches are interesting. Saturday provided something
beyond the ordinary. East Melbourne, opposed to Mel-
bourne, finished 1 wicket for 232— McAlister contributing
103 and Stuckey 92—both not out. Melbourne had 4
down for 119. Fitzroy-North Melbourne engagement
attracted a huge crowd. Former lost 8 for 193, and latter
3 for 61. Left-handed Saunders was in fine form. He
bagged 5 Fitzroyites for 58 and, with last 7 deliveries,
scattered 3 for 2 runs. Match, ’Varsity against Carlton
resulted in 6 for 169 and 2 for 152 as named. Carlton’s
lot owed much to Graham (69) and Warne (52) not out.
* * • *
Next month sees the starting of the Football
season, chief event of which will be the visit of the Maori-
landers in July. Where these matches are to be played is
already troubling the governors of the sport. Last year
the Rugby Union, heading a revolt among the various
associations against the extortions of the Sydney Cricket
Ground Trustees, played the Queensland matches on the
’Varsity ground, but though successful, they would
naturally prefer the 5.0.0. Almost it would seem in
retaliation the Trustees have agreed to let the ground
practically free of charge to two Victorian clubs who are
coming over to give a fillip to the Australian game, neces-
sarily a rival to Rugby football, despite all protesta-
tions to the contrary, the Rugby Union has promptly
reiterated its demands for a concession from the usual
high charges, and a conference is to be held. The Sydney
ground trustees, by their donation of £lOOO to the
Drought Relief Fund, show that a few extra pounds
must be quite a matter of indifference to them, and, in
the interests of other branches of sport, might easily meet
the footballers more than half-way.
• • * *
Movement for firmly fixing Victorian Associa-
tion football in Sydney is spreading ; already nine new
clubs have been formed in big centres. Vic., Tas., and
S.A. governing bodies purpose sending teams to N.S.W.
during season ; first will be forefront Gollingwood and
Fitzroy combination on May 3, when they’ll play their
match for Victorian premiership, and, four days later,
meet a combined N.S.W. team. Gate goes towards for-
warding Sydney movement; visiting clubs will pay their
own exes., besides losing the big gate that would cer-
tainly result if they met in Melbourne. ’Tis anticipated,
too, that an army of Victorian football barrackers, <fcc.,
will come Sydneywards to see the Collingwood-Fitzroy
match, and special trains are to be arranged for. Matter
is so earnestly taken up that Sydney workers in the
game’s cause purpose sending competent players to the
various schools to teach football as she is played in Vic-
toria, and books of rules, &c., will be freely distributed.

“J.F.D.”: “S. the W.” (B. 28/5/’O3) asserts
that there is no skill in “ two-up,’’ as the coin makes a
half-turn in three to six inches, and no man can regulate
the spin to a foot. I know a man who would like to meet
“the Wrinkler,” when that interesting person was
flush. I have seen him practice for hours in a room
and go within an inch of the ten-foot ceiling nineteen
times out of twenty. He turns up sometimes of a lunch
hour at the foot of Druitt-street (Sydney), where on the
dead square he will “ head ’em ” eight times running, and
finding no one to set him, he will “ tail ’em ” for the same
number of times. When “ The Wrinkler” is in town he
should go down and see him, especially if he believes in
the law of chance.
• * • •
There seems to be a burning anxiety on the part
of many athletic bodies in N.S.W. that boast of a huge
credit balance, to give a big portion of it to the Drought
Fund. Hitherto it has been like drawing blood from a
stone to get anything like a decent grant to help clubs
who supply the fun to rake in the boodle ; and now,
while there are many awaiting financial assistance, the
executives of cricket and football in N.S.W. are rushing
to subscribe to a Drought Fund. The usual enormous
advt.
* * * •
Of all branches of sport, the motor-car should
be the most-beloved of the undertaker—since once there’s
a smash the victim seldom recovers. A prominent Aus-
tralian firm proposes to inaugurate a Sydney to Mel-
bourne motor-car fly—not so much as an advt., as to
settle the long-argued point as to how long it will take
an automobile to do the nearly 600 miles sprint.

Giant axeman pug. Bill Heckenburg won wood-
chopping championship at Cataract Dam (N.S.W.) t’other
day. Cracks from other States competed. Champion
bruiser of Tas. (James) is a wood-chopper, too, and ’tis
likely that he and Heckenberg will come together in a
glove fight if latter beats old man Dooley late this month.

“J.F.D.”: A shopkeeper near me has a grind-
stone on the premises. Was in the room behind his shop
the other morning when a man came in with six new
packs of cards, which he carefully unfolded. After
extracting the four Jacks he ground the remainder of the
pack slightly on the side edges, then repacked them and
went. “ Does about eighteen packs a week,” said the
shopkeeper ; “ I don’t know what for.”
• * * *
Final of big N.S.W. Bowling Club event —
“ Rawson ” Challenge Cup—was between champion team
Petersham and St. George, on Redfern green, Saturday.
Petersham was in front from the outset, adding more to
its advantage the longer the game lasted, and ulti-
mately finishing .110 to opposing side’s 68. St. George
was beaten in three rinks —badly in two of them, 38-14
and 34-17. In one rink Petersham notched a 5 and a 7
(the possible was 8) in succession, and two 4’s. Defeated
side undoubtedly played below form, and got worse the
further it went. Some members of the inter-State team to
oppose Victoria in Sydney this week figured on one side
or the other, but only one or two shaped promisingly.
Petersham has now won all the important club events of
the year, viz.: Pennant, “B” and “Rawson” Cup
matches.
• • * *
“Bias”: Petersham’s bowling triumphs this year
stand out ahead of anything ever previously accomplished
in N.S.W., securing Pennant and “B” matches—(latter
thrice in succession) and also Rawson Cup, Saturday last.
One of Petersham’s players (Harber) is in the final with
Whittle (City club) of N.S.W. B.A. championship, to be
played Easter Monday, and their four is still among those
left in the rink premiership now in its final stages, while
the club’s caretaker has won the best of the season’s
events open to him.
* * m m
Vic. bowling champion of champions final,
decided last week, went to West Melb. rep. F. Hardham,
who romped away from North Fitzroy man, W. Burr, and
finished 31 to 10.
* * • •
Prominent Sydney bowler, Frank Lemm, who
died at Balmain last week, built the first big barracks at
South Head. He also constructed Goulburn gaol, which
cost £70,000.

• * * *
How fine they cut it. One Sydney top-grade
pug. last week expressed willingness to fight another on
same plane if latter agreed to weigh lOst. National
Sporting Club tried to match them. Challenged agreed
to scale under limit fixed at noon on day of fight. “ No,”
said challenger, “ you must scale at ring-side.’’ Consider-
able talk, then challenged agreed to weigh at 6 o’clock—
two hours before time of fight—but challenger wouldn’t
have it, insisting that t’other might put on at least 21b.
meanwhile. “Bli me,” pleaded the challenged, “let a chap
have a bit of a tuck-in before he shapes up,” but chal-
lenger was immovable —must be just before entering the
ring or not at all. Remarkable how bruisers stick for
ounces now where a stone or two made little difference in
the old days.
* * • *
Clever welter pug Jack Blackmore faced Yankee
Bert Lundy (heralded a top-notcher in U.S.) at National
Sporting Club, Sydney, last week. It was a fast lively
tussle all one way—Blackmore’s—and ended in the third
round through the interposition of referee when Lundy
looked hopelessly out of it. Where the’Murkan got his
reputation from is mystifying. Blackmore had the ad-
vantage of having tried him out some on his arrival, and
consequently went into the struggle with a big heart and
showed off much of his fine skill, but left himself ’verv
open at times. 3
* * * *
Forefront heavyweight of nearly 20 years ago
and present able tutor, Tom Taylor, stripped to fight
young Sandy Ross at Sydney Gaiety A.C., Saturday night
and, all things considered, especially that he (Taylor) is
45, showed remarkable condition, and made his tough
half-as-old adversary look a very duffer. Half-a-dozen
times in the first round Ross was floored by weighty
lefts and rights. ’Twas very much worse for
Sandy in the next till police interference necessitated a
halt, and Taylor got the decision, the side-bet, and the
bulk of a good “gate." Taylor was Peter Jackson’s pet
pupil, but was always heavy-limbed and muscle-bound
and consequently slow; his trade (that of a blacksmith)
was opposed to the necessary freedom of limb and activity
generally. There’s evidently a good fight in the ancient
yet, and he’ll probably be heard of again, matched with
one of Heckenberg’s class.
* * • *
Said that Major Taylor will retire at the end of
the present season. His Australian trip panned out just
on £2500, and his Parisian engagement will certainly run
into £3OO0 —making a not-too-bad season’s work of over
£5OOO.
• * * »
Auckland (M.L.) Amateur Athletic and Cycle
Club still plods along. For well-nigh a quarter of a cen-
tury this club has weathered the storm without having
to ask public support for trophies, etc. However
matters are financially bad with it at present; so
it is on the cards that if the “necessary” doesn’t soon
come in, the Auckland club will be wiped out.
• • • *
Died at Pascoe Yale (Vic.) t’other day promi-
nent old greyhound-trainer Walter Melrose, in the
business in Australia 25 years. First man to employ him
was Hon. W. B. Rounsevell, of Adelaide. Melrose pre-
pared winners (Rhodanthe, Melfort, High Rent) of three
Waterloo Cups, and his list of successes in Derbies, Oaks
and Legers throughout the Commonwealth is excep-
tionally long. Cancer was the primary cause of death.
« * * •
Rumball, Sydney amateur top-notch billiard
player of 1901, and his vanquisher (Belfield) in following
year, contested a match—not for Belfield’s title—but
“ just for the fun of the thing ” (they explained in effect)
at Sydney National Sporting Club last week. Conditions
were 2000 up. Rumball won by 426. When they met
last, a few months ago, Belfield came out atop—l9s
points to the good in 500 ; then some of Belfield’s breaks
were 156 and 116 unfinished and several higher than 50.
Recent match, however, saw Belfield fail to reach 50 once
and only 11 times did he exceed 30. As evidencing how
little form is worth going on in billiards it is shown that
three or four weeks ago Belfield beat Adelaide crack,
Birkett, by 243 in 500 ; same day Campbell whipped
Rumball by 150 in 500 ; then Rumball squelched Belfield
by 426 in 2000 and Birkett accounted for Campbell by 136
in 2000. Somewhat of a mix up this.

Another extract from Sydney Telegraph’s
serial story, “ A Prince of Sinners ” :
Theoretically freetrade should—l admit it—make us
the most prosperous nation in the world. As a matter of
fact, never since the country commenced to make history
has our commercial supremacy been in so rotten and in-
secure a position. There isn’t a flourishing industry in
the country, save those which provide the munitions of
war, and their prosperity is a spasmodic, and I might
almost add, an undesirable thing.

This is announced as the programme of
the Tasmanian Labor party, now just beginning
to be visible over the rim of the political horizon :
(1) Paying members a sufficient sum to enable them
to live up to the requirements of the position ; (2) adult
suffrage, the members of both Houses to be elected on the
same franchise, and public-houses to be closed during the
hours of election ; (3) free and compulsory education ; (4)
boards of conciliation and arbitration courts ; (6) a
Factory Act similar to the Victorian Act; (6) a legalised
eight hours’ day ; (7) a progressive land tax ; (8) State
control of the liquor traffic ; (9) absentee tax ; (10)
Government to take over monopolies, such as Tattersall’s
sweeps, and run them for the good of the country.
And, as usual, not a word about honest and solvent
finance, or about owning our railways and other
works instead of having them owned by the
London money-lender. Till the Labor party gets
a clean and vigorous financial policy it can’t hope
to prosper.
The conference between Melb. Trades
1 lall and the Vic. Council of Churches has evolved
what looks like a fairly workable scheme for State
ownership and control of the liquor industry. The
conference proposes to ask for a Bill providing
for :
(1) Five years’ notice, in lieu of compensation, to
existing publicans of compulsory resumption by Govern-
ment of their licenses and businesses. (2) Licensed
houses to be reduced to 1 to SUO of population in Mel-
bourne, 1 to 600 in rural, and 1 to 1000 in urban districts.
(.3) Government to acquire, at end of five years, as
rapidly as finances permit, all houses for the sale
of intoxicants, and to carry on liquor traffic
therein. (4) Government to carry on trade under
these conditions: (a) shortened hours ; ( b ) no early
or late permits; (c) only males to be employed ;
( d ) houses to be closed on parliamentary and municipal I
election days ; (e) no one under 18 to drink on premises,
and no one under 16 to take liquor away ; (/) Govern-
ment pubs to sell liquor only. (5) After expenses, inter-
est and sinking fund payments have been met half the
profits to go to support hospitals, asylums, and homes for
aged, and half for the maintenance of institutions sup-
plying public needs and for recreative purposes—these
moneys to be distributed according to population.

Some Ballarat (Vic.) manufacturers having
asked the Government for assistance, Melbourne
Argus proceeded to tell the awe-stricken public
what a hideously-sinful thing is “this raiding of
the State coffers on behalf of an artificial
industry.” And just when Australia’s “vast
natural resources ” are represented principally by
gratis seed wheat, free railway passes, and
Drought Saturday collections, it has the gall to
draw attention to the value of “our primary
fundamental industries” as compared with the
artificial ones, and exhorts the listening State to
notice that it is on these great natural land
industries that our prosperity depends.

Tanna the Sentimentalist.
On the occasion of the turning of the first sod
of the Gladstone-Rockhampton line Governor
Chermside was interviewed by certain Kanaka
residents of the Rockhampton district. The
Brisbane Courier thus describes the interview:—
The senior Kanaka, in well-chosen English, reminded
His Excellency that the strong must help the weak, and
explained that they did not want to break up their little
homes, for “ God has told us that it does not matter
where our bodies lay when we die. . . . We approach you
because we are a weak people and you are strong.” The
Islanders, after some further intercourse with the
Governor, sang together the hymn, “God be with you
till we meet again.”
It is a pity that the people who get up these little
performances so overdo them as to destroy all
appearance of spontaneity. The whole of the pre-
ceding business—right from the speech of the
Kanaka who addressed the Governor “in well-
chosen English ” down to the Moody and Sankey
“God be with you” finale —bears the impress
of being a carefully-rehearsed affair, probably
engineered by some Kanaka missionary, who was
probably engineered by the same crowd that con-
cocted that bogus Kanaka petition of some months
ago. The Philp crowd (in spite of the Rio Loge
6asco) has not yet given up hoping it may persuade
folks that the hapless Polynesian is in imminent
danger of massacre, or expropriation, or being re-
turned with great violence against his will to some
island inhabited by hostile tribes, who kill off
strange Kanakas nearly as quickly as the sugar
industry does.
♦
Queensland Governor Chermside, when up in
the piebald part of his dominions, was bombarded
with numerous pro-Kanaka petitions, some of
them loaded with hard masses of truth. For
instance:—
When we islanders were first brought into Queensland,
the arrangement was that we should serve three years
under an agreement, and at the end of that time the
Government should send us back to our homes. Instead
of doing that, as soon as our three years were up, we had
to enter into fresh agreements with the sugar planta-
tions. According to the law that was passed, we should
have been sent home again at the end of the three years.
The Government carried out the law for a little while, but
then they entered into an agreement with the sugar
plantations, instead of sending us home. If the Govern-
ment had stuck to the law, we would not have had to
bother them now. The Government let us settle down in
this country and buy land without saying anything.
Just so; and Australia doesn’t propose to make
that mistake any more.
Tweedle-Joe and Tweedle-John.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ All Politicians should be brothers,”
Said Mr. See—“except Carruthers.
“ He is, as every one knows well,
A Bounder, and an Ishmael.
“ His hand’s against each honest man—
Like me, and like O’Sullivan.
“ He hates the brilliant Sees and Cricks,
Who are the salt of politics.
“ He never felt the patriot’s fire—
To wipe us out is his desire.
“ And in our places this vain elf
Would put his minions and himself.
“ Then let the hands of true men be
Against Carruthers raised,” said See.
“This Government,” Carruthers said,
“ Has earned a title to be dead.
“ It has, we know, committed crimes
Enough to hang it fifty times.
“ And hanged it certainly would be
If all were patriots like ME.
“ The Sees, O’Sullivans, and Cricks,
Have made a mock of politics.
“ The public coin they’ve sloshed around,
And like a penny spent a pound.
“ A doctor when he finds that he
Is verging on insolvency,
“ Because no patients he can snare,
Sets up a carriage and a pair,
“ To make the public rush his bell,
Believing he is doing well.
“ ’Tis'on this easy, swindling plan
These doctors act —quacks every man.
“ They sent the country on a spree,
A wild financial jamboree.
“ The cheque is blued, the credit done,
And now the Horrors have begun.
“And New South Wales, through their mistakes,
With bloodshot eyes is seeing Snakes.
“ Wake up, O citizens ! Arise !
And on Carruthers cast your eyes.
“ Inspect him : open wide his vest,
And take the heart out of his breast.
“ And you will find—at least you should—
Engraven there —‘The Country’s Good.’
“ The ship of State, that lubbers steer,
The Rocks of Ruin now is near ;
“ But let Carruthers take the wheel,
And she shall glide on level keel.
“ The Rocks of Ruin she shall clear—
For well your Joe knows how to steer—
“ And wafted by a gentle gale
Into the Port of Promise sail.
“ My bleeding country, chuck up See,
And cling to Joseph — cling to ME.”
CREEVE ROE.

The “ Absent-minded Beggar” fund has
broken out again in London under a new name—
a name that is an awful satire on the howling
“ patriotism ” of years ago. It is now
known as the “Distressed Soldiers’ Fund.” A
distressed soldier is presumably, in most cases, a
“reserve” man who left his billet to go to
S’Africa under promise of being welcomed back
by his Jingo employer if he didn’t happen to die
for the Hempire’s cause.
Edwards, the Camberwell (Eng.) murderer,
when sentenced to death showed a disposition to
resent having a judicial homily added to that
punishment. From a newspaper account: —
“ Guilty,” said the foreman, and Edwards moistened
his lips and remarked, “Simply rot.”
“ Have you anything to say why sentence of death
should not be passed,” demanded the clerk.
Edwards replied shakingly, scarcely above a whisper,
“ Right you are. Get on with it as quick as you like.”
Then, as the black cap was placed on the judge’s head,
he stiffened and exclaimed, with an attempt at jaunti-
ness, “Why, it’s like being on the stage.”
His Lordship proceeded: “Prisoner at the bar, the
jury have found you guilty of this crime—the only verdict
possible according to their oaths and their duty to their
consciences.”
Edwards : “ Good ! Right you are ! ”
His lordship proceeded to speak of the career of crime
thus brought to a close, and Edwards said, impatiently,
“ Look sharp ; get it over! ”
His Lordship: “It now remains for me to pass sen-
tence upon you in accordance with the law."
Edwards : “Go on ; set ’em alight, guv’nor t ”
To the formal sentence Edwards remarked : “Ha, ha !
what a farce ! Ha, ha! ”
The Bulletin is still waiting for that lawyer to
arise who will appeal on the ground that the law
only allows hanging, and that hanging and being
jawed at great length by his Honor as well is an
illegal and double-barrelled punishment.
The appointment of J. Lionel Fegan
makes three Honorary Ministers in the See
Government. See’s is the first N.S.W. Govern-
ment that has kept Honorary Ministers without
portfolios. In the other States the unemployed
gratis Minister has long been an institution.
George Turner, of Victoria, once had five of them
simultaneously, but three were M.’sL.C. who had
to be bought with the cheap distinction in order
to prevent the Upper House being unanimously
hostile. The most interesting of the present
N.S.W. trio is Honorary Minister Bennett, who
apes port r olio rank, and alludes to himself as the
Minister of Forestry or Felled Timber or Sleeper
Getting or something—at all events, the job
carries with it a suite of rooms in the Mines
building.
Mr. Edden, deputy leader of the N.S.W.
Labor party:
People seemed to be under the impression that the
Labor party was encouraging- the Government in extrava-
gance ; but as a matter of fact he was the first to object
to pensions for highly-paid civil servants, and it was his
party which Opposed the Bill to increase the Railway
Commissioner, Mr. Kirkcaldie’s, salary from £l5OO to
£2OOO.
A very ineffective reply. The N.S.W. Labor
party is not charged with encouraging extrava-
gance in the payment of brains : but it is more
than suspected of a tendency to sweat brain-
workers and to aspire to getting judges, managers
and administrators at a pound a day. And that
ultimately leads to the greatest extravagance.
But what the L.P. is accused of is this—that it is
willing to let the Government borrow money to
employ labor on work, whether it is useful or not,
instead of striving to force the employment of
labor in the profitable production of the goods
which we now import.


England has started a National Association
to consider what may happen to the country’s
food supply m time of war, and at its very first
meeting these awkward remarks were thrown
at it:
It is of no use to look back to former wars, and tell
people what was done then. The conditions ire com-
pletely changed. Take the case of the Crimean war Thi.
population then was 28,000,000 only. We grew 16 000 oon
quarters of corn at home. We imported 3,000,000 Quar-
ters from abroad. Now the population is more than
40,000,000 ; but, instead of the 16,000,000 quarters I hate
referred to, we grow only 6,000,000 to-day, and are
obliged to buy nearly 25,000.000 from abroad ThatT is
rather a heavy price to pay for the blessings of Freetrade
Just before the Commonwealth began
business, Victoria passed a law providing that
every Victorian civil servant taken over by the
Australian Government should be paid at the
highest rate paid to any man of his class anywhere
in the Commonwealth—even if the other man
received the special rate paid to civil servants in
the most expensive goldfields districts of Westra-
lia. Westralia went one better by giving all the
civil servants it handed over to the Commonwealth
the nght to three months extra holidays on full
pay, or six months on half pay, everv six years—the
result being that almost every Westralian civil
servant taken over is now demanding six months
holiday. And while it has burdens like these to
carry, people want to know why the Australian
Government doesn’t run things at a cheaper rate.
A North of Ireland man, born and bred in
black and bitter Belfast, who carries with him
three vivid scars (a stab caused one, a knife
another, a stone the third) as signs of the religious
enthusiasm of his early associates, is fond of warn-
ing his fellow-workmen in Australia against
sectarianism. He was brought up in the thick of
it, when almost every Sunday there was a row
between Orange and Green, and to hate your
neighbor for God’s sake was the one recognised
mark of the true Christian. Being a fine hand at
‘bullet-throwing” (a Belfast sport—throwing a
slug of lead, weighing some three pounds, the
greatest distance and with the greatest accuracy)
he was an Elder and a Deacon at a very
early age, and might have been a minister
and secured great advancement in his church
but that a pernicious habit of reading radical
literature weaned him from delight in sectarian
rows. There was a noble religious career in Bel-
fast in those days for a young man who could
throw a stone straight for a long distance ; but he
lost his brilliant prospects by reading and
absorbing a little liberal education. Now, in his
middle age, and in supposedly enlightened Aus-
tralia, he is disgusted to find that the Macky-
Moran propaganda is reviving the rows which
made life a sultry hell and a loud and bitter howl
in the North of Ireland.

MIDDLE AGE.
Joints grow stiff—Twinges of Gout—Rheumatic
Pains—Digestive troubles—Disinclination for
exertion—Backache.
THE CAUSE. THE CURE.
ot J N th , ma] .? of cases when a person gets to be over the age of forty, a diminution of energy
subtly makes itself felt. Nothing particular to grumble about, but still there is something that
torces mm to admit to himself that he is not as young as he was. In a large proportion of
instances, however, the advance of middle age is heralded in a more emphatic manner. The
joints become stiff twinges of Gout are felt, Rheumatic and Neuralgic pains begin to trouble
tnem, there is a sharp pain in the back when rising from a stooping posture, a good meal is
eaten with the consciousness that presently they will be sorry that they ate it, the bladder
ecomes weak, and there is pain, difficulty, or unwonted frequency in passing urine. Now,
although there is, unfortunately, no means of preventing the advance of age, it is possible to
ward oft its effects for a long period if adequate care is taken to see that
THE KIDNEYS AND THE LIVER
are in good working order. When these vital organs are efficiently p rforming their office the
encroachments of age are always gradual and painless.
. The Kidneys. It is the duty of the kidneys to rid the body of cer ain urinary poisons. The
kidneys of the average person filter from the blood about three pints of water every day. In this
water should be dissolved waste matter, produced by the wear and decay of the tissues, to the extent
of one ounce of urea, ten to twelve grains, in weight, of uric acid, and other animal and mineral
matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce. If the kidneys are weak or diseased a
certain proportion of this waste urinary matter remains in the system and causes us to suffer from
C !; out ' Lumbago, Sciatica, Persistent Headache, Neuralgia, Anamia,
Debility, Gravel, Stone and Bladder Troubles. When the kidneys are properly doing their
work none of these complaints can exist, as the causative poisons are then duly removed in a natural
manner.
People approaching middle life are very prone to suffer from kidney diseases, the commonest first
sympt om of which is
PAIN IN THE BACK.
A simple test to make as to the condition of the kidneys, is to place some urine, passed the first
thing in the morning, in a covered glass, and let it stand until the next morning. If it is then cloudy
shows a sediment like brick-dust, if particles float about in it, or it is of an unnatural colour, then no
time should be lost in adopting remedial measures, or Bright’s Disease, Diabetes or some other serious
illness will be the result.
Warner’s Safe Cure.
It is nearly thirty years since scientific research, directed specially to diseases of the Kidneys and
Liven was rewarded by the discovery of the medicine now known throughout the world as Warner’s
bafe Lure. It was realised at the outset of the investigation that it was necessary to find a curative
agent which would act equally upon the kidneys and upon the liver, these two organs being so im-
mediately associated in the work of dealing with the waste products of the body. Since the discovery
of Warner s Safe Cure it is not necessary for any one to suffer from any of the complaints mentioned
above as resulting from uric poisoning, nor from Indigestion, Biliousness, Jaundice, Sick-headaclie
nor Blood disorders, which are all caused by the retention of Biliary poisons due to disease or in-
activity of the liver. Warner’s Safe Cure cures all diseases of the kidneys and liver and
all complaints produced by inefficient action of those vital organs.
Mr. Chas. Brown, of 5 Rule Street, Richmond, says:—“For quite ten years I suffered from
kidney and bladder complaint, and, although I consulted doctor after doctor and spent a small fortune
in medicines, I obtained no relief whatever until I began to take Warner’s Safe Cure. I had great
difficulty in passing water. As soon as I started taking Warner’s Safe Cure, the trouble I suffered
from was greatly relieved. I continued to take the medicine regularly, and soon a marked
improvement in my health took place. I am now as nearly in perfect health as I can ever expect to
be. lam very thankful that I heard of Warner’s Safe Cure, and more thankful still that I gave it a
trial, as I despaired of ever obtaining relief.”

will be sent free on application by H. H. Warner and Co., Limited, Australasian Branch
