The Inky Way.
In these days ot degenerate journalism, when the
poundkeepers attack of jim-jams and the funeral of the
sanitary man are duly recorded in the “ Personal ” column
ot the dailies, it is not often that one comes across a per-
so.^,w"° 18 efger to back up his or her desire for publicity
with £ s. d. Such a ram avis happened in at the
Bendigo Independent office one day recently, and in-
quired how much it would cost to secure a paragraph
about her son .Johnny, who had faHen down and cut his
leg. That woman had a proper conception of the value
of newspaper space, and provided a hint which every
reputable journal should act upon. If James Smith
desires to inform his friends that he is going to Sorrento
or is about to have a new washhouse erected, let him do
it at the ordinary advertising rates.
• * » »
Melbourne pressmen who helped their employers to
tight the great fight for “ economic reform ” have them-
selves suffered in the general depreciation of work values
which has resulted. Their wages have not actually been
reduced, but have remained quite stationary. It‘is said
that at the usual “ rise ” time, a couple of months ago,
both morning dailies failed to shell out anj'thing extra to
deserving employees. Also, the man who worked up the
whole Kyabram “reform” boom for his journal, and
organised more than two-thirds of the enthusiastic two-
men-and-a-dog meetings of which it consisted at the
beginning, was promptly sent about his business as soon
as there was no further use for him in the creation of
popular sentiment.
* • * W
Curious, the limitation of journalistic faculties. Dr.
W. H. Russell, Nicholas Woods, and Archibald Forbes,
were splendid at description, but all three were failures
in the production of leading articles. Also, Charles
Dickens, unapproachable as a sketch writer, contributed
but two leaders to his newly-established paper, the
London Daily News, and then abandoned the business in
disgust to John Forster. And the converse holds good.
It rarely happens that the leader writer makes any mark
in the attractive descriptive line. An introduction to a
Melbourne Cup Day some ten years ago, by an Age leader
writer, is a never-to-be-forgotten monument of inappro-
priate stolidity. Even David Sjune, whose taste is for
the solid, carped at it.
* * • •
Thos. Carlyle, on leader writers But indeed the
most unaccountable ready-writer of all is, probably, the
common editor of a daily newspaper. Consider his lead-
ing articles ; what they treat of, how passably they are
done. Straw that has been thrashed a hundred times
without wheat; ephemeral sound of a sound ; such por-
tent of the hour as all men have seen a hundred times
turn out inane ; how a man, with merely human faculty,
buckles himself nightly with new vigor and interest to
this thrashed straw, nightly thrashes it anew, nightly
gets up new thunder about it ; and so goes on thrashing
and thundering for a considerable series of years. This
is a fact remaining still to be accounted for in human
physiology. The vitality of man is great.”
* * • m
Buying a country news-organ is not the simple matter
people think. A country newspaper recently changed
hands at close on £6OO. When the buyer “took over”
he found that of the assets the three newspaper corres-
pondentships, worth, all told, 30s. a week, left the office
with the ex-editor, who had opened an office of his own ;
a good portion of the printing customers did likewise ;
while four-fifths of the “ads.” were dummies that had
run out at any period during the previous two years.
* • * *
Grafton (N.S.W.) Grip thus prescribes for the success-
ful running of a country newspaper : “(1) Mix a large
quantity of smoodge with a great quantity of sponge, and
kow-tow to everything and everybody, including the
town pump. (2) Take a great interest in all local move-
ments, especially those to which advertising and job-
printing are attached. (3) Do notcultivate a large mind.
(4) Never adopt an outspoken, independent attitude. (5)
Do not have the courage of your own opinions, and never
give offence even to the local nightman, as he may be a
possible subscriber. By strict attention to these matters
you will go ahead, and your district will stay where it is.
If you cannot follow out these instructions you must put
up with less butter on your bread.”
• * * ■
“Melb.”: I have seen the vore-time reporters’ books
of the Age, in which Howard Willoughby figures with
such forgottens as Lindsay, H. J. Smith, and Horton.
His name is invariably spelt “ Willoby.” Is this merely
the laziness of the staff head? [No, it was his horse-
sense: if a man named Willoughby is to report “The
Society for the Prevention of Christian Knowledge,” the
entry into the “duty book ” might take two lines. Some-
body’s cat's tail has to suffer, and it is the reporter’s. On
some papers Willoughby would have been “ Wbv ”1
+.
One Senator, anyhow, Staniforth Smith,
of Westralia, who isn’t on the Liberal side in
politics, tells Queensland the straight truth about
its black man :
The Kanaka question is a closed chapter in the history
of the Commonwealth Government, and there is no pro-
bability of its being reopened. It is far better that the
section of the Queensland community which desires the
continuance of the traffic should recognise this, instead
of buoying themselves up with delusive hopes.

Push's Queensland Almanac for 1903 proves
to be even fuller than usual of valuable information
about that part of Australia which at present groans
under the dual inflictions of Philp and the Piebald. There
are trade directories for Brisbane and every other town in
the State. A list of all the Parliamentary Acts passed
since the granting of Constitutional Government
nished, and railway, postal, telegraphic, and telephonic
information is laid on liberally. A specially useful and
highly-commendable feature of the work is the section
“ Queensland Men of the Times,” in which a short
account is given of living men who have raised a dust m

Melba’s announced determination to sing no more in
Australia was foretold by the fact that she didn’t give
the ss. concert at Melb. Exhibition Building, which was
said to have been fixed for March 28th, ere she started on
her Maoriland trip. All the same, Melba is a woman,
after all, and her “determination” is not necessarily
unalterable. Having gone up like a rocket, she will be
loth to splutter out like a damp squib. _ Why not a two-
and-sixpenny farewell at the Exhibition ? 20,000 half-
crowns are £2500.
Poor Kate Vaughan died at Johannesburg, poverty
stricken. And yet S’Africa nowadays abounds in middle-
aged military Gussies who knew dainty Kate when she
was a popular actress and Gel. Wellesley’s wife.
In an amusing assault case heard at Melb. City Court
last week it transpired that a highly mettled spinster had
banged Strong-man Pagel with her ’brella till it broke,
because he pushed her away from the cage of his thought-
less young lion. It also transpired that the lion was a
condensed milk baby. Pagel bought the tawny pet when
he was only six months old and Mrs. P. reared him on the
bottle. Which possibly explains why the growing youth
is so fond of putting his paws through the cage to grab
any available bystander. Much milk from the cow has
made him innocent and calf-like.
Mrs. Kate Hart, one of the Sisters Leoni, once here
with Rickards’Tivoli co., recently sued for a divorce, in
England. She stated that she was a Roman Catholic, and
declared that her husband, a Protestant, used to force
meat down her throat on fast-days.
Adelaide Theatre Royal celebrates its 35th. birthday
on April 13th. It was opened by Geo. Ooppin’s co. which
included Rogers, Greville, Coppin himself, and Mdlle.. St.
Denis (who afterwards committed suicide.) First night
was devoted to “ All that Glitters is not Gold,” and
second to “Aurora Floyd.” Eloise Juno was leading lady,
of the second co. that put in an appearance at this
theatre.
“ E.F.” : In his correction of the original Kate Vaughan
paragraph, “ S.T.S.” is himself strangely incorrect.
Nellie Farren never “ shone in the line of a London street
arab.” She was a Gaiety burlesque-boy—“ principal
boy ” —first, last, and all the time. And a trim burlesque
boy, with even the hoarsest of voices, doesn’t resemble
the street arab of any city under the sun. Once in a way
Nellie Farren would play a dirty-faced young person,
with boots down at the heel, but then it was a girl she
represented, notably “ Nan, the Good for Nothing.”
Doubtless “ S.T.S.” was thinking of Fanny Leslie, who
made a specialty of musical street-urchin parts. He
couldn’t surely have been confusing Nellie Farren with
Jennie Lee, the gifted impersonatress of Jo 1 Jennie
was the one unrivalled artist in the arab line of character,
but she didn’t associate her Jo with song and dance busi-
ness.
The “ wrong ” Robert Henry Nichol—professionally
known as Robert Henry—who introduced “ silver coin ”
dramatic performances to Sydney and suburbs, has been
prohibited for ever by Justice A. H. Simpson from pro-
ducing “The Wrong Mrs. Wright.”
Miss “Ruby de Vere” (Radford), stage office, 16 York-
st., Covent Garden, London, inquires as to the where-
abouts of her father, a miner, whom she last heard of at
Adams’ Gully, near Cromwell, Maoriland.
Irving’s “Dante," at Drury Lane, London, is to cost
£lO,OOO for scenery alone ; costumes, etc., will add
hugely to that cost. And the actors—but what do the
actors matter ? It is to be a spectacular play.
Some recently-published reminiscences of the brilliant
but ill-starred Oscar Wilde state that his last play—since
produced in England with another person’s name attached
—was offered to an authoress famed for her epigrammatic
powers. She was to pay Wilde a certain sum down and a
part of the royalty, keeping the rest in return for the
loan of her name, so that the great aggregate snuffle of
England might not know, or might pretend not to know,
that it was patronising a play by the man who had been
in gaol. She refused the proposal, and said that to rob a
writer of the credit of such a masterpiece would be
monstrous. In the end a name was borrowed ; but other
things were borrowed, too, and Wilde never received a
copper.
Robert Courtneidge has come out to Australia this time
in a holiday spirit for the purpose of staging the two
Shakesperian productions—“ Midsummer Night’s Dream”
and “ As You Like It ’’—which Musgrove bought from
him in Manchester. The management of two theatres
during the past few years has not aged Courtneidge,
although he sorely needed a rest, he says. In fact, he
looks rather younger with a moustache than when he
made his farewell appearance at Melbourne Princess’s as
farmer Rocco in “ The Mascotte.” Then he was an actor,
“ painting his nose red and calling himself a low come-
dian ” (as Snazelle used to say of himself), or, on occasion,
playing a Scotch character part in higher comedy. One
didn’t expect that Rocco would return to Australia in the
capacity of a classic-play producer, steeped in Shake-
sperian traditions, and so forth. Before he left Man-
chester the reformed frivoller was presented with an
address enclosed in a casket of most serious design,
whereon the head of the Immortal Bard is engraven.
When he looks at that handsome compliment Court-
neidge pensively scratches the nose he used to paint red.
From London, regarding certain people known in Aus-
tralia Charles Arnold and his wife have settled down
in a charming home in Kent, which has been christened
“ Coo-ee ” because the money which purchased it was
earned in Australia.—Mr. Sharp, once one of the Arnold
co., is now studying for the Bar at the Inns of Court. —
Mr. and Mrs. Thomas and Miss Hope Mayne go to South
Africa in March with Sach’s co. to play in “If I were
King” and “ Mice and Men.” —Miss Bensusan, just arrived
from New York, leaves also for S. Africa with her brother
at the end of the month. —Florence Schmidt, who marries
Derwent Wood on the 25th March, is busy furnishing a
lovely little house in South Kensington.—Lillian Wheeler
plays in the curtain-raiser that precedes “The Marriage
of Kitty” at Wyndham’s Theatre. —Hetty Holroyd has
been very ill in Milan with influenza. She is fairly on the
road to recovery now.
“ Royalties on tour ” —it takes a daily column now to
tell it all. “A procession of historic barges,” “a pro-
cession of warships ”; it reminds one of how, when
interest flags and business falls away, the touring circus
man harnesses his six white horses to the red van, and
produces his tall hat, and gives the band an extra beer to
hit the drum harder than usual, and so wakes up the
tired, unsympathetic town.
“ Gnark ” : A par. re the Beatty girls in your last issue
is to me an interesting example of the prudence which
guides even young actresses (or actors) in speaking of
their ages. They look ahead. Not satisfied with being
young at present, they already begin to make arrange-
ments for being young in the future. The par. in ques-
tion states that “ May Beatty is just 21, being two years
younger than her sister Maud.” Excuse my cough. The
first and la«t time I had the pleasure of seeing Maud
Beatty as a juvenile comic-operatist (sister May was also
figuring on the programme) she played Guiseppe, the bari-
tone character in “The Gondoliers,” and because she was
so well-grown and matured in her style I expressed
grave doubts as to her juvenility. An introduction to
the damsel was the result of this scepticism. Mr. Tom
Pollard called the blushing Maud forth from her dress-
ing-room, and when I asked her, in a blunt, parental
tone, how old was she, Miss Maudie replied, “ Fifteen.”
Also, she smiled a cute and winsome—not to say wicked
—smile, which seemed to say she had been 15 for several
months. But I will drop those extra months out of the
calculation. The occasion in question may have been
Miss Maudie’s fifteenth birthday. Anyway, it was the
night of her first appearance with Pollard’s Lilliputian
Opera 00. at Melbourne Princess’s, and I find, on looking
up the programme, that the date was Oct. 15, 1892.
Therefore I cough again.”
A narrow escape from arrest had Nellie Stewart. The
lady was sued by a suburban medico for five guineas, pro-
fessional fees. She refused to pay, did not defend, and
allowed judgment to go by default. As Australia’s Nellie
was announced to sail on Saturday for Maoriland, an
application was made to Judge Rogers for a ca. sa. order
for her arrest. The Judge issued the order, but mercifully
allowed it to lie low until 2 o’clock. By that time Miss
Stewart had paid up.
The first modern circus was at the old Olympic Theatre,
London, built by Philip Astley with the timbers of a
condemned man-of-war ship.
There probably wasn’t a man in Australia
who knew more of either pastoral finance or of the
holes there are in the States’ land laws than
George Moore, the Union Bank’s station inspector,
who fell from a Cobar hotel balcony, the other
day, and was killed while there were still years of
hard work in him. For many years he kept his
eye on Goldsbrough, Mort’s squattages, and knew
practically every holding in Eastern Australia.
When the N.S.W. Land Appeal Court was con-
stituted Moore was among those “tipped” fora
seat as squatters’ representative, but he declined
to push his claims, and continued his life’s work
of overhauling hopeless station accounts, and
puzzling out how to make one cheap man manage
about eight holdings.

The Cardinal had his nightshirts stolen last
week, and the Evening News published a full
description of the “ broad-collared, ermine-lined
robes de nuit, marked with P.X.M. on the south-
west corner.” Next day a letter and a parcel
arrived at the presbytery, and Dean O’Haran read
that, “ Touched by the enormity of his crime, the
penitent thief begged to return the sacred vest-
ments.” “ They’ve been sent back, your Emin-
ence.” “Bless me!” piped the aged prelate,
“how remarkable!” But the parcel only con-
tained some relies of the Moore Park tip. It was
doubtless a wicked and nasty joke of the P.D. A.
Some curious anomalies have arisen out
of the recent examinations in connection with the
Federal service. In one case, a female officer,
placed in charge of a number of others, signally
failed to pass the examination for the minimum-
wage, with the result that, though she still holds
her superior position, she now receives much less
salary than such of her subordinates who did pass.
N.S.W, Art Society.
AUTUMN SHOW.
Atmnra Exhibition of N.S.W. Art Society has
collected a mostly charming set of tame, fed-out-of-
the-hand little artisticisms. There is not one
effort at a really fine picture; but there are very
many neat little, sweet little works, which would
adorn any drawing-room with amiable grace.
Truth is, that this autumn exhibition is designed
not to show what the artists can do, but to see
what they oan sell. There is accordingly an
absence both of the fine effort which comes
close to a great picture and of the weird
monstrosity which represents the perverted
ambition of the ultra-impressionist. Every-
thing has been painted to sell, and so every-
thing strives at an air of quiet prettiness. Some-
times the effort is successful, and there is,
aooordingly, much dainty, graceful, artistic work
on the walls; sometimes it falls just short of
success, and the effect is a futile and rather
irritating pretty-prettiness ; very rarely is there
to be seen anything palpably bad, for this is an
exhibition for sale purposes, and the artist may
stake his hidden soul that a woman should have
the thighs of an elephant and the hair of a clover
meadow, but he won’t stake his chance of a sale on
that contention.
• • • •
Looking at the collection with an eye to its
main object, seeing so much work —291 pictures—
of various grades of merit, one is tempted to roam
through the homes of Sydney and bring them
therein, that they may buy, buy, buy. Anything
in the room oan be had for about 10 guineas, most
of the works (frames and all, and a frame costs
some shillings) for one guinea or two guineas or
three guineas. Yet there are hundreds of houses
where the carpets are rich and the furniture is
expensive and the walls are bare, except for
framed oleographs, or photographic enlargements
of some curious ancestor or some priggish progeny.
And'there are other houses, hundredsof them, which
their owners enter by a carriage drive to the
greeting of a score or so of servants, and their
walls are covered with “ copies of the old masters ”
—atrocious, mechanical Chinese-art sort of things,
ground out in Italian factories under sweating
conditions to meet the demand from Australia and
other countries of the ignorant rich. Pictures—
not great works of art—but pleasant, clever little
bits by men who have done moderately big things
with the brush —Australian pictures, can be had
far more cheaply than the copy of the old master,
almost as cheaply as the photographic enlarge-
ment, or the poor, tinted reproduction. Why
don’t the Australian public buy ?
• • • •
So "much for the main purpose of the show. As
for its Art—well, Art is neither bludgeoned with
atrocity nor seduced with mastery, but is by many
of the exhibitors tickled very pleasantly, and
smiles, as is right. There is a landscape (No. 11)
by Sid. Long with a touch of imagination, and a
moonrise from him showing such color in sky and
land as never was at moonrise except in the Land
of Moonshine. Then one confronts a long series
of Blue Mountain studies by J. Salvana ; praise
to him that, he does study the Blue
Mountains, the enchanted atmosphere of which
will never be really put on canvas until
another Turner comes. Most of Mr. Salvana’s
studies fail very palpably ; others bring some
suggestion of the radiant sky-effects and the vast,
mysterious distances of the great blue range.
Nos. 21 and 60 are, on the whole, his best
attempts. Mr. Lister Lister sends a batch of
effective land and seascapes, not one of them
failing to show quiet merit; he has been experi-
menting with the new Raffaeli solid-oil colors,
and the new medium seems in his hands to be
about as good as the old, and no better. There
are very many exhibitors who have been Lister-
Listering for this show. One can see dozens of pal-
pable imitations of his style—most of them pretty
enough in their way, and some of them likely to
get to the haven of being sold at two guineas. And
Mr. Dattilo Rubbo ‘ ‘ old - mas ter s ” as per ti n aciousl y
for a five guinea as for a 100-guinea show. All
his work (he has seven canvases) is of the same
order, with now very bright and now very dark,
and always very shiny, colors. With a little
judicious “cracking ” of the varnish, Mr. Rubbo’s
pictures would pass in some circles for “old
masters”; “Forget-me-not” shows best of his
work. MN Arthur Collingridge has some very
graceful work in water-color —“Evening” show-
ing best, and “ Mountain Creek, Lithgow,” next.
Miss Sophie Steffanoni has done very well in a
Blackheath sketch. Howard Ashton’s work is not
at all pleasing, too stiff in outline and exaggerated
in color. Fred Leist’s “Sorceress” makes an effort
to be distinguished from the rest of the pictures,
and only calls attention to its failure; but a pastel
from him, “ Red Hat,” is really good. A pleasing
picture, in spite of several defects, is “Love Me,
Love My Dog,” by Edith Cusack : the pose and
coloring of the girl are pleasing. H. G. Gar lick
does very well indeed in “ Kangaroos— Grey
Evening,” good in technique and excellent in
subject. R. Riches has two landscapes, noticeably
rich and effective in coloring. A portrait by A. J.
Fischer is very good. Other commendable work
is from A. J. W. Burgess, W. F. Hughes, G. W.
Hadfield, W. A. Moir (who gets good color with
his “Terrigal Lake”), H. Moore Jones, Henry
Crisp, Mathie Wearne, Alice Norton, and R. M.
Ronald.
• * * *
There is some “black-and-white,” but, except
that some of it is in red chalk, it calls for no
special notice.

London Times has been publishing a serie*
of articles on the “Future of the Army,” in which,
among other things, it is proposed that a force of
12,000 men be stationed in Westralia, for the
defence of one end of the Indian Ocean naval base.
A correspondent dusted the floor with this propo-
sal by reminding the Times that Australians
would not accept the presence of an army corps
paid and maintained by a Parliament on the
opposite side of the world, in which they have no
representatives, and controlled by an Executive
and generals responsible solely to that Parlia-
ment. In short, Australia doesn’t want to have
12,000 bailiffs in possession.
The Hotel Belmont, now being built in
New York, will be the finest in the world—of 27
storeys, with 750 bedrooms. It is to be mosquito
and noise-proof, as vyell as fire-proof, and if it
could only be made proof against the tertium quid
and the co-respondent it would be the boss hotel
of the earth.
The thing that generally happens to a
community when it starts to give concessions to
railway or tramway syndicates is beginning to
happen to Fremantle, in Westralia. So far the
case has given work to only about half-a-dozen
lawyers; but it is very young yet, and looks a
promising infant, capable of doing a lot of
mischief.

THE BENEFIT OF THE GENUINE.
Read how the genuine Kutnow s Powder restores a Sluggish Liver.
The liver is the mainspring of the system. When it is healthy it is the
®reat purifying organ of the body. When it is not, it is like a poisoned
cesspool, contaminating the entire physical organism.
A healthy liver secretes from two to three pounds of bile daily. Bile is
sent from the liver into the blood to gather up all the impurities. In due time
1 t returns laden with this refuse matter, which the liver secretes and expels
from the system.
When the liver refuses to take back the bile from the blood, Biliousness
is the inevitable result. Biliousness is bile out of place. The bile cannot go
wrong when the liver does its duty. Since the liver is the sieve of the system,
it must be kept clean in order to do its duty.
There is a right way and a wrong way to keep the liver clean and healthy.
Some folks believe in punishing the poor stomach and bowels with powerful
purgatives when the liver lags. This is a wrong idea; the right way is the
rational way.
KUTNOW’S POWDER is a rational remedy for BilioUSn63S, because it
restores natural action of the liver. It cleanses, cools, and strengthens the
liver and bowels. It expels all poisonous deposits and cleanses the systems
from all impurities.

Unscrupulous dealers are stocking cheap and worthless imitations of

The Emotions and the Intellect.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Malcolm, Fraser, and Stevens were discussing a
recent shooting case in which a bricklayer (whose
wife had left him for another man) had shot his
faithless spouse.
Malcolm said: “X think the poor beggar was
perfectly justified.
“Whilst sympathising deeply with the poor
devil,’’Fraser drawled, “it seems to me he made
one great error. He should have shot the other
fellow. What’s the good of shooting the woman
and letting the man live who enticed her away ? ”
“Now, in my opinion,” Stevens commenced in
his jocular style,” “you chaps are talking like
beastly barbarians. A woman isn’t a piece of
merchandise; she’s a perfectly free agent, and
even after having been married for some years, as
this woman was, she had a perfect right, as a free
agent, to go wherever her affections ’ led her.
Why can t you look at it in a philosophical way
and see that she was carrying out one of Nature’s
highest laws by leaving the man she disliked to
live with the one she loved. Speaking as a
reasoning being, I think her act quite justifiable
and the man’s act in shooting her quite barbarous ”
“ Yes,” said Malcolm. “ Most of us can agree
with your argument when it refers to the other
man s wife ; but you must remember that there
are always two influences at work, Intellect and
Emotion. You, with your calm philosophical
training, and your naturally unemotional tempera-
ment, are better fitted than this bricklayer to curb
a momentary passion. But this chap’s feelings
got him under, so he shot his wife. I daresay
sorry now.” J
Just suppose for a moment,” Fraser chimed
in, “it was your wife, Stevens, who left you for
another man. With all your fine talk about
philosophy and selection .”
“You chaps misconstrue the point of view I
look from,” Stevens remarked, in that superior
pained tone of his. “ What I was endeavoring
to instil into your particularly thick skulls is that
when a man develops his intellect sufficiently
(understand, Malcolm, I said sufficiently), he
rises superior to the ordinary emotions which tend
to drag every man down to the level of brute
creation.”

THE PIANOLA
r
!>
What
It
Means
To
You.
If you are the owner of a piano which is seldom or never used, little
benefit is derived from it by either you or your family. By the purchase
of a Pianola every member of your family can play the piano, although he
or she may never have played one note in their life.
Ihe striking of the notes of the selection, in proper time and place, is
no concern of the player. This is correctly done by the Pianola.
The player’s only task is to decide how slow or how fast the notes shall
be played, or how loud or soft he wants them to sound.
Three little levers serve to impart the player’s wishes to the piano.
Should the selection not be familiar, a very simple set of markings is given
on the music-roll which unwinds in front of the player, directing the proper
movement of the levers.
If you will write us, asking for Catalogue B, we will mail you one
free, explaining more fully the Pianola.
The price of the Pianola is £6O prompt cash, or £65 on moderate
monthly payments.

80LE AGENTS FOR AUSTRALASIA.
SYDNEY : 323 George St.
MELBOURNE: 185-187 Collins St.
The Hebraic Aboriginal.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There are innumerable tribes that practise cir-
cumcision, and several that have individuals
showing distinctive Jewish features, notably on
the overland telegraph line and in central Aus-
tralia.
It is ijow over 40 years since I first witnessed in
South Australia the operations of circumcision
and introcision. Circumcision is quite common
amongst the aborigines both north, south, east
and west.
The great Duyeeri tribe inhabits the country
from Mount Frieling, on the south, to Cooper’s
Creek, on the north.
They are a wild lot, and pretty numerous. And
they, and many of the tribes surrounding them,
practice circumcision, besides koolpie, or intro-
cision. They are almost booka, or vegetarians.
I don’t think I ever saw a kangaroo near their
country.
Rats, however, at times abound, even unto
plagues thereof. Ardoo, usually written nardoo,
and other seeds form their principal food. The
operation of circumcision (Kurrawellie Wonkanna)
is performed on the boys when they first begin to
show unka (a beard).
The lad is decoyed away from camp by some
woman for whom he has shown a partiality. When
she has him alone she suddenly slips over his head
a koorie, a twisted cord with shell attached. Of
course the old men, knowing what is going on,
are on the alert. Quarrelling between the boy’s
relatives and the others then goes briskly on.
After peace is once more restored they camp as
usual. Only the boy Kurrawellie (name before
circumcision) does not come back.
He remains alone in the bush. Next day, in the
company of his chums, he visits the neighboring
tribes. All about do “big one walk round,” when
these people learn what is to happen and come in
to the spot fixed for the ceremony. I believe
the neophyte camps alone on this visiting tour.
After some days he is hunted up and brought
home; this is on the day prior to the rite. He is
taken into camp, and all have a good look at him.
His father (apirrie) and near relations fondle
him.
Then a number of the warriors rush him, catch
him up, and he is borne bodily away amid a ter-
rible uproar of shouting and screaming. He is
taken a goodly distance from camp and covered
over until morning. , More quarrelling now takes
place in the camp, but rarely reaches the fighting
stage, though occasionally fights do occur. Fight
or no fight, there is always much bluster and
threatening, with screaming of hysterical women.
Then the women are ordered away “long way
off,” where they remain by themselves until day
once more dawns. The time is now drawing close,
and a youth commences to run and sprinkle dust
as he runs. This is an appeal to the divinity or
good spirit, Moora-Moofa, to be with them. The
operation, kurra-well-yee wonkanna, is then per-
formed with a quartzite or flint knife, or, more
often nowadays, with a fragment of sharp glass.
Then the apirrie (father) re-names his son,
who, after receiving his yinka or human hair belt,
often several hundred yards long and much prized,
is led off by the young men and remains away for
months, eventually returning with all the privi-
leges of a man of his tribe.
It is this tribe or collection of tribes that uses
the great two-handed sword boomerang, mura-
wirrie, often six feet long jmd nearly four inches
broad.
The Port Lincoln tribe call circumcision par-
dnap-pa, or rather the boy is thus known. The
women are driven off when the ceremony is about
to take place as with the Duyeeri. A great battue
then takes place, and a store of animal food is
laid in. The women gather fruits, seeds, &e.
When the feast is over, “ all about bingey tight,’’
the youlli or operator makes his preparation. This
man not unfrequently is a member of a neighbor-
ing tribe, the performing of the operation being
granted to him as an honor. The boy is brought
in blindfolded, amid a general murmur or low
growl of all assembled. A number of men, stoop-
ing down, form a rude human altar on which the
victim is pressed, lying on his back, firmly
held by arms, legs, and head, and a man sitting
across his chest.
The yuntha, or bull-roarers, are sounding now
in all directions, whilst the youlli deftly and
effectually uses his flint knife, or chip of quartz,
as the case may be. At this stage several magical
words are repeated, whilst others cast dust into
the air to a peculiar rhythmical movement of the
feet, the while they bite savagely at their beards.
The operation being over, another big hunt takes
place The youth then has his hair held up by a
net of opossum fur, and an oonpah, or covering
is hung round his loins.
This tribe also performs the operation known as
Sturt’s Terrible Rite.
HARRY STOOKDALE.
The Justice of the Crows.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
As the sun rose the gums grew greener, and the
sky more deeply blue. From the delicate fronds
of oxidised copper at the roots of the blue gum in
the foreground to the purpling ranges that showed
between the peristyle of gleaming white boles,
the view was one that an Australian —or an artist
—would linger on.
Something moved a mile to the right. Through
the heat waves the figures of a man and a horse
showed, distorted as through cheap window glass.
At last, the hoof-beats arose above the chorus of the
locusts. Mounted Constable Donnon was twenty
miles out of Ballaba with only half his ride
accomplished His duty led him to undertake a
journey to the far side of the purple ranges to
serve a summons under the Thistle Act, and he
was heavy at heart in consequence. Had there
been a tragedy of any magnitude, a navvies’ row,
or anything calculated to arouse his interest, he
would not have minded a ride of forty miles. But
the service of a summons, and a trumpery one at
that, provided nothing to occupy his mind, and
Mounted Constable Donnon’s one object in life
was to prevent himself thinking. That he had
failed in his object was manifest in the set smileless
jaw, the furrowed forehead and the sunken cheeks.
In the township v :here was always something to
be done, always somebody to talk to, and, for 20
years, in different stations of the State, hard work
and,absolute will-power had kept his brains within
the lines of the normal. But Time had not been
land to him. It had poured on his wound neither
the ointments of philosophy nor forgetfulness.
now ’ w, thin five years of his pension, he
could feel as keenly as the terrible day ever so
many years back.
ITis two years at Ballaba had provided him
with more thinking than the whole of his previous
experience in the police force. He was alone on
the station, and there were occasional journeys,
such as he was taking now, which gave the
biograph of his brain a chance to defy him and
work in an endless series of pictures that wrung
his very soul to shreds.
As he rode along, he saw neither the purple
ranges nor the eucalypts around him. Before him
was a landscape purely English ; and among the
pictures, a man and a woman, both young. The
woman was beautiful, and with big blue eyes that
poured love into those of the man. There was a
cottage, with the man and the woman again, and
a r ij’ The story of the life in the cottage was
unfolded and unfolded by the mental biograph
until the door opened, and he saw another figure—
another man. The machine stopped working.
Mounted Constable Donnon looked ahead and
saw the tracery of green leaves against the sky,
the lace of shadows playing on the grass. The
biograph had suddenly ended.
c“ er a w^e b e l?an again slowly and mistily.
J.he first man and woman were in the picture. The
woman, with her face puffed almost out of recog-
nition, lay upon a slab of marble, with her golden
hair clinging clammily about her. Then there was
a wild, confused picture of a man rushing aim-
lessly, frantically, murderously ; and after that a
blank.
Twenty years,” thought Mounted Constable
Donnon, “why didn’t I make an end of it at
once ? What chance is there ever of meeting him
again. Heaven knows I sought for him hard
enough.”
He caught sight of a thin thread of smoke rising
from a hollow to the left. He welcomed it as
giving him something to think about, and urged
his horse towards the fire. A man dressed in a
shooting costume was seated beside it with his
horse tethered to a bough.
‘ Ah, constable,” announced the stranger, “I’m
glad you’ve come. I’ve lost my way, and I just
lighted the fire as a signal of distress.”
It s a wonder you were found,” laughed Don-
non, 1 ‘ nobody comes within five miles of here once
m six months. How did you get here ? ”
“I left Mr. Marshall’s station to ride into a
place called Ballaba,” explained the stranger.
“ They told me I couldn’t go wrong; I had only
to follow the road.”
“ So you have,” retorted Donnon.
“But,” argued the stranger,” “you call any-
thing a road in this infernal country. I thought
I was on the right track until I found myself in
this wood here.’’
Donnon’s patience gave out very suddenly.
‘ They shouldn’t have let you out by yourself,” he
said shortly ; “some of you people would lose
your way in George-street, Sydney, if there
weren’t walls on each side.”
“Come now, sir, no insolence,” cried the
stranger. There was something in his tone that
made Donnon look more closely at him. He was
English. That was manifest even before he
spoke. He was about 50 years of age, well-pre-
served, well groomed, and apparently accustomed
all his life to the best things the world could
provide. He was still good-looking, but his
moustache and curly hair were tinged with grey.
“ Well, what are you staring at ? ” he demanded
of Donnon. The constable’s eyes were fixed upon
him. The biograph was recommencing at a
tremendous rate.
“ Are you mad, man?” asked the stranger;
“Why don’t you speak ? Damn you, sir, are you
a specimen of the police in this place ? The
sooner you take a lesson from the London police
the better.”
Donnon was thinking— not of the past this time
—but the immediate future. At last he spoke,
slowly and deliberately, with his hand behind him
on his hip.
“So this is your latest game, Black Darby
Fagafa, posing as an English visitor, eh ? ”
“What the devil do you mean?” cried the
other, indignantly.
“ Mean,” replied Donnon, swinging a revolver
into line with the head of the stranger; “I mean
that I know you, Black Darby. I saw you in
Darlinghurst when you went up for three years in
’95, and I never forget a face, although I didn’t
expect you about here.”
The man in the shooting-suit laughed. “By
Jove ! this is too good. You mistake me for a
criminal! When I get back to London, I’ll have
one good story to tell, at any rate.”
Donnon kept his revolver levelled at the man’s
head. “You’ll never get back to London,” he
said. “ I knew that horse the moment I saw it.
Stolen from old Minton, of South Bandong, last
Wednesday night. With your record, that’ll get
you seven years at least, Darby Fagan. Besides,
you’re wanted for the affair at Black Creek, and
you’ll be mighty clever to save your neck if they
fit you for it.”
The stranger seemed more and more composed.
“So I have a capital charge over my head as
well? This is getting more and more interest-
ing. Black Darby, I think you said. A most
picturesque name, suggestive of the bushranger of
fiction.”
Donnon took a step forward. With his left
hand he disengaged the handcuffs from his braces.
“ Walk back to that tree behind you, ” he ordered.
The stranger obeyed, and, snapping one of the
manacles over the man’s right wrist, Donnon
suddenly pulled his left hand around the sapling
and shut the second handcuff over it.
“Well, Black Darby, I think you are harmless
enough now,” he said, facing the prisoner.
“ Would you like to know some of your aliases?”
“By heavens! you won’t remain in the force
long, I tell you. I have some influence though I
am a stranger in the country, and I’ll have
your uniform stripped from you, you ignorant
scoundrel. ”
“It comes with bad grace for you to speak of
scoundrels, Harold. As for ignorance, we both
gained what little knowledge we possess at Oriel
College, Oxford.”
The prisoner jumped as far as his manacles
would permit. “Good God!” he cried, “you
know me. Then what is the meaning of this?
What in heaven’s name do you mean by asserting
that I am identical with this notorious criminal?”
“Merely humor, Harold ; merely humor. It
has been so scarce with me during the past 20
years that I could not resist it. For all 1 know
there isn’t such a person as Black Darby in the
world.”
“ You call me Harold ; who the devil are you,
then ? I don’t know you.”
“Mounted Constable Donnon, at present sta-
tioned at Ballaba, N.S.W.”
“But what do I know about you; and how
dare you take such a liberty with me ? You will
pay dearly for your practical joke, I tell you.”
“ I don t think so,” replied Donnon. “If you
don’t possess an alias, I do. At one time I hap-
pened to reside at Twickenham, on the Thames,
and at that time I was known as Devine—Jim
Devine— ‘ dear old Jim, ’to use the term I have
often heard you use in those good old days.”
“Great God,” whispered the prisoner. Mounted
Constable Donnon seated himself on his heels and
teased a string of ants with a twig.
Mortyn spoke at last. “Jim,” he said softly—
“ Jim ! ”
“Go ahead,” said Donnon, diverting a bull-ant
two inches out of his course.
“Jim,” continued the prisoner, “it’s 20 years
ago since we—we saw one another. I did you an
injury, but, so help me God ! I’ve spent my life
repenting it. We are old men now, both of us.
Let bygones be bygones and shake hands. Re-
member what friends we were before—before ”
“ Before you showed yourself to be the most
treacherous hound I had ever heard of, let alone
ever met. You, my friend ! You didn’t remem-
ber our old friendship 20 years ago. If you expect
me to do so now, you are a fool as well as a black-
guard.”
Five minutes passed. “ What do you intend to
do?” asked Mortyn.
“Nothing,” replied Donnon. “ Absolutely no-
thing.” The prisoner looked at him inquiringly.
“I have done all I intend to do to you. In five
more minutes I will take the saddle from your
horse and drive him out towards the ranges where
the brumbies will pick him up. For myself, I
will ride to a homestead 25 miles away, serve a
summons and return to my duty at Ballaba. That
is all.”
“And me?”
“ You will remain, unless your ingenuity can
devise some means of getting out of those brace-
lets. You will not be alone. There are crows in
large numbers. See”—he waved his hand
towards the sky—“they are appropriately attired
in black. It is said by naturalists that they regard
human eyes as great delicacies.”
Mounted-constable Donnon swung himself into
the saddle, and with the bridle of the other horse
in his handi cantered towards the horizon.
• * • *
One morning, a week later, he rode out of
Ballaba and returned to spend the afternoon clean-
ing the rust from a pair of handcuffs.
Six months later, a stray swagman found a
mummified body far off the track on Marshall’s
run. Mounted-constable Donnon reported that
there were no marks of violence on the body and
no suspicious circumstances.

Imperial Bagmen.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
11 Worse employment might be found for Secretaries of
State than tramping round the glohe preaching the
Empire’s Imperial mission.— Chamberlain, per Cable.
Worse Employment ? That may be—
They might take to poetry,
And, with fiercer, frantic fire,
Strike the strings of Kipling’s lyre ;
And might leave that Bard of Blood
Stuck in Seven Seas of Mud.
Worse Employment ? Little fear.
Could the first of Cecils hear
Words like these —so cheaply brave—
He would shudder in his grave.
Were his ashes in an urn.
They from white to red would turn.
England of the centuries,
England, Mistress of the Seas,
Wilt thou change thy diadem
For the paste of Brummagem ?
Wilt thou pawn thy crest of pride
To these Bagmen Glorified ?
Wilt thou, Shakespeare’s England, keep
Ships for this upon the deep ?
Wilt thou, Milton’s England, hold
Glory cheaper far than gold ?
Cromwell’s England, wilt thou see
Chamberlains make trade of Thee ?
I can see the Secretaries
Tramp about like Missionaries;
I can see them call around
Stating that the Empire’s sound,
Saying that all Men should turn
To a Going Good Concern —
Sidney’s England can it be ?
—Thou art grey with leprosy 1
0 thou British Babylon,
Throned beside the ship-choked Thames,
Decked with plundered gold and gems,
Haggard, full-fed, famine-wan,
Take thy harp whose strings should be
Rigging of thy thousand ships—
Sing the song of Infamy-
Sing the song of thine Eclipse,
All thy glory now is gone
Babylon ! O Babylon !
Sing it: thou shalt sing in vain :
Pardon shall wait long for Thee —
But the Mistress of the Sea
Shall in charitable way
On some not-too-distant day
Mingle in the prayers for rain
Names of Judas and of Cain
And of Joseph Chamberlain.
N.s.w.
V.J.D.
Our New Selection.
(A LIVELY MORNING WITH DAD.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
After breakfast.
We were getting ground ready for wheat, and
all of us were busy—especially Dad. He was
bobbing up everywhere, trying to be in half-a-
dozen places all at once.
Dave went off to the ploughs, leading three
horses; Joe followed with three more.
Barney, the new man, a few minutes late, was
throwing the harness on his set.
Dad happened along,
“Y’ going t’ be here all day ?” he roared. The
man nearly fell down. He fumbled with the
coupling-strap, but failed to fasten it, and hurried
the horses away without giving any answer.
Dad shuffled to the rear of the stable, where
Lion, a draught horse, was invalided. Dad tried
him with a fistful of new hay. Lion refused.
Sneezer Wingfield rode into the yard and asked
Dad could he lend him a horse for a few days to
draw his chaff to the railway.
Dad ignored Sneezer’s presence. He poured
the balance of a bottle of saltpetre into Lion’s
nostrils, then began rubbing him hard about the
flanks and whistling encouragingly to him.
Sneezer r pe ted his remark.
Dad got annoyed.
“ Where th’devil d’y’expect I c’n get horses
from t’ lend y’ ? ” he snapped, turning round, and
glaring savagely at Sneezer.
“I thought maybe y’ had one, that’s all,”
Sneezer murmured, and went away.
He returned again, accompanied by Bill. Bill
and Sneezer were close friends. Bill sometimes
took Sneezer’s sister out riding.
“Oh,” said Bill, approaching his parent with
great respect, “Gipsy’s down th’paddick there>
doin’ nothin’; I s’pose Sneezer c’n have th’ lend of
her, Dad, to draw their ” .
“Well, why th’ devil don’t he go an’ get her?”
Dad yelled, interrupting him. “ Does ’e want me
t’ go runnin’ after ’er fer ’im ? ”
Sneezer smiled, and went and got Gipsy.
Dad trotted across to the ploughs, and worried
Dave and Joe for an hour, then returned and
yelled at Sarah to know why th’ deuce she left the
door of the dairy open, and threatened to sell out
and go and live in town by himself if “ th’ damn
place isn’t looked after.”
• * » •
A young man, clean-shaved, and wearing a
straw hat and breeches and boots, rode up to the
gate on a grey horse, shod all round and well-
groomed.
“ Good morning,” he said.
“ The same,” said Dad.
“ I’m a dairy inspector.”
“Ob, you are?” Dad answered, with a surly
stare.
“ Yes-s,” nervously returned the other.
Dad lifted a back-band that was hanging on the
fence, put it in the stable and was walking off.
“ I’m to inspect all the dairies round here,” the
official remarked, by way of reminding Dad of his
presence.
“ "Well ” —Dad grunted—“ I should ’a’ expected
t’ find a man with a stronger growth o’ hair on
his face fer t’ be inspector.”
“ I don’t know,” the other said mildly. “P’raps
it would surprise you to know that I’m also a
veterinary surgeon ? ”
It did surprise Dad, as his face showed. But
he thought of Lion and his expression changed.
“Wull, if y’ are, me boy,” he said kindly,
“ You’re the very man I want. Come round here
and look at Lion.”
But the inspector wished to see the dairy first,
and Dad, after hesitating, conducted him to it.
He complimented Dad on the appearance of the
butter. Dad received his remarks m silence.
“You had better shift these old cases out of
this, though, I think ? ” and the official quietly sug-
gested a pair of good butter-boxes, built in town
specially for Dad.
“Young man,” Dad snorted, showing the white
of his eye—“ do you know a butter-box when you
see one ? ”
The officer scanned them closely again, and
seemed in a quandary. “Anyway,” he stam-
mered, looking at the door, “the dairy is built
too near the pig-stye, ain’t it ? ”
“ Look here, me shaver,” Dad said, “ when you
own this proputty you can put the dairy where
you d well like; but just now, let me tell y’,
it belongs t’ me ! ”
“ It’s my duty though, M ”
Dad interrupted him.
“Do you know” —he shouted —“ do you know,
me boy, that the Gov’nor an’ th’ Premier of th’
country have been in this dairy ? ”
The official wasn’t aware of it.
“O’ course you ain’t,” Dad snarled. Then,
raising his voice to squealing pitch, and poking
his hairy chin into the other’s face : “ Well, then,
they have! ”
The inspector got uneasy.
“ You had better see th’ horse,” Dad growled,
showing the way to the door; and when the
inspector stepped out he pulled it to violently and
turned the key.
They walked across the yard.
“If you’re a doctor, as you say you are,” Dad
grumbled, “ then y’ know all the ins an’ outs as
well as every bone and muscle of a horse’s body.
.... Now, tell me what’s up with Am,” pointing
to the sick horse.
The inspector threw off his coat, walked round
Lion a few times and eyed him closely. Then he
took him by the head, peered into his mouth,
into his eyes, and examined both ears.
“Nothing the matter with him,” he said, turn-
ing to Dad.
“Pshaw!” Dad blurted out and hobbled
away a yard or two. “Y’might just— Pshatv /”
he broke out again, walking off another yard or
two. ... “Y’ might as well ’samine bis tail
now, y’ eejit. . . . Look here” (Dad flung out his
arm furiously) “d’ y’ see that gate —now get
through it as quick as you can, or I’ll make an
inspecter o’ y’!”
The official mounted his horse and went over to
Regan’s.
** * •
Bill was trying to hunt a bullock out of the big
yard into the killing-pen—a rowdy brute it was,
too, just off the range, and Bill wasn’t taking any
risks.
Dad came along.
“Take ’im quietly—take ’im quietly,” he
shouted—“rushin’ ’im about; what’s th’ use o’
that ; wan’t th’ meat ter go bad ? ”
“ You keep back out o’ there,” Bill called out
in warning as Dad crawled through the rails —
“ you ain’t quick enough t’ keep out of his way.”
But Dad entered and leisurely strode across the
yard, the bullock staring fiercely at him, and
walked into the killing-pen and stood there in
full view of the beast,
“ Now give him a start, Bill,” he shouted.
“He’ll give you a start,” Bill shouted back, “if
you don’t have some sense an’ get out o’ there.”
"Y’ whelp! is that all y’ know yet about
pennin’ a bullock ? ” And Dad marched right out
of the pen and courageously shook his hat at the
beast. The bullock instantly charged. Dad turned
and hurriedly led the way back into the pen.
The bullock bounded in at his heels. Dad
dexterously popped behind the large swing-gate,
opening into the pen, and with a bang and a chuck
shoved it shut; then, as the enraged bullock
swung round and located him, dived to escape
under the bottom rail. But Dad didn’t calculate
correctly. Only his head went under the rail—
the rest of him jammed. The beast made a great
dust and knelt on Dad and bellowed in his ear
and knocked splinters off the rails with his horns,
just above his head. Dad yelled to Bill. Bill
rushed up and shoved the gate in, and the bullock
plunged through into the big yard again.
“ Confound it! ” Dad howled when he got up—
‘ ‘ what th’ devil did y’ want t’ open th’ gate fer
an’ let ’im out again ? ”
Dad was an ungrateful parent.
* * *
A man with a pack on his back, a billy-can in
one hand and a wretched skeleton of a horse towing
behind him passed the gate. Three children were
on the back of the horse—stowed away amongst a
pile of bedding and tin dishes and things ; a lame
woman without boots limped beside the horse
poking it at intervals with a stick. Two miser-
able dogs slunk behind the woman. The family
looked up and stared at the house, but didn’t call.
At the hay-shed, further down, they saw Cranky
Jack mowing lucerne. The man asked Jack for a
bit of hay for the horse. Jack was nothing if he
wasn’t generous. He opened the rails and led the
animal into the shed where Dad’s best hay was.
“There isn’t many like you about,” the man
said to Jack, with gratitude in his eye.
“Nice horse that, Jim,” Jack remarked after
staring long and vacantly at the sad object. There
was a lot of unconscious humor in Jack.
The nomads sat on the ground and rested while
the horse took its fill of Dad’s hay.
Jack went on mowing.
Dad rushed on the scene.
“ Who told y’t’ put that d horse in here ?”
he roared at the man.
“ He did,” the man answered quietly, pointing
to Jack.
“ Take it out, ” Dad yelled, slashing the brute
on the head with his old hat and digging the toe
of his boot into its stomach.
The man jumped up in a rage, and swore and
kicked Dad twice. Then there was a fight.
Dad’s own account of battles he had fought had
often impressed us, but now he seemed to
lose all his skill. The man gob all over him, but
Dad caught him round the waist and down they
went in each other’s arms among the hay—Dad
underneath. The enemy mobilised. The lame
woman joined in, and threw hay on Dad’s head
and prevented him seeing, while the man squeezed
his windpipe. Cranky Jack threw down the
scythe, jammed a finger into each of his ears, and
grinned and grimaced. Jack was a useless man
in a row. But Bill appeared—and Dad’s life was
saved.
«* * •
At dinner, Dad said he hadn’t known such a
busy morning for a long while.
